

2006 WL 901435 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HERODE PIERRE
File: AXX XX5 606 - Boston
February 28, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David McHaffey, Esq.
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated September 27, 2005. This case was last before the Board on December 8, 2005, when we denied a motion to reconsider filed by the respondent and his former counsel. On December 27, 2005, the respondent submitted a motion to reopen proceedings, asserting that his former representative afforded him ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Specifically, the respondent contends that his former counsel failed to contact and/or subpoena his ex-wife to testify about the bona fides of their marriage during the proceedings below despite his repeated requests that she do so, and did not have his former wife’s affidavit notarized. In support of this contention the respondent has submitted an affidavit outlining his complaints against his former counsel, sent the same to her, filed a complaint with the appropriate state bar authority, and has demonstrated that he suffered prejudice as a result of her actions. He has also provided a notarized affidavit from his former wife stating that she informed his former counsel she was willing to testify on his behalf, and that she had been willing to sign a notarized statement but was not asked to do so.
 
We note that this Board questioned the authenticity of the non-notarized affidavit from the respondent’s ex-wife in light of other evidence of record, and found denial warranted given the lack of apparently readily available corroborative evidence. That such evidence was provided in conjunction with the respondent’s motion to reconsider, filed by former counsel, further underscores her deficient performance. In sum, we find that as the respondent has met the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), reopening is warranted. On remand the respondent should be permitted to submit any evidence relevant to his request for a waiver of the requirement to file a joint petition, and his application for adjustment of status.
 
Accordingly, the motion is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings in accordance with the foregoing opinion.

FOR THE BOARD

2006 WL 901433 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SOFIAN BEN ABDELHAFIDH KHALSI
File: AXX XX8 470 - Arlington
February 28, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Elizabeth Krukova, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on December 12, 2005, at which time the Board denied the respondent’s appeal and denied his motion to remand proceedings. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
An alien may seek to reopen proceedings based upon new and previously unavailable evidence See generally 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2; Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). A party seeking to reopen proceedings “bears a heavy burden” as the regulations “plainly disfavor” such motions. INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 110 (1988). In his motion to reopen, the respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from his former attorney. The respondent argues that his former attorney failed to file a proper motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge and failed to adequately argue his case on appeal before the Board. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen. The respondent submits a letter to his former attorney and a copy of the complaint he filed against his attorney with the Virginia State Bar. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent argues that his former counsel filed a deficient motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge on December 8, 2004. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 2-3. However, the evidence in the record indicates that the respondent was not represented by counsel at the time he filed this letter with the Immigration Judge seeking to reopen his proceedings. See Respondent’s December 8, 2004, letter (signed by the respondent); Respondent’s Motion to Remand, Exh. E (sworn statement by the respondent indicating that he wrote the letter to the Immigration Judge seeking reopening of his proceedings and that he hired his former attorney after he received the July 11, 2005, briefing schedule by the Board). Furthermore, the respondent’s former attorney filed a detailed brief and motion with the Board. We decline to reopen proceedings based on respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent argues that he misread his hearing notice and erroneously thought his December 1, 2004, hearing was at a later date. This explanation was considered by the Immigration Judge and he concluded that the respondent’s explanation did not amount to “exceptional circumstances.” See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995); Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993). We observe that the term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1). This Board, in our December 12, 2005, decision, also considered the respondent’s explanation and agreed with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s misunderstanding of the hearing date did not amount to exceptional circumstances. We find no reason to disturb that conclusion.
 
*2 In addition, the respondent argues his case should be reopened because he has potential avenues of adjustment through his legal permanent resident fiancé or his employer. First, there is no evidence that the respondent has an approved employment-based visa. In addition, there is no evidence establishing that the respondent is currently married and that an immediate-relative visa petition has been filed on his behalf. See Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002). The respondent has not demonstrated that he is currently eligible for relief from removal. Accordingly, we conclude that reopening proceedings in not warranted.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.

2006 WL 901477 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PATRICIA MONTOYA-AGUILAR
File: A96 197 314 - Omaha
February 27, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sonia H. Parras Konrad, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Arrived at time or place not designated by the Attorney General
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 6, 2005. In his decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s “motion to reopen under ¿the Violence Against Women Actº (VAWA) and ineffective assistance of counsel.” In that regard, he found that he lacked jurisdiction to entertain the respondent’s applications for relief under VAWA, either in the form of a self-petition or a U visa. With regard to the respondent’s motion to reopen for VAWA cancellation of removal, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent failed to comply with any of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel. On appeal, the respondent maintains that she is eligible for protection under VAWA and that she received ineffective assistance of counsel. The appeal will be sustained, in part, and the record will be remanded for the Immigration Judge to adjudicate the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal pursuant to VAWA.
 
The Immigration Judge properly found that he did not have jurisdiction over the respondent’s VAWA self-petition or U visa and that he only had jurisdiction to adjudicate the respondent’s application for VAWA cancellation of removal. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.20. Additionally, he properly looked to Matter of Lozada, supra, to determine if the respondent should be excused from not having previously sought an application for VAWA cancellation of removal. Contrary to the Immigration Judge’s findings, we find that the respondent has minimally complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of Counsel.1 Specifically, we will accept the respondent’s counsel’s letter with the counsel’s signature and the bar complaint form referring to it, reflecting service on prior counsel as well, for purposes of Matter of Lozada, supra. However, the respondent’s compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, should have been better explained by the respondent, and might well have avoided denial of the motion below. For example, the respondent should have filed an independent affidavit in her own words setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with her former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken by her former counsel and what representations her former counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard as required by Matter of Lozada, supra. Additionally, she should have more clearly demonstrated that her former counsel was informed of the allegations against him as required by Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent, however did file significant evidence in the record to prove her prima facie eligibility for protection in the form of cancellation of removal pursuant to the VAWA, thereby demonstrating that she was actually prejudiced by the actions of her previous counsel. See Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105 (BIA 1984). Finally, we will vacate the Immigration Judge’s prior order granting the respondent the privilege of voluntary departure because that order resulted from the ineffective assistance of prior counsel. This extinguishes the concerns arising from the respondent’s prior acceptance of voluntary departure and subsequent failure to depart. Based on the foregoing, the respondent’s appeal will be sustained. The motion to reopen will be granted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for him to adjudicate the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal pursuant to the VAWA.
 
*2 Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is sustained. The motion to reopen is granted. The Immigration Judge’s March 22, 2005, order is vacated, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

We note that it appears that the respondent’s prior counsel would have had an obvious conflict of interest in simultaneously representing the respondent and her spouse, and we assess the respondent’s compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, with that as a backdrop.

2006 WL 901486 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDGAR RINCON
File: AXX XX4 060 - Newark
February 24, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Arthur Linderman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa Golub
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen of Colombia, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his motion to reopen. We have jurisdiction over this matter pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. The record is remanded.
 
The record reflects that on July 20, 2005, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal, ordered his removal from the United States, but allowed him the opportunity to depart the United States voluntarily. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on August 18, 2005. Therein, it was alleged that the respondent had hired an individual to represent him who may not have been an attorney. That individual supposedly prepared the respondent’s cancellation of removal application, but did not appear at the hearing. According to the respondent’s affidavit, he was informed just prior to the hearing that Ana Reyes could not attend the hearing because she was on vacation. The respondent appeared without counsel, his application was denied, and he later found Ana Reyes in her office shortly after the hearing. The respondent argued in his motion to reopen that he was the victim of negligent representation, and wished an opportunity for a new hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion on September 2, 2005, and this appeal followed.
 
In considering this appeal, we note our decision in Matter of M-P-, 20 I&N Dec. 786 (BIA 1994). Therein, we indicated that when a motion to reopen deportation proceedings is denied, the Immigration Judge must identify and fully explain the reasons for such decision; otherwise, the parties are deprived of a fair opportunity to contest the Immigration Judge’s determination on appeal, and the Board is unable to meaningfully exercise its responsibility of reviewing the decision in light of the arguments advanced on appeal. See generally also Matter of A-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 468 (BIA 1999). In the instant case, the Immigration Judge’s Immigration Judge’s two-line decision fails to even mention the issue of ineffective assistance of counsel, and merely states that the respondent failed to present supporting documents in support of his application for cancellation of removal.
 
Accordingly, we find it necessary to remand the record for inclusion of an appropriate decision by the Immigration Judge. On remand, the Immigration Judge shall address whether the respondent satisfied the requirements for proving that he was the victim of attorney negligence as contemplated under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent and the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”), formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service, should be served with a copy of the decision and should thereafter be given an opportunity to submit briefs in accordance with the regulations. The record should then be forwarded to the Board for further consideration.
 
*2 ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.

2006 WL 901310 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DURID BAHJAT HANA
File: AXX XX4 008 - Detroit
February 22, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David K. Wenger, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mark J. Jebson
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s May 18, 2005, decision denying his motion to reopen his proceedings. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
In his May 18, 2005, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen, the Immigration Judge first found the respondent’s motion untimely as it was filed over 4 years after the final administrative decision was issued on June 6, 2001 (I.J. at 1). In addition, citing our decisions in Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Rodriguez-Ruiz, 22 I&N Dec. 1378 (BIA 2000), the Immigration Judge denied the motion based upon his conclusion that the respondent failed to establish that his June 13, 1989, narcotics conviction had been vacated on the basis of a procedural or substantive defect in the underlying proceedings rather than upon a rehabilitative statute or immigration hardship (I.J. at 1). In this regard, we note that the test for deciding whether a conviction has been vacated for immigration purposes as set forth in Pickering is whether the conviction has been vacated on the merits. Id. A conviction vacated due to ineffective assistance of counsel qualifies as a vacation on the merits. Id. Therefore, it appears from the record that the respondent has established that his 1989 conviction has been vacated and is no longer a conviction for immigration purposes. Cf. Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878 (BIA 2006).
 
However, the record before us does not contain a transcript of the Immigration Judge’s prior June 6, 2001, decision nor does it contain a transcript of the underlying proceedings resulting in the order of removal against the respondent. As a result, we cannot determine with certainty whether or not the respondent remains removable from the United States and, if so, whether the respondent is now eligible for relief from removal that was previously unavailable due to his conviction. As a result, we find it necessary to remand the record to the Immigration Judge for review of the complete record and for entry of a new decision.
 
*2 Accordingly, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.

2006 WL 901313 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WALID HASSAN TAMAN A.K.A. WALTER HASSAN TAMAN
File: A78 330 707 - Buffalo
February 22, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marwa Taman, Esquire1
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joy A. Merriman
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent was found subject to removal in absentia by an Immigration Judge, who declined to reopen his case on March 28, 2003. On April 9, 2004, the Board remanded the case to the immigration court for further proceedings, based on the respondent’s assertion that he received ineffective assistance of counsel. The Immigration Judge on April 26, 2004, scheduled a master calendar hearing for May 14, 2004. The respondent failed to appear, and the Immigration Judge issued an order on May 14, 2004, finding the respondent again subject to removal in absentia. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, which was denied on August 17, 2004, and reissued on January 20, 2006. The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) argues that the Immigration Judge’s decision was correct. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5), may be rescinded upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he did not receive notice of the hearing. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act. The Immigration Judge determined that written notice of the May 14, 2004, hearing had been provided to the respondent, and that reopening would therefore be denied. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act.
 
The Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Notice to Appear informed the respondent that he had to notify the immigration court whenever he changed addresses. See section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Act. At the time that the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen concerning the first in absentia order, on March 28, 2003, the respondent’s address was listed with the immigration court as 27 Smith St.; Sidney, NY 13838. The respondent submitted a Notice of Appeal with the Board on April 3, 2003, and provided the Board with this same address. On June 19, 2003, the respondent filed a “Change of Address Form” with the Board, listing his address as 143 Main Street, Apt. 4; Delhi, NY 13753. The Board therefore sent its April 9, 2004, decision to the respondent at 143 Main Street, Apt. 4; Delhi, NY 13753.
 
Two weeks later, on April 26, 2004, the immigration court sent a “Notice of Hearing in Removal Proceedings” to the respondent at 143 Main Street, Apt. 4; Delhi, NY 13753. This was the most recent address in the respondent’s file, as it was the address provided to the Board on June 19, 2003. It was the address to which the Board’s April 9, 2004, decision had been sent 2 weeks earlier. Therefore, the respondent fails to show that the Immigration Judge sent the Notice of Hearing to an incorrect address. Rather, notice was properly sent to the last known address of the respondent. The respondent submitted a “Change of Address” form to the Board on July 8, 2004, which was sent to the immigration court. This form gave the respondent’s address as 48991 State Highway 10, Apt. 4; Delhi, NY 13788. As the Immigration Judge noted in denying reopening, this form was submitted 2 months after the May, 14, 2004, in absentia order.
 
*2 Therefore, the Immigration Judge properly denied the motion to reopen, and this appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent is represented by his spouse.

2006 WL 729793 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALVA MERCEDES DUARTE-GARCIA
File: AXX XX5 042 - Newark
February 14, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Arturo Suarez-Silverio, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on October 17, 2005, when we adopted and affirmed the decision of an Immigration Judge dated April 11, 2005, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen deportation proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of deportation entered in her case on April 17, 1992, for failing to demonstrate reasonable cause for her failure to appear at her scheduled hearing. See Matter of Marallag, 13 I&N Dec. 775 (BIA 1973); Matter of Patel, 19 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA-1985), aff’d, Patel v. INS, 803 F.2d 804 (5th Cir. 1986). The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of her former counsel. The motion will be denied.
 
Even if we were to find the respondent has generally complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 627 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), regarding ineffective assistance of counsel claims, and accepting her allegations as true, it does not alter any aspect of the Board’s decision. The respondent argues that her counsel was obligated to file a timely petition for review before the united States Circuit Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit, and provided ineffective assistance when he failed to do so. We note, however, the petition for review that her attorney failed to timely file was with the Third Circuit and not with the Board. Consequently, this alleged ineffectiveness, if true, occurred after the Board rendered its October 17, 2005, decision. We find no evidence of irregularity or Board error regarding the service of our October 17, 2005, decision. Thus, any error before the Third Circuit by her counsel does not implicate an action by the Board since the Board made no error as a result of her counsel’s alleged ineffectiveness on this ground. Moreover, the respondent can show no prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The Board can provide no remedy for any alleged ineffectiveness that arose after the Board rendered its decision. The proper forum over her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel lies with the Third Circuit and not with the Board. Accordingly, the motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2006 WL 729793 (BIA)

2006 WL 729785 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RODRIGO IVAN GRIJALVA-ROJAS
File: AXX XX6 322 - Honolulu
February 10, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Edgardo Quintanilla, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
June Ito,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
In a decision dated August 23, 2005, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, and this appeal followed. We note that the respondent appeared before an Immigration Judge on November 5, 1990, and was allowed to depart the United States voluntarily by May 5, 1991. On August 15, 2005, he filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge arguing that the former Immigration and Naturalization Service (“Service”), now the Department of Homeland Security, may have arrested him and placed him in deportation proceedings based upon his Hispanic features, and not on account of evidence that he was in the United States illegally. He further argues that his former counsel was negligent because he did not attempt to have the proceedings terminated against him. In denying the motion, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent had not proven ineffective assistance of counsel because none of the procedural requirements were satisfied.
 
Section 1003.23(b)(4) of 8 Code of Federal Regulations provides that “except as provided in paragraph (b)(4)(ii) of this section, a party may file only one motion to reopen proceedings (whether before the Board or the Immigration Judge) and that motion must be filed not later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened, or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later.” We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent failed to prove that he complied with the requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel because none of the procedural requirements were satisfied. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Even if he had, the respondent having waited 15 years to seek reopening is prima facie evidence of his failure to pursue reopening with due diligence. We will therefore not toll the statute of limitations for seeking such. Consequently, we find that the motion was filed untimely before the Immigration Judge.
 
On appeal, the respondent requests that the Board reopen these proceedings sua sponte presumably based upon his contention that he is not deportable because of the illegality of his arrest. Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000). The Board has held that we will not consider on appeal issues which were conceded at the hearing. See Matter of Pichardo, 21 I&N Dec. 330 (BIA 1996). When an alien concedes that he is removable or deportable, he is generally not permitted to later argue that he is not. See Matter of Pichardo, supra. In Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), we emphasized the importance of the time and numerical limits on motions to reopen, noting the need to discourage dilatory motions and Congress’ mandate that we issue regulations to do so. We recognized, however, that we retained “limited discretionary powers” to reopen or reconsider cases on our own motion. Id. at 984. We cautioned though that such powers should be exercised only in “exceptional situations.” Id. We further indicated that it is the respondent’s burden to demonstrate that such a situation exists. Id. at 984-85.
 
*2 In the instant case, the respondent does not allege that he is legally present in this country, or that relief from deportation exists. Instead, he asks the Board to either reopen or terminate these proceedings sua sponte based solely on his allegation that he was arrested without reasonable suspicion for being in the United States illegally. We have reviewed the documents submitted by the respondent to support his argument. Those documents state that the Service received information from two independent sources that the respondent’s former employer was hiring illegal Mexican workers (See respondent’s Exh. E). That information was also corroborated by the Hawaii County Police in Hilo. Id. The document further indicates that with the consent of an undetermined individual, Service officers questioned the workers, and determined that the respondent was a citizen of Ecuador, and illegal in this country. Id. The Record of Deportable Alien further states that the respondent was questioned about his immigration status, admitted that he was not legally in the United States, and he stated that his employer knew of his illegal status in this country.
 
The “exclusionary rule” arose in the context of criminal proceedings and requires a court to suppress evidence that is the fruit of an unlawful arrest or of other official conduct which violates the Fourth Amendment. It is well-established that the Fourth Amendment exclusionary rule is not applicable in deportation proceedings. See Matter of Cervantes, 21 I&N Dec. 351, 353 (BIA 1996). However, the use of the exclusionary rule might apply in deportation proceedings in the presence of egregious circumstances which transgress the notions of fundamental fairness and undermine the probative value of the evidence in question. See INS v. Lopez-Mendoza, 468 U.S. 1032, 1051, n.5. (1984). The use of this narrow exception to the rule that the exclusionary rule does not apply to deportation proceedings has been limited. See, e.g., Orhorhaghe v. INS, 38 F.3d 488 (9th Cir. 1994); Gonzalez-Rivera v. INS, 22 F.3d 144 (9th Cir. 1994) (both cases involved conduct of government officers which was deemed to be racially discriminatory). We note that “¿oºne who raises the claim questioning the legality of the evidence must come forward with proof establishing a prima facie case before the government will be called on to assume the burden of justifying the manner in which it obtained the evidence.” Matter of Burgos, 15 I&N Dec. 278, 279 (BIA 1975); see also Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980); Matter of Wong, 13 I&N Dec. 820, 821-22 (BIA 1971); Matter of Tang, 13 I&N Dec. 691, 692 (BIA 1971).
 
*3 In the instant case, notwithstanding that the respondent has the burden of showing egregious government action, we cannot find any evidence of government wrongdoing. The Service was notified from reliable sources of the respondent’s illegal presence in this country, questioned him, and he admitted to such. We find no error on behalf of the Service, and see no reason to sua sponte exercise our authority to reopen or terminate these proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2006 WL 729785 (BIA)

2006 WL 729768 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TANIA SANTAMARIA-ARGUETA
File: AXX XX3 222 - San Pedro
February 9, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION AND APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Susan S. Han, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Termination, cancellation of removal
 
This case was last before us on June 24, 2005, when we dismissed the respondent’s motion to reconsider our dismissal of her appeal as untimely. The respondent now contends that she is entitled to reinstatement of her appeal because she was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent also asks that her case be remanded so that she may apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). The respondent’s motion will be granted, and the record will be remanded for further proceedings consistent with this order.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of El Salvador. The Immigration Judge found her removable as an aggravated felon based upon a conviction for robbery. The respondent hired an individual whom she thought was an attorney to appeal from the Immigration Judge’s ruling, but the individual failed to file a timely appeal. The individual also proceeded to file a baseless motion to reconsider before the Board and a petition for review before the Ninth Circuit. When all of the filings before the Board were rejected, the respondent became suspicious and hired another representative. At this time, she learned that her “attorney” was actually only a notary and had improperly held herself out as an attorney. The respondent then, with her new counsel, undertook actions to address her criminal and immigration matters. These actions included moving for reinstatement of her appeal due to ineffective assistance of counsel and petitioning the criminal court for modification of her sentence for her crime.
 
According to the law of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the court in which this case arises, an alien who has been the victim of fraud at the hands of a notary is entitled to equitable tolling of appeal or motion deadlines provided that the alien acts diligently in uncovering the fraud and in pursuing a remedy. See Fajardo v. INS, 300 F.3d 1018, 1020 (9th Cir. 2002), and cases cited therein. The respondent in this case, who has been detained during her entire immigration proceeding, has established that she has acted diligently in both respects. Accordingly, we find that she is entitled to equitable tolling of the deadline to file a timely appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision, and we accept her appeal.
 
In addition, we find that the respondent has offered evidence to show that the sentence for her robbery conviction has been modified so that she has been sentenced to no time in prison. This modification means that her crime no longer qualifies as an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G). See Matter of Cota-Vargas, 23 I&N Dec. 849 (BIA 2005). Accordingly, she appears entitled to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act. Given this fact, we remand her case to the Immigration Judge for a full hearing on her application for this form of relief, and for the entry of a new decision.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reinstate the respondent’s appeal is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this order.

2006 WL 729777 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANDRADE, ROBERTO
File: AXX-XX3-106 - Boston
February 8, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lawrence Gatei
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated September 13, 2005. The respondent has filed a motion to reopen based on his prior counsel’s ineffective assistance before the Immigration Judge below. Reopening or remanding a case based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved applicant setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the applicant in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) evidence that a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent has supported his motion with numerous affidavits, exhibits, and has otherwise complied with Board precedent, insofar as he has taken the steps necessary to proffer an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and has also shown sufficient prejudice as a result of the claimed insufficiently competent legal representation previously. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to the respondent’s motion, although we note the points raised by the DHS regarding the defects in the respondent’s first motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge. In sum, based on the supporting documentation presented by the respondent, as well as the compliance with Board precedent, coupled with a showing of prejudice, we will grant the respondent’s motion.
 
Accordingly, the motion is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.

2006 WL 901468 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GINA SOTOMAYOR DE CASTRO
File: A79 706 448 - Newark
February 8, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert Frank, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa M. Golub
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
In a decision dated August 19, 2003, the respondent was ordered removed in absentia for failing to appear for her hearing on that date. She filed a motion to reopen on December 2, 2003, seeking to rescind the in absentia order. In her affidavit, she averred that Edwin Rivera, an individual who portrayed himself as an attorney, advised her that she would not have to appear for her hearing because he had requested a continuance. On December 22, 2003, an Immigration Judge denied the motion. The respondent has appealed. On appeal, she has submitted additional evidence, which we will construe as a motion to remand. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4). The appeal will be sustained and the motion will be granted.
 
In her denial, the Immigration Judge first found “no evidence that Edwin Rivera held himself out to Respondent as an attorney licensed to practice law” (I.J. at 1). However, with her motion, the respondent submitted numerous brochures and business cards in which Mr. Rivera represented himself to be an attorney.1 The Immigration Judge then noted that there was no evidence that Mr. Rivera had agreed to represent the respondent, given that there was no attorney or representative of record at the time of the in absentia hearing. However, the Immigration Judge ignored, the other evidence submitted by the respondent, including the receipts of payment to Mr. Rivera for legal services, and correspondence to the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) from Mr. Rivera regarding the respondent’s case (Motion, tabs A and C).
 
The Immigration Judge also found the respondent’s “delay” in filing her motion to be “unacceptable” (I.J. at 1-2). However, the motion was filed approximately 3 1/2 months after the issuance of the Immigration Judge’s order, which is well within the 180 days allotted by Statute. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C).
 
Finally, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent had not satisfied all of the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. However, on appeal, the respondent has provided evidence that she has informed Mr. Rivera of the allegations and that she has filed a complaint with the Bronx District Attorney (Appeal Br., tabs A and C). Furthermore, the respondent has submitted a response from Mr. Rivera, in which he admits that he advised the respondent not to appear for her hearings (Appeal Br., tab B). She has also included newspaper articles reporting that the Attorney General of New York has filed a lawsuit against Mr. Rivera for defrauding immigrants (Appeal Br., tab D). The DHS has not opposed the submission of any of this evidence. Moreover, the Board recently remanded the records of the respondent’s children (A79 706 449 and A95 823 255), based on nearly identical facts. In light of these circumstances, we find it appropriate to remand the record for consideration of the evidence. On remand, the respondent may present her request for consolidation of the cases to the Immigration Judge.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.

2006 WL 557821 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARL ANTHONY RICARDO MCGREGOR A.K.A. ANTHONY MCGREGOR
File: AXX XX1 449 - York
February 6, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marcia Binder Ibrahim, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kent J. Frederick
Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal will be dismissed. The transcript of proceedings shows that the respondent, by counsel, waived appeal (Tr. at 6). The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent had waived appeal. On October 1, 2004, the respondent nevertheless filed a Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) argues that the appeal should be dismissed for lack of jurisdiction, due to the fact that the respondent waived appeal.
 
The Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320 (BIA 2000); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(G) (summary dismissal of appeal appropriate where appeal is barred based on waiver of right to appeal). The respondent argues that his attorney provided bad advice, and should have not conceded that the respondent was subject to removal as charged, and this led to his accepting the waiver. See Matter of Patino, 23 I&N Dec. 74 (BIA 2001) (a party wishing to challenge the validity of an appeal waiver may file either a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Judge or an appeal directly with the Board). However, despite making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has complied with none of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making such a claim. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Zheng v. Gonzales, 422 F.3d 98, 106 (3d Cir. 2005) (discussing procedural requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel to the Board). Among other things, there is no affidavit from the respondent setting out his claims. The assertion in the motion that the respondent intends to comply with the procedural requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel only if the case is first reopened, see Respondent’s Mot., at p. 7, is insufficient as a reason to excuse the failure to comply with the Lozada requirements. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARTINEZ-SANCHEZ, OSCAR ALEXIS
File: AXX-XX0-121 - Newark
February 1, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Perez, John D., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa M. Golub,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURLAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 10, 2005. The record reflects that respondent is a minor. He was ordered to be removed in proceedings conducted in absentia. The respondent filed a timely motion to reopen. He stated that miscommunication between his mother and his counsel caused his absence. He claimed that exceptional circumstances warrant reopening. In denying the respondent’s motion, the Immigration Judge stated that the respondent’s mother was instructed to appear on the respondent’s behalf, and that his mother’s failure to appear waived the respondent’s right to contest his removal. We find that the Immigration Judge failed to address the reasons the respondent provided for his failure to appear at the hearing. See Matter of M-P-, 20 I&N Dec. 786 (BIA 1994) (the Immigration Judge’s decision must identify and fully explain the reasons for denial of a motion to reopen immigration proceedings). Accordingly, the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge to further consider the motion and to enter a new decision which explains the reason for such decision. On remand, the respondent’s counsel shall supplement the record with his affidavit and an affidavit from the respondent’s mother. If the respondent is asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, he must provide a complaint and other evidence to meet the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Accordingly, the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MIGUEL NAVARRO-OROZCO
File: AXX XX0 379 - Phoenix
January 31, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John Ayala, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sandra B. Myles
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was previously before us on January 8, 2004, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. We found that the respondent had not shown prejudice due to the actions of his prior counsel. On September 2, 2004, we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The matter is now before us pursuant to the November 14, 2005, decision of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. The court found that the respondent did establish ineffective assistance of counsel, and showed that he was prejudiced by such ineffective assistance. The court remanded the case for the Board to reissue its decision granting the respondent a period of voluntary departure.
 
Following the court’s remand, the respondent filed a motion to remand. The motion states that the respondent is married to a woman who is now a United States citizen and the couple has two citizen children. The respondent seeks to apply for adjustment of status and also to have his application for suspension of deportation further considered in light of the family members that he did not have at the time of his 1997 hearing. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition to the respondent’s motion, asserting that the Board’s decision of January 8, 2004, and September 2, 2004, properly disposed of the case, and stating that the motion to remand is number-barred.
 
Our January 8, 2004, and September 2, 2004, decisions were based on our finding that the respondent had not shown prejudice from any claimed ineffective assistance of counsel. The Ninth Circuit has rejected those findings, concluding both that there was ineffective assistance, and prejudice as a result of it. In view of those findings, as well as upon further review of the record, including the evidence regarding the respondent’s marriage and citizen children, we conclude that a remand is appropriate. Accordingly, the decisions of the Board dated January 8, 2004, September 2, 2004, and September 24, 2003, are vacated, the deportation proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.

2006 WL 448192 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUANA SANTOS-MARTINEZ A.K.A. JUANA ANGELES SALAZAR-JUAREZ
File: AXX XX9 514 - San Diego
January 31, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Thomas A. Mix, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Marc H. Pachon
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). We find no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s factual findings and agree with the Immigration Judge that by failing to timely file a waiver of inadmissibility application or timely request an extension for filing, the respondent waived her opportunity to seek such relief. 8 C.F.R. § 1003-31(c). On appeal, the respondent contends that she was denied a fair hearing when the Immigration Judge refused to continue proceedings to allow her to submit an application for a wavier of inadmissibility and her witnesses in support of such waivers at a later date. An Immigration Judge may grant a motion for continuance for good cause shown. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. This Board has held thai the decision whether to grant a continuance is within the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge, and that decision will not be reversed on appeal unless the denial caused actual prejudice or materially affected the outcome of the case. See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). In this case, the Immigration Judge did not err in denying a continuance where proceedings had been on-going for over 1 year. Moreover, the record reflects that the Immigration Judge twice reminded the respondent and her counsel of the respondent’s possible eligibility for section 237 or 212(i) relief in two separate bearings (Tr. at 18, 39). Moreover, the respondent failed to establish that she was prejudiced as the respondent did not submit on appeal a waiver application, supporting evidence, or an affidavit of what testimony she and her witnesses would have presented which would have demonstrated the respondent’s eligibility for a section 237(a)(1)(H) waiver of inadmissibility. Id.; Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105 (BIA 1984) (an alien in deportation proceedings has been denied a fair hearing only if prejudiced by some deficiency so as to deprive him of due process). The respondent does not allege ineffective assistance of counsel. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2006 WL 448191 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALFONSO RIGOBERTO ORELLANA-AGUILAR
File: AXX XX4 980 - San Antonio
January 31, 2006
 
IN DEPOTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Roberto Hernandez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anibal Martinez,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s June 27, 2005, decision to deny his motion to reopen an order of deportation that was entered in absentia on January 19, 1988. The respondent argues in his January 26, 2005, motion to reopen filed before the Immigration Judge that the Notice of Hearing was incorrectly sent to Jose Salvador Tellez, Esquire, because Mr. Tellez was never legally authorized to represent him in these deportation proceedings. The Immigration Judge found, however that counsel had filed a Notice of Appearance on behalf of the respondent, had acted as his representative before the Immigration Court, and even filed a motion to withdraw as the respondent’s counsel. The Immigration Judge further found that the respondent had received adequate notice of his hearing through counsel, denied the motion, and this appeal followed.
 
On appeal, the respondent no longer argues that Mr. Tellez was not authorized to represent him. Instead, he argues that Mr.Tellez sent nonce of the hearing to an incorrect address, 116th Bushwick, Apt. #2, Brooklyn, New York 11206. He indicates that he notified counsel that he had moved to 31 Montrose Avenue, Brooklyn, New York 11206, and Mr. Tellez was apparently negligent for sending him notice at an address that he should have known was outdated. The respondent does not explain why he waited so many years to check his immigration status.
 
We note that in Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993), this Board held that in all deportation cases where the Notice of Hearing for which the alien failed to appear was served after June 13, 1992, an alien must satisfy the standard for reopening cited in sections 242B(c)(3)(A) and 242B(f)(2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(c)(3)(A) and (f)(2). If an alien was served a Notice of Hearing prior to June 13, 1992, he must satisfy the standards for reopening set forth in section 242(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252(b). Under section 242(b) of the Act, the alien must establish that he had reasonable cause for his absence from the proceedings. Matter of Ruiz, 20 I&N Dec. 91 (BIA 1989); Matter of Haim, 19 I&N Dec. 641 (BIA 1988). On the issue of timeliness of the motion, we note that in Matter of Cruz-Garcia, 22 I&N Dec. 1155 (BIA 1999), the Board held that the regulation at 8 C.F.R.§ 3.2(b)(4)(iii) (1998) imposes no time or numerical limitation on aliens seeking to reopen deportation proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 242(b) of the Act.
 
*2 We initially find that the Immigration Court did not err by sending the Notice of Hearing to prior Counsel. A Notice of Hearing sent to an alien’s attorney of record is proper notice. See 8 C.F.R. § 292.5. The question remaining is whether the respondent’s failure to receive notice of his hearing was caused by the negligence of Mr. Tellez.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639.
 
It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
In the instant case, the respondent has not complied with any of the procedural requirements for proving that Mr. Tellez was negligent by possibly sending the respondent notice of the hearing to an incorrect address. Having failed to do so, we cannot find that he had reasonable cause for missing his hearing by virtue of attorney negligence.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

2006 WL 729641 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WENDDY SITTYC-BECERRA
File: AXX XX3 599 - Dallas
January 31, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nicolas Olano, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
In a decision dated September 2, 2005, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen an order of removal entered in absentia on May 9, 2005. The record reflects that on May 6, 2005, the respondent filed a motion for a change of venue with the Immigration Judge. Therein, the respondent indicated through former counsel, Alfonso Oviedo-Reyes, that she conceded removability, and requested that venue be changed to Miami, Florida. She indicated that she wished to be considered for adjustment of status in Miami. According to the respondent, it was financially difficult for her and her witnesses to appear in Dallas, and her counsel had prior engagements making it impossible for him to travel there. The Immigration Judge did not rule on the motion prior to the hearing, but ordered the respondent removed for failing to appear for her May 9, 2005, hearing. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, but it was denied. This appeal followed.
 
On appeal, the respondent now insinuates through her above captioned counsel that proceedings should be reopened because she was the victim of ineffective representation by her prior counsel. The respondent repeatedly cites the Board’s decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). We therefore presume that she believes that her former counsel was negligent in these proceedings. We shall address the merits of her contention.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639.
 
*2 It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553. (BIA 2003). The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
In the instant case, counsel for the respondent has informed prior counsel of the allegations of negligence, and prior counsel has responded. However, the respondent has failed to submit a complaint against former counsel with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Even if the Board overlooked that flaw, the respondent has failed to explain how former counsel was negligent in his representation of the respondent. In fact, former counsel indicates in his reply to the negligence allegation that he represented the respondent competently. Upon review, we cannot identify any example of attorney negligence, or determine that the actions of the respondent’s former counsel caused her failure to appear for the hearing. We therefore dismiss this aspect of the appeal.
 
The respondent further vaguely argues that “exceptional circumstances” existed to excuse her failure to appear for the hearing. We note that the motion to change venue was filed only three days prior to the hearing. The respondent should therefore have presumed that the request may not have been adjudicated before the respondent’s hearing, and made arrangements to be present. The Immigration Judge’s decision carefully analyzes this aspect of the reopening claim, and we affirm the Immigraiion Judge’s determination on this issue for the reasons provided therein.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

2006 WL 448188 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YU-LIAN, HAN
File: AXX-XX2-344 - Harlingen
January 27, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gonzalez, Ricardo
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Dyann Bernstein,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The respondent failed to appear for her removal hearing on July 13, 2005. She was ordered be removed in proceedings conducted in absentia. The respondent submitted a timely motion to reopen, claiming that her counsel failed to inform her of the date of the hearing. The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the notice of hearing was properly served on the respondent’s counsel of record, and that the respondent has not presented a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel that is compliant with our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). He appropriately denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. We are not persuaded by the respondent’s assertion on appeal she did not receive proper notice because neither her counsel or the Immigration Court informed her of the date of her hearing. Notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. § 3.26(C)(2); Matter of Barocio; 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985). We note that, on appeal, the respondent still has not met the requirements to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, supra. We find no merit in her contention that she was denied an opportunity to present evidence, or that the in absentia order of removal was not properly served. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2006 WL 448299 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANABELE CHAVEZ
File: AXX XX8 207 - Los Angeles
January 27, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gabriella Navarro Busch, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
The record reflects that the respondent was ordered deported in absentia on December 22, 1999. She filed a motion to reopen with an Immigration Judge on January 20, 2000. Therein, the respondent’s former counsel indicated that he was at fault for the respondent missing her hearing. He indicated that he recorded in his schedule book that the hearing would be held on December 27, 2000, and instructed the respondent to attend that day. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on April 27, 2000, finding that the circumstances described did not constitute an “exceptional circumstance” as contemplated under section 240(e)(1) of the Act. No appeal of that decision was submitted to the Board.
 
On August 19, 2005, the respondent, through new counsel, filed with the Immigration Judge a second motion to reopen arguing that the respondent’s failure to appear for her hearing was caused by ineffective assistance of prior counsel. The motion was denied on September 2, 2005, and this appeal followed.
 
We note initially that the Immigration Judge’s decision on the second motion to reopen is cursory, and does not meaningfully address the issues raised by the respondent. Normally, the Board would remand the record to require the Immigration Judge to fully explain the reasons for a decision. See Matter of M-P-, 20 I&N Dec. 786 (BIA 1994) (holding that when a motion to reopen deportation proceedings is denied, the Immigration Judge must identify and fully explain the reasons for such decision; otherwise, the parties are deprived of a fair opportunity to contest the Immigration Judge’s determination on appeal, and the Board is unable to meaningfully exercise its responsibility of reviewing the decision in light of the arguments advanced on appeal). However, inasmuch as the Immigration Judge carefully detailed his reasons for denying reopening in his April 27, 2000, decision, we will refrain from remanding the record based upon an insufficient Immigration Judge decision.
 
The issue for the Board to resolve is whether the respondent established that reopening of these proceedings is appropriate based upon attorney negligence. We initially note that a request for rescission of an order of removal based on “exceptional circumstances” must be filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). The respondent’s second motion was clearly filed more than 180 days after the order of deportation was entered, and is untimely.
 
*2 We further note that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639.
 
It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855 (9th Cir. 2004) (alien must show outcome might have been affected). The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998):
 
In addition, we have considered that the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals has held that an alien need not fulfill all the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, to assert claim of ineffective assistance of counsel when the record reflects a clear and obvious case such. See Castillo-Peres v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that attorney’s failure to timely file a suspension of deportation application was negligence). See also Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331 (9th Cir. 2000), Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
In the instant case, the respondent appears to have established compliance with the above procedural requirements, and has shown a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel. On appeal, the respondent acknowledges that the second motion to reopen was untimely filed, but requests that the Board equitably toll the 180-day period for seeking reopening.
 
In denying the second motion to reopen, the Immigration Judge indicated that the respondent’s motion was number-barred from consideration. We note that the time and number restrictions set forth in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b) do not apply to motions to reopen in absentia orders of deportation. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(iii)(D). Consequently, the respondent’s motion is not numerically-barred, but may still be time-barred.
 
*3 Turning to the equitable tolling issue, in Fajardo v. INS, 300 F.3d 1018 (9th Cir. 2002), the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals held that the limitation period on motions to reopen based on exceptional circumstances must be tolled until a petitioner was aware of the harm resulting from an attorney’s misconduct. In the instant case, the respondent was aware on December 27, 2000, that she may have been the victim of attorney negligence when she learned that she was ordered deported in absentia on December 22, 2000. While we recognize that the respondent may have trusted former counsel to rectify her deportation order, and thus allow her an opportunity to do so, we cannot ignore that the second motion to reopen was not filed until August 19, 2005. Having waited for such an extensive period to seek reopening based upon attorney negligence is indicative of the respondent’s failure to pursue her case with due diligence. We therefore cannot equitably toll the statute of limitations for reopening, and find that her second motion to reopen was untimely.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2006 WL 448306 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: THE OAI HA
File: AXX XX8 200 - Los Angeles
January 26, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John R. Alcorn, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
In a decision dated July 22, 2005, the Board summarily affirmed without opinion the June 4, 2004, decision of the Immigration Judge which found the respondent subject to removal as charged and granted him voluntary departure.¿FN1º On October 22, 2005, the respondent submitted a motion to reopen removal claiming ineffective assistance by his prior counsel and requesting reopening based on his marriage to a United States citizen. The motion will be denied.
 
In his motion, the respondent claims ineffective assistance of counsel by his attorney who represented him at his hearing. He claims that such attorney failed to submit an appellate brief or pursue relief on his behalf based on marriage. The record in the respondent’s case reflects, however, that the respondent represented himself on appeal. There was no Notice of Appearance submitted on the respondent’s behalf in connection with the appeal, which the respondent himself signed on his own behalf. The record further reflects that all briefing notices were sent to the respondent, not to an attorney. The respondent therefore was clearly on notice that he was unrepresented before the Board. On this record we therefore find that the respondent has not set forth a meritorious ineffective assistance of counsel claim.¿FN2º
 
In his motion the respondent also asserts that his case should be reopened and remanded so that he may apply for adjustment of status based on his September 27, 2004, marriage to a United States citizen. The record reflects however that the respondent’s voluntary departure period ended September 22, 2005, but the motion was not filed until October 20, 2005. The respondent is therefore ineligible for reopening. Section 242B(e)(2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(e)(2); see also Matter of Shaar, 21 I&N Dec. 541 (BIA 1996).
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.

2006 WL 729731 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ABD EL AZIZ FAHMY MINSSY
File: AXX XX5 473 - Los Angeles
January 25, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gary Silbiger, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reconsider
 
*1 In an order entered on October 4, 2005, we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent has filed a motion to reconsider our order. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider shall state the reasons for the motion by specifying the errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). A motion to reconsider must assert that, at the time of our previous decision, an error was made. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). The very nature of a motion to reconsider is that the original decision was defective in some regard. Id.
 
The respondent has presented no persuasive evidence or argument that we committed error of law or fact in denying the motion to reopen. Contrary to the contentions in the motion to reconsider, the motion to reopen was untimely. The adoption of December 31, 2004, as the date that “fraud” was discovered, so as to invoke “equitable tolling” for a motion filed on March 31, 2005, is not persuasive when it is clear that the respondent had concluded that his first counsel committed ineffective assistance of counsel by mid-2003, and when current counsel was already exploring the issues as early as October 22, 2004. We also note that documents complying with this Board’s Lozada requirements were not filed until April 2005 and were clearly untimely, even though they were headed as part of a “supplementary motion.”
 
Further, as the Immigration Judge correctly noted in an addendum to his July 22, 2003, decision, the respondent has failed to prove that former counsel committed prejudicial error. The motion does not persuade us that our decision to deny reopening was based on error, and there is, consequently, no basis for reconsideration of our order, in the absence of persuasive evidence of error.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.

2006 WL 448271 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ONOHARIGHO MAREYO
File: AXX XX8 832 - Houston
January 24, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John O. Mukoro, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Angela A. Crider
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s motion to reopen is untimely and will be dismissed. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) argues that the appeal should be dismissed.
 
On October 13, 2004, an Immigration Judge denied a motion to reopen filed by the respondent. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had failed to show exceptional circumstances for missing his removal hearing, which resulted in a March 11, 2004, in absentia order. The Immigration Judge rejected the respondent’s contention that he had received ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent filed an untimely appeal on February 7, 2005. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(b) (appeal of Immigration Judge’s decision must be filed within 30 days). There is no excuse provided for the late appeal. The respondent was represented by current counsel at the time of the Immigration Judge’s October 13, 2004, decision, and there is therefore no basis for arguing that the respondent was unable to file a timely appeal.
 
We note that, with the untimely appeal, the respondent submitted a document entitled “Motion to Reopen.” The respondent states that the “Motion” is filed for the purpose of “appealing the decision of the immigration court respecting the in absentia removal proceeding hearing on March 11, 2004, and the subsequent appeal signed on October 13, 2004.” As such, the “Motion” is actually part of the respondent’s attempt to appeal the Immigration Judge’s October 13, 2004, decision, which, as discussed above, is untimely. Moreover, the Board does not normally have jurisdiction over motions filed in cases where the Board never assumed initial jurisdiction. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974). The Board lacks jurisdiction over the untimely appeal. Therefore, to the extent that the filing is considered to be a motion, the Board lacks jurisdiction to consider it.
 
Accordingly, we find that the appeal is untimely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
