2006 WL 448263 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMED BENMALEK
File: AXX XX9 698 - Boston
January 23, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mario Bozza, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The motion is denied. This case was last before us on August 2, 2005, when we upheld an Immigration Judge’s denial of adjustment of status and denied the respondent’s appellate claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.¿FN1º The respondent’s motion to reopen, filed with a new Petition for Alien Relative (Form I-130) from the same purported spouse, largely puts forth the same arguments an evidence that were part of his previously denied appeal. The respondent has made no attempt to cure the defects, as previously noted by this Board, in his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The unsupported assertion by the respondent’s current attorney, that the respondent misunderstood some of the questions asked at the merits hearing, is not evidence. Matter of S-M-, 22 I&N Dec. 49 (BIA 1998). As noted in our prior decision, a significant credibility problem arose with respect discrepant witness statements as to who was the best man at the respondent’s wedding. The current attorney’s own assertion that the respondent misunderstood questions about this because Moroccan weddings do not have a best man, is not evidence. Id. Morever, this assertion is contradicted by a new written statement, submitted with the motion, from an individual named Khalid Abboud, who again claims—still in contrast to previous statements by other witnesses—to have been the best man at the wedding. This resolves none of the discrepancies in this case.
 
We previously rejected the generalized explanation that the credibility problems could be explained by the respondent’s nervousness at the hearing. Moreover, this suggestion does not account for the other previously noted discrepancies among the other witnesses. Many of the reasons which the Immigration Judge gave for finding that the respondent and the supposed spouse lacked credibility remain unchallenged. The new evidence, including photocopies of recent third-party written statement—which are neither sworn nor notarized—does not resolve the conflicting witness accounts previously noted by the Immigration Judge and this Board. The new written statements purport to contain, inter alia, some personal opinions from various individuals about whether the respondent’s marriage might be bona fide, but no explanation has been provided why such evidence could not have been submitted sooner. The written statements and other materials submitted with the motion are essentially cumulative of what was already of record. Thus, the new evidence is unpersuasive. Notwithstanding the respondent’s apparent argument to the contrary, the couple’s conduct, before and after marriage, is relevant to determining their intent at the time of marriage. See Lutwak v. United States, 344 U.S. 604 (1953); Matter of Soriano, 19 I&N Dec. 764, 765-66 (BIA 1988). We note that these considerations, for purposes of this case, relate to whether the respondent put forth credible evidence and whether he therefore would merit relief in the exercise of discretion. In this regard, we further note that a number of the discrepancies involve what supposedly happened on the day of the wedding. As we indicated in our prior decision, for the reasons stated therein, we would deny adjustment of status in the exercise of discretion.

2006 WL 448358 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BENSABEUR BELHADS MOKHTAR
File: A73 075 848 - Philadelphia
January 20, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Bradford Bernstein, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has requested that the Board reconsider its decision dated November 10, 2005, to deny a request to reopen an order of deportation entered in absentia. The motion is denied.
 
A motion to reconsider must state the reasons upon which the motion is based and be supported by such precedent decisions as are pertinent. There is no requirement for allegations of new facts or even new precedent. 8 C.F.R. § 103.5 (2001); Sanchez v. INS, 707 F.2d 1523, 1529 (D.C. Cir. 1983); Chudshevid v. INS, 641 F.2d 780, 784 (9th Cir. 1981); see Artukovic v. INS, 693 F.2d 894, 898 (9th Cir. 1982). The motion to reconsider is generally used to question a decision for alleged errors of fact or law. Sanchez v. INS, supra. It is a request that the decision be reexamined in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case which was overlooked. See Chudshevid v. INS, supra; Hurwitz, Motions Practice Before the Board of Immigration Appeals, 20 San Diego L. Rev. 79 (1982), reprinted in 6 Immigration and Nationality L. Rev. 472 (1984); see also Matter of Cerna, supra.
 
In our prior order, the Board found that the respondent failed to establish that it was appropriate to grant an untimely motion to reopen based upon a showing that the 180-day period for reopening should be equitably tolled based upon ineffective assistance of counsel. We further noted therein that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. See also Ponce-Leiva v. Ashcroft, 331 F.3d 369 (3d Cir. 2003) (concluding that the three-prong test is a reasonable exercise of the Board’s discretion); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). In addition, we noted that the Third Circuit Court of Appeals specifically requires that the party alleging ineffective counsel provide such counsel with an opportunity to respond. See Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127, 132 (3d Cir. 2001).
 
*2 We have again reviewed the record, and still find that the respondent has failed to satisfy the procedural requirements set forth above. The respondent has not submitted an affidavit in which he provides his version of what his former attorney, Laura Weiss, allegedly told him. He also has not provided any proof that he had a business relationship with Laura Weiss, and that she is even an attorney. The respondent indicates through counsel that Laura Weiss practiced in Pennsylvania in 1995, but does not indicate what city, or how he located her. The respondent indicates he tried to find her on the Internet, but could not find an attorney of that name in Pennsylvania. He did not indicate whether he tried to find her under any other states attorney records. We further note that Laura Weiss likely had a phone listing, and the respondent has not indicated whether he tried to locate her through phone company or Post Office records. Moreover, the respondent indicates that Laura Weiss told him in 1995 that he need not appear in court because his deportation case would be terminated. The respondent was therefore under an obligation to verify that such occurred. The respondent’s having waited 7 years to check on his immigration status after having been served with the Order to Show Cause shows his lack of due diligence in protecting his immigration rights. For these reasons, and those expressed in our prior order, we see no error in our prior decision not to reopen these proceedings. It follows that the respondent’s motion to reconsider is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2006 WL 729673 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SALVADOR ORTEGA-SANDOVAL
File: AXX XX2 016 - San Pedro
January 20, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Murray D. Hilts, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has filed a timely motion seeking to have the Board reconsider its October 14, 2005, decision. Our earlier decision denied an untimely motion to reopen which sought to have the Board reconsider its August 29, 2003, decision dismissing the respondent’s appeal as untimely. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
We denied the motion to reopen, as it contained no legal argument, but rather several “Attachments”, including the respondent’s affidavit. The respondent contends that the motion was among his earlier submission; this is not the case. However, we will reconsider the reasons for our earlier denial, based on the respondent’s explanation that his attorney’s office made a clerical error in omitting the actual motion to reopen. Upon reconsideration, however, the motion to reopen will again be denied.
 
The respondent blames his former attorney for filing an untimely appeal. However, the respondent’s affidavit does not show that he made sufficient effort to keep in touch with counsel concerning the case from the time of the Immigration Judge’s January 10, 2002, decision, until the respondent says he learned of the Board’s decision 3 years later, in March, 2005 (Respondent’s Affidavit). An individual seeking to rely upon “equitable tolling” of a filing deadline must show that he has acted with due diligence. See Albillo-De Leon v. Gonzales, 410 F.3d 1090, 1099-1100 (9th Cir. 2005); Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1186 (9th Cir. 2004); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir.2003). The respondent does not make this showing.
 
In any event, a respondent claiming ineffective assistance of counsel must show prejudice resulting from his attorney’s failure. Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1189 (9th Cir. 2004); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556, 561-62; (BIA 2003), Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The Immigration Judge found the respondent ineligible for relief from removal based on his conviction of an aggravated felony. The respondent argues that the respondent was eligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(e). This is incorrect. The United States Supreme Court held that the restrictions on section 212(c) relief do not apply retroactively to aliens who pled guilty to offenses prior to April 24, 1996. See INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001). The respondent was found to have committed an aggravated felony based on his 2001 conviction for possession of heroin, after a previous conviction, and relief under 212(c) of the Act was therefore unavailable even under that court ruling. See I.J. at 3. The respondent does not otherwise suggest that the Immigration Judge erred in denying relief.
 
*2 The pending motion is, therefore, denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2006 WL 448166 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WENSHENG ZHANG
File: XX XX8 193 - Los Angeles
January 18, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Xia Zhao, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reissuance of prior decision; stay of removal
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen alleging that his prior counsel was ineffective. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 f.2d 10 (1st. Cir. 1988). Specifically, the respondent alleges that one of the attorneys who has represented him in these proceedings contracted out certain duties to another attorney, who failed to carry out those duties. The respondent alleges that the attorney failed to properly inform him that his appeal to this Board had been dismissed. The respondent alleges that he has been prejudiced because he was unable to file an alternative application for relief while still in proceedings, and unable to file a timely motion to reconsider and a timely appeal. The respondent’s present motion is untimely, but he asserted that it should be accepted pursuant to the doctrine of equitable tolling. See Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001).
 
The respondent has not established that the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel prevented him from filing a meritorious motion to remand while an appeal was pending wiih the Board, or in the alternative within the time limits for a motion to reopen after the Board’s decision in this case.¿FN1º The Board’s decision was issued in April 2004 and, according to the evidence submitted with the motion to reopen, the respondent’s marriage did not take place until March 2005, approximately 11 months later. Hence, the respondent could not have filed a legitimate motion to remand while the case was on appeal and any motion to reopen filed with the Board on or after the date of his marriage would have been untimely. See generally 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2.
 
In his motion, the respondent incorrectly states that the Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision without opinion. The record reflects that the appeal was dismissed with a short opinion which, inter alia, briefly explained our reasons for finding the Immigration Judge’s adverse credibility determination to not be clearly erroneous. The respondent’s motion to reopen contains no arguments or evidence that would lead us to conclude differently, and we thus conclude that the respondent has not established that he was prejudiced by former counsel’s failure to file a motion to reconsider.
 
Since the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective assistance of prior counsel, he has not met the general standards for reopening applicable in the present context. See, e.g. Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105 (BIA 1984); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640. Since the respondent did not establish prejudice, we need not address the issue of equitable tolling. To the degree that the respondent’s argument is based on counsel’s failure to extend the possible duration of removal proceedings by filing an appeal with a federal circuit court of appeal, we note that we have no jurisdiction to consider issues involving the jurisdiction of federal courts.
 
*2 The respondent moves that the Board reissue its decision to allow him to seek review in the United States Court of Appeals. In the instant case the respondent does not allege any Board error or administrative problems; therefore, we have no procedural or substantive basis on which to reissue our decision. Further, as we stated above, we have no authority to consider issues involving the jurisdiction of federal courts.
 
For the foregoing reasons, the respondent’s motion to reopen is denied. The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is also denied.

We note that the respondent’s motion substantially complied with Matter of Lozada,. See generally Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SHRUN LATA
File: AXX XX7 739 - San Francisco
January 12, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tsz-Hai Huang, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Stephen A. Johnston
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on May 23, 2003. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions not pertinent here), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before August 21, 2003. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until October 27, 2005, more than 2 years later. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time.
 
Current counsel for the respondent alleges that prior counsel, Steven Englander, provided ineffective assistance of counsel and that therefore equitable tolling should be applied to the regular filing deadlines in this case. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has filed an opposition to the motion, noting that it is untimely, asserting that due diligence has not been shown, and arguing that case law in the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit would not lead to a conclusion that the respondent established ineffective assistance of counsel in this case. See, e.g., Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (equitable tolling will be applied where alien prevented from timely filing by deception, fraud, or error, so long as alien acted with due diligence). The respondent must also show prejudice in such cases. See Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1185-86 (9th Cir. 2004).
 
Prior counsel has rebutted the claims made by the respondent, and current counsel has stated that the respondent has not filed a formal bar complaint against Mr. Englander because “his making a bad judgment and legal analysis to waive these relief ¿sicº…did not rise to the level that a formal complaint would need to be filed” (Respondent’s Motion at 13). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (requirements for establishing claim of ineffective assistance of counsel). The respondent has been in the United States for 14 1/2 years and has failed to adjust her status during that period of time. The respondent now wishes to file an asylum application for the first time, without any showing that it should be excused from the 1-year filing deadline, and changed circumstances specific to the respondent’s case have not been shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(ii), 1208.4(a). The record before us also does not establish that her current “marriage” to a United States citizen is bona fide or that a visa petition has been filed on her behalf by her third husband in the United States, and she has not met the requirements of Matter of Velarde-Pacheco, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002), for such a motion on the basis of a pending visa petition. The respondent has failed to establish that equitable tolling would be warranted in this case. We conclude that reopening would not be warranted in this case.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied. The request for a stay is also denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for stay of deportation is denied.

2006 WL 448319 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DEYI DUE CHEN
File: AXX XX1 016 - El Centro
January 12, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gary J. Yerman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeff Lindblad
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On November 8, 2004, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision, in which the judge denied his motion to reopen removal proceedings, which were conducted in absentia. The respondent filed a late motion to reopen on June 13, 2005.¿FN1º See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen, filed with the Immigration Judge. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act; Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999). The Board is precluded from considering such a challenge to an in absentia order in the first instance. See Matter of Guzman, supra. The Board has jurisdiction to consider an appeal, when a motion to reopen an in absentia order is denied by an Immigration Judge. Id. As stated, we considered, and denied, the respondent’s appeal from such a motion in this case. The respondent now seeks to have the Board reconsider its decision, based on a claim that prior counsel was ineffective.
 
An individual seeking to rely upon “equitable tolling” of a filing deadline must show that he has acted with due diligence. See Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1186 (9th Cir. 2004); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003). This showing is not made here. The respondent complains that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from Ren Rong Pan, Esquire, prior to the scheduled removal hearing. We considered these claims in our November 8, 2004, decision, where the respondent was represented by Joan Xie, Esquire. Moreover, the respondent states that he met with current counsel “in early 2005”, yet the pending motion was not submitted until May, 2005, and not filed until June 13, 2005. See Respondent’s Affidavit; Iturribarria v. INS, supra (court found that to the extent that equitable tolling applies, based on actions of prior counsel, limitations period begins when respondent meets with new counsel and learns of fraud).
 
Because the motion is untimely, we could only reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). We take this action only in “exceptional situations.” Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); see also Matter of Jean, 23 I&N Dec. 373 (A.G. 2002); Ekimian v. INS, 303 F.3d 1153 (9th Cir. 2002). We decline to take such action here. The pending motion is, therefore, denied.

2006 WL 211054 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDEN JOHANA CABRERA-ARDON
File: AXX XX4 826 - Los Angeles
January 10, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrew Knapp, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; rescission
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on April 11, 2005, when we remanded the record to the Immigration Judge for the preparation of a full decision. The Immigration Judge, in a decision dated May 23, 2005, detailed the reasoning for denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. We agree with the Immigration Judge’s reasoning as set forth in the decision dated May 23, 2005, and we find no clear error in the factual findings therein. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(iv).
 
On August 20, 2004, the respondent had filed an untimely motion to reopen proceedings. See generally 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (stating that a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the date of the final administrative order or by September 30, 1996, whichever is later); cf. section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C) (setting a statutory limit of 180 days for the rescission of an in absentia order where the failure to appear was due to exceptional circumstances). The central basis on which the Immigration Judge denied reopening was the respondent’s failure to exercise due diligence in pursuing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. The respondent has furnished an affidavit which cites no reason which rises to the level of due diligence for not challenging her deportation order at any time between reaching the age of maturity, in March, 1997, at the earliest, or July 1, 2003, when she concedes learning of the deportation order, at the latest, and August 2004. See Respondent’s Exh. A, paragraphs 14-16.
 
Typically, motions to reopen should be filed within 3 months of the previous order; due diligence in this case can reasonably be construed as requiring a filing by October, 2003, or soon thereafter, i.e., within approximately 3 months of the latest date on which the respondent might have been aware she had been ordered deported. We note in particular that the respondent’s grandfather, with whom she apparently had regular contact, both before and after reaching the age of maturity, was fully aware that her previous attorney had not contacted him since the date of their 1990 hearing, and the respondent has not alleged that she was unaware of this failure in the period between March, 1997 and April, 2004. Further, the affidavits filed by her relatives, while they support her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, are not persuasive evidence with respect to her alleged lack of awareness, when she was an adult, of a deportation order and the lack of effective assistance of counsel. In light of all the factors presented, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not exercise due diligence and that the facts asserted thus do not establish a basis for reopening based on equitable tolling of the applicable time limits.
 
*2 The respondent argues that her request that an in absentia order of deportation should be rescinded on improper notice grounds is without merit because, as the Immigration Judge pointed out, there has never been an in absentia order issued in this case. Thus we need not address the arguments raised by the respondent based specifically on principles underlying the validity of certain in absentia orders of deportation issued with respect to minor respondents.
 
We note that the respondent has presented evidence of positive discretionary equities, and her assertions that her former attorney did not effectively represent her are not contradicted by any evidence in the record. While neither the Board nor the an Immigration Judge have the jurisdiction to reopen proceedings solely for the purpose of countermanding the effect of the time and number limitations on motions, there exist in certain cases exceptional circumstances which may be sufficiently compelling to warrant an intervention of our authority to reopen sua sponte. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000). While the respondent did not exercise due diligence in pursuing her ineffective assistance of counsel claim, it is apparent from the record that she has pursued other legitimate claims for relief in a diligent manner. One or more of those claims may have been granted if her former counsel had rendered even minimally effective assistance of counsel, or if any of her relatives had exercised due diligence on her behalf at any time during the many years between the deportation order and her turning 18. In light of these factors and other factors in the record, we will reopen these proceedings and remand the record to the Immigration Judge.
 
For the foregoing reasons, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed with respect to her motion to rescind her previous deportation order and with respect to her motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Proceedings are reopened pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion.

IN RE: KELECHI GERALDINE UCHE-JENNINGS A.K.A. GERALDINE KELECHI UCHE
File: AXX XX0 407 - Baltimore
January 9, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ofelia L. Calderon, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent’s “Motion to Correct Decision”, which will be construed as a motion to reconsider the Board’s October 18, 2005, decision, will be denied. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion.
 
An Immigration Judge issued an order of removal against the respondent on June 9, 2004. He denied a second continuance sought by the respondent, and found her to be ineligible for relief from removal. The respondent appealed this decision to the Board. In her brief, she argued that the Immigration Judge had erred in denying the continuance. She also argued that a former attorney had provided ineffective assistance of counsel, and sought a remand to the immigration court for further proceedings.
 
On October 18, 2005, we affirmed the decision of the Immigration Judge. We further found that the respondent’s documents and claims asserting ineffective assistance of counsel “constituted a motion to remand” to the immigration court. We declined to remand the case to the immigration court, finding that the respondent had not made out a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against her former attorney. We concluded by stating that “the motion to reopen is denied”.
 
On November 16, 2005, the respondent submitted the “Motion to Correct Decision”, which was rejected for a filing defect, and filed on December 2, 2005. We will consider the motion to reconsider to be timely filed.
 
The respondent states that she “does not contest” the substance of the Board’s October 18, 2005, decision denying her appeal. Rather, she objects to our stating that we were denying a “motion to reopen”. The respondent argues that she has not filed any motion to reopen. She wishes to preserve her opportunity to file a motion to reopen that is timely and not numerically barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
Contrary to the respondent’s arguments, we did not err in characterizing her request that we remand proceedings to the Immigration Judge as a “motion to reopen”. See Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4) (motion to reopen decision rendered by Immigration Judge while an appeal is pending before the Board may be deemed a motion to remand, but in substance remains a motion to reopen); see also Obioha v. Gonzales, __ F.3d __, 2005 WL 3312762 (4th Cir. Dec. 8, 2005)(where respondent filed motion to remand to Immigration Judge based on new theory of relief, motion properly viewed as motion to reopen).
 
*2 However, we note for the respondent’s benefit that the time and numerical limitations for filing a motion to reopen found in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) do not begin to run until the entry of a “final administrative decision.” Akhtar v. Gonzales, 406 F.3d 339, 409 (6th Cir. 2005) (acknowledging that Board has ruled that a motion to remand filed in the absence of a final administrative decision, while in substance a motion to reopen, is not subject to the time and number limitations placed on motions to reopen “because the clock for filing a motion to reopen begins to run only after the entry of a final administrative decision”, citing Matter of Oparah, supra). The final administrative decision was entered in this case on October 18, 2005.
 
The respondent’s motion is, therefore, denied.

IN RE: RAHIM MAKNOJIYA
File: AXX XX7 316 - Houston

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brian K. Bates, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Merilee Fong
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on March 29, 2004, when we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent removed in absentia for his failure to appear on February 13, 2003. The respondent has now filed a motion to reconsider. The motion has been filed out of time and will be denied.
 
The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on March 29, 2004. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 3.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions not pertinent here), a motion to reconsider a decision of the Board must be filed within 30 days after the date of that decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 3.2(b)(2). Furthermore, a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until October 26, 2005. The motion was therefore filed out of time, whether accepted as a motion to reconsider or construed as a motion to reopen.
 
Furthermore, even if equitable tolling is applied due to the respondent’s new claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, we must find that it is untimely. Current counsel began representing the respondent in June 2003, as evidenced by the EOIR-27 filed by the law firm of Quan, Burdette & Perez, P.C. Therefore, the respondent was represented by current counsel when the Board dismissed his appeal on March 29, 2004. Yet the motion to reconsider based on ineffective assistance of counsel was not filed with the Board until almost nineteen months later. Consequently, we find that the respondent failed to exercise due diligence in pursing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and his motion must be denied as untimely. Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2005 WL 3709287 (BIA)

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OTTO FERNANDO RIVERA-BOTTZECK
File: AXX XX6 746 - Canon City
December 28, 2005
 
ORDER:
  
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Bonnie K. Brady
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
  
ORDER:
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.¿FN1º We affirm the Immigration Judge’s August 1, 2005 decision denying the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). Specifically, we find no legal error in the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent’s conviction for securities fraud constitutes an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(M)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(M)(i). Nor do we find clear error in the factual findings leading up to that determination. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (mandating that the Board “will accept the determination of factual issues by an immigration judge … unless the determination is clearly erroneous”).
 
As the Immigration Judge noted, to be considered for such discretionary relief, the respondent must prove that he has not been convicted of an aggravated felony. See section 240(A)(a)(3) of the Act. The respondent does not dispute that he pleaded guilty to one count of securities fraud and was granted a deferred judgment and sentence. The respondent, however, argues that the record does not support the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent was convicted of an offense in which “the loss to the victim or victims exceeds $10,000.” Section 101(a)(43)(M)(i). We disagree.
 
The record contains evidence indicating that the respondent was required, by the conditions of his deferred judgment, to pay $65,000 in restitution to certain named victims (Exhs. 3, 6, 8 at Attachments). Further, as the Immigration Judge noted, section 101(a)(43)(M)(i) does not require that the statute underlying the respondent’s conviction contain as an element loss to a victim in excess of $10,000. Rather, section 101(a)(43)(M)(i) requires only that the underlying offense involve fraud or deceit. Once the requisite fraud or deceit has been established, the conviction qualifies as an aggravated felony if the loss to the victim or victims exceeds $10,000. See section 101(a)(43)(M)(i) of the Act. It is undisputed that the respondent was convicted of an offense involving fraud or deceit. However, on this record, the respondent has not demonstrated either that the victims of his crime suffered no monetary loss or that they suffered a loss less than $10,000. Consequently, the respondent has failed to prove that his securities fraud conviction is not an aggravated felony under the Act.
 
*2 Furthermore, contrary to the respondent’s assertions, the record contains no evidence indicating that the respondent’s conviction is not a final conviction for immigration purposes. Thus, it appears that respondent has a final aggravated felony conviction, which renders him ineligible for cancellation of removal. See section 240A(a)(3) of the Act. In addition, any alien who has been convicted of an aggravated felony is ineligible for cancellation of removal regardless of whether the alien is actually charged with removability based on that conviction. Thus, as the Immigration Judge noted, it was not necessary to include such information in the Notice to Appear.
 
Finally, to the extent the respondent claims that he was denied due process during his hearing before the Immigration Judge, the respondent’s arguments are without merit. The record reveals that the respondent was represented by counsel and received a full and fair hearing on the merits of his claim.¿FN2º Although the respondent alleges error, he has not articulated or identified prejudice stemming from such errors. More importantly, the respondent has failed to demonstrate that the outcome of his case would have been different had the hearing been conducted in any other manner. See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356-57 (BIA 1983).
 
On the whole, the record supports the Immigration Judge’s resolution. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

2

To the extent the respondent claims ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has not sufficiently shown patent error or complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).

2005 WL 3709242 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JIACONG CHEN
File: AXX XX7 073 - El Centro
December 27, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Theodore C. Chen, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
The respondent is a 26-year-old native and citizen of China who entered the United States without being admitted or paroled, on or about November 28, 2004. On March 16, 2005, the date of the respondent’s scheduled removal hearing, neither the respondent nor his attorney was present in court. Based upon the respondent’s admissions and concessions, he was found removable as charged, and on March 16, 2005, the Immigration Judge ordered him removed in absentia to the People’s Republic of China.
 
On April 22, 2005, the respondent filed a motion to reopen to rescind an in absentia order of removal, arguing that exceptional circumstances prevented him from appearing at his hearing, specifically that he relied on the erroneous advice of his attorney that he did not need to appear at the March 16, 2005, hearing, because it was likely the Court would grant his pending motion to change venue. On May 18, 2005, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion, and we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision without opinion on September 12, 2005.
 
We received the respondent’s second motion to reopen to rescind an in absentia order on November 17, 2005. In the alternative, he has requested sua sponte reopening by the Board. The respondent acknowledges that an alien is limited by regulation to only one motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia order, and that motion must be filed within 180 days of the final administrative order of removal. 8 C.F.R § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). See also Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998) (ineffective assistance of counsel claim as an “exceptional circumstance” must be filed within 180-day period). He argues, however that the time and numerical limitations on motions to reopen are subject to equitable tolling in the Ninth Circuit, citing Socop-Gonzales v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1188 (9th Cir. 2001 (en banc). The respondent argues that the Ninth Circuit recognizes equitable tolling in cases involving assistance by an attorney or representative, coupled with fraudulent or deceptive conduct.
 
We note that most of the instances in which the 180-day period has been found to be subject to equitable tolling by the Ninth Circuit involve non-lawyers, notaries or immigration consultants engaging in ineffective assistance. See, e.g., Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000), Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999), and Fajardo v. INS, 300 F.3d 1018 (9th Cir. 2002). The Ninth Circuit found equitable tolling to apply where ineffective assistance involved an actual attorney who engaged in fraudulent activity causing essential action in a client’s case to be undertaken ineffectively, out of time, or not at all. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003). We do not find that the circumstances in this case warrant equitable tolling. Here, the first motion to reopen was filed by an attorney, who conceded that he had rendered ineffective assistance of counsel, by advising the respondent not to appear at his removal hearing because he believed his motion for change of venue would be granted. However, it does not appear that this attorney engaged in any fraudulent or deceptive activity. We conclude that equitable tolling does not apply, and the respondent’s second motion to reopen is barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23 (b)(4)(ii).
 
*2 Moreover, we find that we agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusions with respect to the issue of ineffective assistance of counsel. He found that the respondent had not established that he was prejudiced by counsel’s advice, despite the fact that it was clearly erroneous. He bases this on the fact that the respondent received numerous warnings of the consequences of failing to attend his removal proceedings beginning with the warnings contained on the Notice to Appear. As explained by the Immigration Judge (I.J. at 3), the respondent’s notice of hearing also explained that he may be removed in his absence if he failed to appear at his hearings. He was also informed that if he requested a change of venue, he must appear at the date and time listed on the form, unless he received written notice from the Executive Office for Immigration Review that venue has been changed. The Immigration Judge pointed out that the respondent had not explained why he chose to ignore these warnings, and rely on the advice of counsel. The Immigration Judge’s conclusion was that the respondent was not prejudiced by his former counsel’s actions, but finds himself in his current situation by failing to heed the Court’s warnings. Id.
 
Accordingly, in addition to concluding that the respondent’s second motion to reopen is barred, we decline to reopen the respondent’s case sua sponte as we do not find that the respondent has established exceptional circumstances which would warrant rescinding the in absentia order entered in this case.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 3709314 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YAN, WEI JIAN
File: AXX-XX1-194 - New York
December 27, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Zhang, Henry, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 07, 2004. The respondent, a native and citizen of China, contests the Immigration Judge’s denial of his second motion to reopen his April 23, 2004, in absentia removal order. The respondent previously filed a motion to reopen his in absentia removal order on June 1, 2004, which the Immigration Judge denied on June 3, 2004. The respondent filed the second motion to reopen on July 2, 2004. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s second motion to reopen because it exceeded the numerical limitations for the filing of motions to reopen before the Immigration Judge. We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision. The regulations provide that an alien may only file 1 motion to reopen removal proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent has not met any of the exceptions to the numerical limitations for the filing of motions to reopen before the Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4). Subsequent to the entry of the in absentia removal order, the respondent retained a new attorney to file his motions to reopen. The respondent alleged in his second motion to reopen that his prior attorney’s decision to attend his master calendar hearing more than 1 hour after it was scheduled constituted ineffective assistance which resulted in the entry of his in absentia removal order. The regulations provide that a motion to reopen may be filed within 180 days of an in absentia removal order if the alien demonstrates that he failed to appear due to exceptional circumstances. However, in the instant case, the respondent’s first motion to reopen did not meet the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to show ineffective assistance of counsel. He only attempted to meet the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, in his second motion to reopen. Furthermore, we note that the respondent does not allege that he received ineffective assistance from his current counsel who filed the first motion to reopen. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:

ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The respondent has failed to establish that the address to which notice was sent, 100 SW 12th Avenue, Deerfield Beach, Florida 33442-3117, was not his correct address. We note that the Notice to Appear (NTA) was sent by regular mail to that address and was not returned as undeliverable. Moreover, the respondent has never claimed non-receipt of the NTA, which contained the requirement that the respondent notify the Immigration Court of any change of address.
 
Although the respondent claims ineffective assistance of counsel because his prior counsel failed to advise him of his hearing date, we note that the hearing notice was sent to the respondent, not to an attorney. Moreover, he has failed to comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1997). Indeed, he has failed to establish that he had an attorney during the time period in question, for no attorney filed a Form EOIR-28 “Notice of Entry of Appearance as an Attorney” on his behalf until August 31, 2005, after the in absentia decision had been issued. We find that the Immigration Judge correctly found no basis to support a motion to reopen and dismiss the appeal.

2005 WL 3709377 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUVENCIA GARCIA
File: AXX XX2 097 - San Diego
December 23, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kevin M. Tracy, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lucinda Love
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - Entered without inspection
  
APPLICATION: Adjustment of Status
 
In a decision dated September 13, 2004, an Immigration Judge pretermitted the respondent’s application for adjustment of status but terminated the deportation proceedings. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings and for entry of a new decision.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, concedes that she entered the United States without inspection in January 1991, rendering her subject to deportation under section 241(a)(1)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B) (1995). In August 1996, an Immigration Judge ordered her deported in absentia after she failed to appear at a scheduled deportation hearing, and this order was executed by the former Immigration and Naturalization Service (the “INS”), now the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”), in November 1996.
 
In June 1997, the respondent reentered the United States without being admitted or paroled by an immigration officer and, in July 1998, she filed an affirmative application for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i), with the former INS. The INS denied the application in September 2000, concluding that the respondent was not eligible for adjustment of status because her prior deportation order was subject to reinstatement under section 241(a)(5) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(a)(5), and because she was inadmissible to the United States under various subsections of section 212(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a).
 
In June 2001, the respondent filed a motion in Immigration Court requesting rescission of her in absentia deportation order on the ground that she had not received proper notice of the hearing at which such order was rendered due to her former attorney’s deficient performance. (Exh. 7). The Immigration Judge denied the motion on June 20, 2001, finding that the respondent’s deportation in November 1996 deprived him of jurisdiction to entertain any motion to reopen on her behalf. (Exh. 8, at 1). In addition, the Immigration Judge noted that the respondent’s motion did not establish that her failure to appear was due to the ineffective assistance of her former counsel, (Exh. 8, at 2).
 
Two years later, in July 2003, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen in Immigration Court, alleging that she had received ineffective assistance of counsel from both her original attorney (who failed to inform her of the hearing at which she was ordered deported) and the attorney who filed her first motion to reopen (who failed to properly allege the ineffectiveness of her original lawyer as a basis for reopening). (Exhs. 9, 10). On September 30, 2003, the Immigration Judge granted this second motion, concluding that principles of equitable tolling excepted the motion from the numerical limitation on motions to reopen and that the respondent’s original failure to appear was due to the ineffective assistance of her first attorney, an “exceptional circumstance” that ostensibly warranted rescission of the in absentia deportation order. (Exh. 11). In this order, the Immigration Judge made no mention of the jurisdictional preclusion, arising from the respondent’s deportation in November 1996, which he had found dispositive in his order denying the first motion to reopen.
 
*2 Subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s September 2003 decision rescinding the in absentia deportation order issued in August 1996, venue was changed and a different Immigration Judge assumed jurisdiction over the proceedings. Upon determining that the respondent had been deported in November 1996, the new Immigration Judge concluded that his predecessor had lacked jurisdiction to entertain the respondent’s July 2003 motion to reopen. Accordingly, in a decision dated September 13, 2004, he set aside the prior Immigration Judge’s decision reopening the proceedings as “null and void,” and then terminated the proceedings on his own motion, apparently on the authority of this Board’s decision in Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281 (BIA 1998).
 
Upon review, we conclude that the Immigration Judge’s decision must be clarified with respect to several points. First, the Immigration Judge did not adequately explain his rationale for terminating the deportation proceedings, the effect of which is to dismiss the Order to Show Cause and negate the in absentia deportation order issued in August 1996. We note that these are not subsequent removal proceedings initiated upon the unlawful reentry of a previously-deported alien, as was the case in Matter of G-N-C-, supra. Rather, these are the original deportation proceedings, which the Immigration Judge concluded were erroneously reopened by a prior Immigration Judge. If the Immigration Judge terminates these proceedings, there is no valid order of deportation to be reinstated.
 
Second, although we understand the potential importance of an alien’s deportation on his or her ability to procure reopening of prior deportation proceedings, Matter of Okoh, 20 I&N Dec. 864 (BIA 1994); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), the Immigration Judge’s decision does not adequately address the fact that the respondent’s deportation in November 1996 may have been effected pursuant to an in absentia deportation order that was rendered at proceedings of which she did not receive notice. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(iii)(A)(2) (stating that a motion to rescind an in absentia deportation order may be filed “at any time” if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice); see also Mendez v. INS, 563 F.2d 956 (9th Cir. 1977) (holding that alien’s “departure” from the United States did not deprive court of jurisdiction to review deportation order where the departure was not lawful).
 
On remand, the Immigration Judge shall enter a new decision addressing the foregoing issues and may conduct such further proceedings as he deems appropriate under the circumstances.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.

2005 WL 3709344 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DIANA JONAH
File: AXX XX5 921 - New York
December 21, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eric O. Darko, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge dated July 26, 2004, finding the respondent removable as charged, denying her request for a continuance, but granting her application for voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b). See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his decision”).
 
As a preliminary matter, the respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge did not have jurisdiction over her proceedings because the Notice to Appear (“NTA”) was improperly issued.¿FN1º In particular, the respondent contends that the Immigration and Naturalization Service (the “Service”) ceased to exist on March 1, 2003; however, the NTA which placed the respondent in removal proceedings indicates that it was issued by the Service on January 21, 2004. Consequently, the respondent alleges that because the Service did not exist at the time the NTA was issued, the NTA was not valid and the Immigration Judge did not have jurisdiction over the respondent in these proceedings. We are not persuaded by these arguments.
 
First, we note that the duties and responsibilities of the Service were transferred to the offices of the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) by the Homeland Security Act of 2002, Pub. L. No. 107-296, 116 Stat. 2135 (2002). See also 6 U.S.C. 271 et seq. The regulations indicate that references to the Service on or after March 1, 2003 mean the offices of the DHS to which the functions of the former Service were transferred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1001.1. Further, the record reflects that the NTA was issued by an authorized officer and properly filed with the Immigration Court. 8 C.F.R. § 1239.1(a). Finally, the NTA provided the respondent with proper notice of the allegations and charges against her, and she was given ample opportunity, through counsel, to reply and contest these allegations and charges. We find no reason to conclude that the respondent’s NTA was improperly issued or that the Immigration Judge lacked jurisdiction in these proceedings.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent further argues that the Immigration Judge erred by refusing to continue her proceedings so her United States citizen husband could file a new petition for alien relative (Form I-130) on her behalf. The record reflects that the DHS had already denied a visa petition which the respondent’s husband had previously filed because of inconsistencies in the personal interview between the respondent and her husband. The respondent did not appeal this denial. An Immigration Judge may grant a motion for continuance only “for good cause shown.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of Silva-Rodriguez, 20 I&N Dec. 448 (BIA 1992). We do not find that the Immigration Judge abused his discretion in denying a continuance in this case where a visa petition, filed by the same petitioner on behalf of the same beneficiary for the same visa classification, had already been denied. Moreover, the denial of the respondent’s continuance in these removal proceedings does not preclude the respondent’s husband from filing a new visa petition directly with the DHS. Given these circumstances, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny a further continuance.
 
Finally, the respondent argues on appeal that due to the ineffective assistance provided by her former counsels, she and her husband were not properly prepared to provide evidence of their bona fide marriage at the interview before the DHS, and that this issue was not properly raised before the Immigration Judge. However, the respondent has not met the requirements for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Therefore, the respondent failed to make a proper claim against her former attorneys. Moreover, the respondent has not raised this issue in her visa petition proceedings. Consequently, we find no reason to remand the record to the Immigration Court.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

IN RE: YALDA MILAD YOUSSEF

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Russell W. Pritchett, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:

Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. An Immigration Judge issued an order of removal against the respondent on January 15, 2003. The respondent was granted a period of 120 days’ voluntary departure, after he waived the right to appeal to the Board. The Immigration Judge denied a motion to reopen, seeking adjustment of status, on April 17, 2003. We affirmed this decision on February 2, 2004.
 
The respondent submitted an untimely motion to reopen on September 29, 2005. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied, as he became statutorily ineligible for adjustment of status because he failed to voluntarily depart the United States, or seek an extension of the time to voluntarily depart. See section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d)(alien who fails to depart following a grant of voluntary departure is statutorily barred from applying for certain forms of discretionary relief, including adjustment of status). The respondent claims that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from an attorney who has been disbarred, Grosvenor Anschell. The respondent argues that Anschell submitted a deficient motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, and provided ineffective assistance before the Board and Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals. However, the respondent does not demonstrate that Anschell is to blame for his failure to depart as ordered by the Immigration Judge on January 15, 2003. Significantly, the respondent was represented by a different attorney at the time of that order, than the complained-of attorney. The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.

    1  Vacated by IN RE: KRIKOR ATAMIAN, BIA, November 12, 2008
2005 WL 3709351 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ATAMIAN, KRIKOR
File: AXX-XX0-480 - Las Vegas
December 19, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sarkisian, Ruben N.
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charles W. Baccus,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). On March 11, 2004, the respondent appeared with his attorney Seth Reszko, who was acting as co-counsel with attorney Rita Mahdessian. He was granted voluntary departure until July 9, 2004, and waived appeal. The respondent, through attorney Rita Mahdessian, appealed the March 11, 2004, decision, and on August 20, 2004, this Board dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction.
 
The respondent filed a motion to reconsider on September 17, 2004, claiming that his waiver of appeal was not knowing and intelligent and claiming that his former attorney, Rita Mahdessian, ineffectively represented him. The motion to reconsider was denied by the Board on October 28, 2004, because the respondent complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) with regard to attorney Rita Mahdessian, but not with regard to Seth Reszko, who had actually appeared on his behalf on March 11, 2004.
 
On November 15, 2004, the respondent made a motion to reopen, asserting that the regulatory time bar for motions to reopen should be tolled because he did not know of Rita Mahdessian’s ineffective assistance of counsel until August 20, 2004, when the Board dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction. He asserted that she ineffectively represented him by advising him not to voluntarily depart, and by filing a dilatory and frivolous appeal on his behalf even though she was aware that he had waived appeal. He noted that this dilatory appeal was not dismissed for lack of jurisdiction until after his voluntary departure had expired.
 
On May 11, 2004, an Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, noting in relevant part that the respondent had been previously advised that he needed to depart by July 9, 2004, and that he had waived appeal. On appeal, the respondent claimed that the Immigration Judge gave him too much credit for understanding immigration law. The fact that he waived his appeal based on his attorney’s assurance that everything would be taken care of, and that he relied on his attorney’s assurances in failing to depart, amounted to ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The record reflects that, at the time of the Immigration Judge’s March 11, 2004, decision, the only relief for which the respondent was eligible was voluntary departure (Tr. at 31). Although the respondent had potential eligibility for lawful permanent residence through his employer, he did not have that eligibility on March 11, 2004. Accordingly, there is no indication that attorney Seth Reszko, who represented him at that hearing, ineffectively represented him, or that he was prejudiced by any action of that individual. He applied for and received the only relief for which he was eligible. Moreover, the respondent received the extraordinary relief of 120 days of voluntary departure in exchange for the agreement not to appeal. As the respondent has not established that he would have been eligible for any form of relief had he been permitted to appeal, we find that the Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent had not established prejudice through any actions taken on his behalf by attorney Seth Reszko.
 
*2 The respondent has also asserted that attorney Rita Mahdessian ineffectively represented him by advising him not to voluntarily depart, and by filing a dilatory and frivolous appeal on his behalf. He complied with Matter of Lozada, supra, with regard to attorney Mahdessian. He further noted that, although the November 15, 2004, motion was technically untimely, it was filed within 90 days after the respondent discovered the ineffective representation, which occurred on August 20, 2004, when the Board dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction.
 
Upon our review of the record, we find that, even if the respondent’s motion was timely, he is ineligible for any form of relief from removal because of his failure to depart pursuant to the March 11, 2004, voluntary departure order. Section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. 1229c(d), provides in relevant part that if an alien is permitted to voluntarily depart under that section and fails to do so within the time period specified, that person is subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and is ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. 1229c, and 1229b, 1255, 1258, and 1259. Accordingly, even if the Immigration Judge had found the motion to be timely and had reopened proceedings, the respondent would have been statutorily barred from relief. Although it appears that the respondent relied to his detriment on attorney Mahdessian’s advice to file an appeal and to not depart during the pendency of that appeal, and although it appears that he may have been severely prejudiced by his attorney’s advice, there is no remedy in the immigration laws for an individual in this respondent’s situation. The respondent would need to seek remedy against his attorney outside these proceedings. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2005 WL 3709338 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OSCAR EDINBORO A.K.A OSCAR GORWIN RONALDO EDINBORO A.K.A. OSCAR EDIMBORO A.K.A. OSCAR EDENBORO
File: AXX XX9 524 - York
December 15, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
William Lore,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM: The respondent is a citizen of Guyana, and a lawful permanent resident of the United States. He was found removable from this country based upon the convictions alleged in the Notice to Appear, and charges contained therein. He was also found ineligible for any form of relief from removal. The respondent appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision, but that appeal was dismissed on September 21, 2005. That order was vacated by the Board on October 12, 2005, and the respondent’s appeal was reinstated.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge incorrectly found that he was convicted of an aggravated felony as defined under section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act. The respondent was convicted of violating New York Penal Code Section 220.39(1). Under this provision a person is guilty of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the third degree when he knowingly and unlawfully sells a narcotic drug. A violation of this provision is a 3rd degree felony.
 
We find that the respondent’s state drug conviction is an “aggravated felony” under sections 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) and 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act. To determine whether a state drug conviction constitutes a “drug trafficking crime” such that it may be considered an “aggravated felony,” we must look to the federal circuit court of appeals under which the case arises. See Matter of Yanez, 23 I&N Dec. 390 (BIA 2002). The United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit, under which jurisdiction the instant case arises, defers to the Board’s prior interpretation of the issue. See Gerbier v. Holmes, 280 F.3d 297, 299 (3d Cir. 2002). Under the Board’s former approach, a state drug conviction constitutes an “aggravated felony” if (1) it contains a trafficking element, or (2) it would be punishable as a felony under the Controlled Substances Act. See Matter of Davis, 20 I&N Dec. 536 (BIA 1992); Matter of Barrett, 20 I&N Dec. 171 (BIA 1990).
 
To determine whether a state drug offense involves “illicit trafficking,” we look to the state statute under which the alien was convicted. See Matter of Davis, supra, at 541-42 n.5. Trafficking includes unlawful trading or dealing for profit, in a drug classified as a “controlled substance” under the federal Controlled Substances Act (CSA). See Gerbier v. Holmes, supra. Herein, the respondent was convicted for knowingly selling a narcotic drug that the record reflects was crack cocaine. The respondent’s conviction was also punishable as a federal felony. See 21 U.S.C. § 841(b)(1)(c). Based upon this information, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent’s conviction constituted an aggravated felony.
 
*2 Having found that the respondent is a lawful permanent resident convicted of an aggravated felony on January 23, 2003, it follows that he is ineligible to be considered for cancellation of removal, or a waiver of inadmissibility under sections 212(c) and (h) of the ACt. See Matter of Yeung, 21 I&N Dec. 610 (BIA 1996) (Therein, we explained that an alien, such as the respondent, who previously has been admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident and who has been convicted of an aggravated felony since the date of such admission is ineligible for a section 212(h) waiver). The respondent has also not established that he merits a new hearing based upon having been the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel as none of the procedural requirements for proving such has been met.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2005 WL 3709338 (BIA)
End of Document

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
    1  Opinion Vacated in Part by IN RE: JORGE LUIS MORENO a.k.a. Jorge Luis Moreno Gomez, BIA, January 24, 2007
2005 WL 3709334 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MORENO, JORGE LUIS
File: AXX-XX3-832 - Baltimore
December 15, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL/MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hernandez, Arturo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Smith Turner, Leslie E.
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The respondent appeals the order of removal, arguing that prior counsel allegedly failed to notify the respondent of the August 17, 2004, due date for the filing of an application for cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. However, the record reflects the respondent was provided with the Motion to Withdraw Appearance filed by counsel, which included notification of the due date for the cancellation application. The respondent does not deny timely receipt of this motion, or of the Immigration Judge’s July 20, 2004, order granting the motion. Furthermore, the respondent has submitted no evidence that he has complied with the procedural requirements for claiming ineffective assistance of counsel, as outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), as there is no evidence that the attorney was notified of the allegations and provided an opportunity to respond, or that a complaint was filed with the state bar.
 
While the appeal has been pending, the respondent filed a motion to remand for an opportunity to apply for adjustment of status via a United States citizen spouse he married while in proceedings. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4). The record includes a receipt for the Form I-130 filed by his current wife, a completed Form I-485, a naturalization certificate for his wife dated November 22, 1985, a marriage certificate dated August 31, 2004, a Judgment of Absolute Divorce from the respondent’s prior spouse dated September 13, 2002, a divorce decree for the new spouse’s prior marriage dated February 5, 1999, income tax returns filed by his current wife prior to the marriage, and 3 affidavits from friends.
 
The evidence of the bona fides of the current marriage is conflicting, in that one of the affidavits from a friend indicates the couple purchased a home from the friend’s client in 1997, which would have been while the couple were previously legally married to other spouses. Furthermore no additional evidence of the bona fides of the marriage have been introduced, such as documentation showing joint ownership of a property, a lease showing joint tenancy of a common residence, or evidence of the commingling of financial resources. 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(a)(1)(iii)(B). The evidence regarding the bona fides of the marriage is not clear and convincing. See Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002), modifying Matter of Arthur, 20 I&N Dec. 475 (BIA 1992). Accordingly, the motion will be denied, and the appeal dismissed.
 
*2 FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the DHS. In the event of the failure to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order. See section 240B(b) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f).
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
