2005 WL 3802121 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IFEANYI OKPECHUKWU
File: AXX XX3 971 - Detroit
December 12, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rickie Emmanuel Ibe, Esq.
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mark Jebson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has filed a timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s March 3, 2005, decision denying his motion to reconsider the denial of his motion to reopen in absentia proceedings. The appeal will be sustained, and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
The record reflects that jurisdiction over the respondent’s claim was originally vested in the Houston Immigration Court. While there, the respondent apparently appeared at numerous hearings¿FN1º without fail, and properly informed the Immigration Court when he changed his address in February of 2002. In June of 2004 the respondent’s former representative filed a motion to transfer venue to Michigan, which was granted in a July 14, 2004, Immigration Judge’s decision (Exh. 4). A copy of the decision was sent to the respondent’s then-counsel of record in Houston, Texas, Mr. Anugwom. The notice clearly identified Mr. Anugwom as appearing “on behalf of respondent,” with the certificate of service on the notice reflecting that it was served not on the respondent, but on the “alien’s attorney/representative.” A hearing notice was thereafter sent to Mr. Anugwom at the same address to which all prior correspondence had been sent, informing him that the respondent was scheduled for a master hearing before the Detroit Immigration Court on August 11, 2004.
 
When he did not appear as scheduled, the respondent was ordered removed in absentia in an Immigration Judge’s August 11, 2004, decision. On December 30, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings, asserting that he never received notice of his hearing. In doing so he noted that his address in Michigan had been incorrectly listed in the order granting his change of venue, and stated that Mr. Anugwom had stopped representing the respondent after his move. The respondent stated that he believed he would be notified of all court hearings following the change of venue, but was not informed that a hearing had been scheduled by either the Immigration Court or his former attorney (Motion to Reopen at 3). As such, he maintains that he did not get proper notice of his hearing.
 
In denying the respondent’s motion to reopen the Immigration Judge noted that, as notice had been directed to the respondent’s former counsel, he did receive proper notice. Citing section 239(a)(2)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. The Immigration Judge also noted that the respondent did not comply with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637(BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
*2 The respondent thereafter filed a timely motion to reconsider in February of 2005, with which he submitted a letter from Mr. Anugwom. In the letter, the respondent’s former counsel asserted that after the motion to change venue was granted, his office had not received any further correspondence from any Immigration Court regarding the respondent’s hearing date. The Immigration Judge denied this motion in a March 3, 2005, decision finding that the respondent had presented no factual or legal error to support the claim. Citing Matter of Ramos, 23 I&N Dec. 336, 338 (BIA 2002).
 
A respondent alleging ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra at 639; namely, the allegation must be supported by (1) an affidavit describing the agreement that was entered into with the attorney with respect to the actions to be taken and the representations made by the attorney; (2) proof that the petitioner has notified the attorney of the allegations and that the attorney has had an opportunity to respond; and (3) a statement about whether a complaint has been filed with an appropriate disciplinary authority if the representation involved a violation of legal obligations to the client. In addition, the petitioner must demonstrate that he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel. See Id. at 640; see also Huicochea-Gomez v. INS, 237 F.3d 696 (6th Cir. 2001).
 
The Immigration Judge correctly found that the respondent had not satisfied the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, supra. However, we find that the record supports a finding that the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel from Mr. Anugwom. We do not find Mr. Anugwom’s unsworn and self-serving statement that he did not receive the respondent’s hearing notice to be sufficient evidence of nondelivery. In the absence of clear evidence to the contrary, it is presumed that public officers, such as the United States Postal Service, properly discharge their duties. United States v. Chemical Foundation, Inc., 272 U.S. 1, 14-15 (1926); Butler v. Principi, 244 F.3d 1337, 1340 (Fed. Cir. 2001); see also Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995). The hearing notice was sent to the same address that all the other numerous items of correspondence from the Immigration Court had been sent, without any reported delivery problems. There is no evidence in the record that the hearing notice was returned by the Postal Service due to delivery or other difficulty. As such, we do not find that Mr. Anugwom’s unsworn and very generalized statement establishes that service of the heating notice was not properly effected upon him. Compare In respondent The Yoder Co, 758 F.2d 1114, 1118-9 (6th Cir. 1985). Moreover, as noted, the decision granting the change of venue made it clear that not only was he considered to be still representing the respondent, but that service of that notice was made only on him and not also to the respondent. Mr. Anugwom, as counsel to the respondent, should have known that until he filed a motion to withdraw as the respondent’s representative and/or another representative had filed a notice of entry, he would continue to represent the respondent. Whether Mr. Anugwom was not aware of this fact, or whether he chose to ignore the same, he provided the respondent with ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*3 We recognize that the respondent has some responsibility in the situation presented, but we find, on this particular set of facts, that the record clearly establishes that the respondent has acted responsibly in all matters before the Immigration Courts to date. His United States citizen wife has filed a visa petition on his behalf, further supporting the respondent’s implicit claim that he is motivated to diligently prosecute his case. Moreover, while his assumption that he would directly receive all notices from the Immigration Court after the change of venue was granted was incorrect, he clearly was not informed of his misapprehension by his former counsel, who should have done so. The respondent has unequivocally stated that his former counsel never contacted him after the motion to change venue was granted.
 
In light of our findings above, we find that reopening of the respondent’s claim was warranted, and the Immigration Judge erred in denying both the respondent’s motion to reopen and the motion to reconsider. On remand, the respondent should be allowed to pursue all forms of relief for which he may her eligible. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s August 11, 2004, January 11, 2005, and March 3, 2005, decisions are hereby vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings in accordance with this decision.

It appears that the respondent was scheduled, and appeared, for about nine different hearings at the Houston Immigration Court.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

IN RE: GUO CHUAN NIANG A.K.A. SAUI MA A.K.A. GUO QUAN LIAN
File: AXX XX7 878 - New York
December 8, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frank R. Liu, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa A. Radcliffe
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 21, 2003, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States, following the respondent’s failure to appear for a hearing on May 2, 2003. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that he established exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear due to the ineffective assistance of former counsel. With his appeal brief the respondent submitted an affidavit, in which he claims that he arrived at his attorney’s office early, but she sent him to the Immigration Court late, and the court had already closed by the time he arrived. Subsequently he discovered that he had been ordered removed in absentia. The respondent has also submitted a letter to his former attorney, Florence Segler, Esquire, dated July 30, 2004, in which he informed her of the allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel against her; and a response from Ms. Segler, dated August 9, 2004.
 
We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&NDec. 599 (BIA 1996).
 
In his decision, the Immigration Judge appears to imply that ineffective assistance of counsel is not an exceptional circumstance, which is incorrect based on relevant case law. On appeal the respondent is represented by new counsel and now alleges ineffective assistance of counsel as the basis for his failure to appear. He has submitted additional evidence on appeal, including his affidavit and a letter written by his former counsel, in which she admits that she should have sent the respondent to the Immiigration Court earlier than she did. The Board ordinarily will not consider any previously available evidence first proffered on appeal, as its review is limited to the record of proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995). However, we find such evidence probative of the respondent’s claim of exceptional circumstances due to ineffective assistance of counsel and will remand the record to the Immigration Judge for consideration.
 
*2 ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.

IN RE: ROBINSON RAFAEL MARTINEZ

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ismael Gonzalez, Esquire
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 27, 2005, in which the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for his failure to appear for a hearing on February 18, 2005. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded.
 
On appeal and in his motion to reopen, the respondent, who may be eligible for relief under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, contends that he etablished exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear. See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i), (e)(1). The respondent, through his attorney, argues that the respondent did not purposely fail to attend his hearing. Rather, the respondent did not attend the hearing based upon the specific instruction of his attorney, who was awaiting a decision on a motion to change venue.¿FN1º
 
While the Board has held that the mere filing of a motion for a change of venue does not relieve. the respondent of his responsibility to appear, in the instant case, the respondent’s failure to appear was clearly the result of the ineffective assistance of his counsel, who has admitted that he advised the respondent that he did not need to appear for the February 18, 2005, hearing.¿FN2º Thus, based on the facts presented in this case, we find that the respondent has established exceptional circumstances which would excuse his failure to appear on February 18, 2005. Accordingly, the appeal will be sustained, the proceedings will be reopened, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.
 
ORDER: Respondent’s appeal is sustained, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to change venue is granted, and venue is changed from Buffalo, New York, to Newark, New Jersey.

IN RE: ROSITA PASCUAL MATEO
File: AXX XX2 670 - Honolulu
December 7, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carmen DiAmore-Siah, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Heith M. Kaneshige
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact.
Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed an Immigration Judge denial of a motion to reopen. We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision for the reasons stated therein, with the following additions. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872 (BIA 1994). In an affidavit accompanying her motion, the respondent first asserted, without evidentiary support, that she had not been “in the right state of mind” and did not know what she was doing when she had previously withdrawn her claim for a fraud waiver and accepted voluntary departure. Yet, the respondent also indicated therein that she deliberately pursued this course because she allegedly feared incarceration and deportation if she lost her case. We concur with the Immigration Judge that the respondent had essentially failed to set forth a sufficient reason for reopening proceedings.
 
The respondent’s assertion on appeal that her former attorney provided her with ineffective assistance of counsel was not made before the Immigration Judge, nor does the respondent explain why she waited until her appeal to attempt to make this new claim. In addition to her failure to fully set forth a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, there is no evidence that the respondent acted with reasonable diligence to preserve any of her claims. See Rodriguez-Lariz, 282 F.3d 1218, 1225-26 (9th Cir. 2002); see also Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The record reflects that the respondent admitted she fraudulently secured her lawful permanent resident status, yet in her motion, there is no acknowledgment of her own responsibility for perpetrating this fraud. To the contrary, she attempts to absolve herself, merely stating that she signed paperwork “without being aware of the contents.” Her affidavit fails to acknowledge the fraudulent documentation which she presented to immigration authorities of her husband’s purported death, even though she knew he was actually alive. Given her failure to acknowledge her culpability, much less shown any remorse for her actions, we would deny the underlying waiver the respondent seeks in the exercise of discretion. Accordingly, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s discretionary denial of the motion to reopen. See Section 237(a)(1)(H) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(H).
 
*2 FURTHER ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

2005 WL 3802131 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PEDRO ARNOLDO PARADA-FERRERA
File: AXX XX1 852 - Los Angeles
December 5, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carlos A. Cruz, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was before the Board on three prior occasions. On February 8, 2000, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal as moot for having been abandoned. Subsequently, on November 9, 2001, we dismissed the respondent’s motion to reconsider and reissue our decision of February 8, 2000. On August 4, 2005, we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, which he had filed on May 9, 2005, finding it was untimely and the respondent did not act with due diligence in asserting his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against his first attorney, Mr. Antonio Bueno. The respondent has now filed a “Motion to Reconsider,” arguing that he also received ineffective assistance from Mr. Alexander N. Lopez, the attorney who filed his original motion to reconsider. The motion will be denied.
 
Motions to reopen and motions to reconsider are separate and distinct motions with different requirements. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N 399 (BIA 1991). A motion to reconsider is designed to request that a decision be reexamined in light of additional legal arguments or a change of law. The respondent has not established what errors existed in our prior decision that would warrant a different result in this case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). In contrast, a motion to reopen requests to reopen proceedings so that new evidence can be presented and a new decision entered on a different factual record, normally after a further evidentiary hearing. The respondent’s present motion is, in fact, a motion to reopen because the respondent seeks to present new evidence regarding the alleged ineffective assistance he received from his attorney, Alexander N. Lopez. However, the respondent has not provided an adequate explanation why this evidence was not previously submitted to the Board. An alien may only file one motion to reopen his removal proceedings before the Board. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Because the respondent has already filed a motion to reopen in this case, the respondent’s present motion is subject to the numerical limitations on motions provided in the regulations. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.

2005 WL 3802139 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AINSLEY DONAVAN PECKOO
File: AXX XX7 365 - Baltimore
December 5, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION/APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Juan C. Washington, Esq.
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Termination
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent filed a timely appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 13, 2005, denying his motion to reopen the removal proceedings in his case. The respondent had argued that subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s October 4, 2004, decision, finding the respondent removable as charged, the conviction¿FN1º underlying the charge of removal was modified by the District Court for Baltimore, Maryland, pursuant to a writ of error coram nobis alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, and as the respondent was resentenced to nine months imprisonment, the conviction could no longer sustain the grounds of removal. In denying the respondent’s motion, the Immigration Judge cited the Board’s decision in Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003), and stated that “¿wºhile the coram nobis writ raises various reasons for the filing of the writ, there is no indication (e.g., order of the court) to reflect that ¿theº modification did not occur to help the respondent avoid the immigration consequences.” The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision, and the entry of a new decision.
 
In order to qualify as an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act, the sentence imposed for a theft offense must be for imprisonment for at least one year. The new sentence imposed for the respondent’s 1998 Maryland theft conviction is now less than one year. The question here is how the criminal court’s modification of his original sentence affects the respondent’s removability from the United States, as charged.
 
At the outset, we agree with the Immigration Judge that it is unclear as to the particular basis for the criminal court’s modification of the respondent’s sentence. However, subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s decision, the Board determined in Matter of Cota-Vargas, 23 I&N Dec. 849 (BIA 2005), that a trial court’s decision to modify or reduce an alien’s criminal sentence nunc pro tunc is entitled to full faith and credit by the Immigration Judges and the Board of Immigration Appeals, and such a modified or reduced sentence is recognized as valid for purposes of the immigration law without regard to the trial court’s reasons for effecting the modification or reduction. Matter of Song, 23 I&N Dec. 173 (BIA 2001), clarified; Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003), distinguished.
 
*2 Consequently, the record will be remanded to enable the Immigration Judge to consider the modification of the respondent’s sentence for his 1998 Maryland theft conviction in light of our decision in Matter of Cota-Vargas, supra, as it affects his removability for an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act, and to ascertain whether there presently remain grounds for removal. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion, and the entry of a new decision.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

Therecord reflects the respondent was convicted on March 30, 1998, in the District Court for Baltimore, Maryland, for the offense of theft over $300, in violation of Article 27, section 342 of the Annotated Code of Maryland, for which a sentence of 1 year imprisonment, was imposed.

2005 WL 3802101 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: THEIEVENDRAN KIRITHARAN
File: AXX XX9 880 - Miami
December 2, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Visuvanathan Rudrakumaran, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 15, 2005, in which he denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for his failure to appear for the hearing on February 25, 2005. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded.
 
On appeal and in his motion to reopen, the respondent contends that he established exceptional circumstances due to ineffective assistance of counsel. His attorney of record, Mr. Rudrakumaran, states that he failed to advise the respondent that if the Immigration Judge did not make a determination on his motion for a change of venue, the respondent would have to attend the hearing. He also asserts that his failure to advise the respondent to attend the hearing and his failure to address the allegations in the motion should not result in penalizing the respondent. He notes that he submitted an attorney affirmation to that effect. Mr. Rudrakumaran also argues that the attorney affirmation attached to the motion to reopen satisfies the first two requirements pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Specifically, it encompasses the agreement entered into with counsel regarding the alien’s representation and provides an opportunity for counsel to address them. In reference to the requirement of filing a State Bar complaint, Mr. Rudrakumaran states that the primary goal of this requirement is to protect against collusion between an alien and his attorney to achieve delay. However, he contends that there is no suggestion of any collusion between the respondent and his attorney. Finally, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion for a change of venue on the same day of the hearing constitutes exceptional circumstances.
 
We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996). In the case at bar, the respondent’s attorney has admitted that the respondent failed to appear at the hearing due solely to his failure to advise the respondent that he must attend the hearing if the Immigration Judge did not make a decision on the motion for a change of venue prior to the scheduled hearing. The respondent’s attorney submitted an attorney affirmation in which he discusses his representation of the respondent and his failure to properly advise the respondent regarding the pending motion for a change of venue. We find that the respondent has presented a valid claim of ineffective assistance. of counsel. We note that the requirement that a bar complaint be filed is to ensure that collusion does not occur, and that the claim of ineffective, assistance of counsel is a valid one. See Matter of Lozada, supra. Therefore, as we find that counsel did afford the respondent ineffective assistance and in light of the lack of any bar complaint, we shall forward this matter to the Executive Office for Immigration Review Bar counsel for review and the institution of any disciplinary measures deemed appropriate. The appeal will be sustained. The proceedings will be reopened, and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded for further proceedings

2005 WL 3802127 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OLGA MARITZA SABILLON-ABRIGO
File: AXX XX0 041 - Harlingen
December 1, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Heidi J. Meyers, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)º - Entered without inspection
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER
 
PER CURIAM. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s April 13, 2005, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen deportation proceedings that became final in February 1987.
 
Specifically, we find no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent, in December 1986, did knowingly, intelligently and voluntarily accept an order of voluntary departure from the Immigration Court with an alternative order of deportation in the event of her failure to timely depart. (R. Motion to Reopen, Exh. D). Furthermore, we find no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent failed to depart in compliance with her voluntary departure order, and that this failure made her subject to an administratively final order of deportation in February 1987. Because the respondent was present in the United States under an outstanding order of deportation as of February 1987, any motion to reopen she wished to file in Immigration Court was due by not later than September 30, 1996. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). The respondent’s motion was not filed until March 2005, and therefore the Immigration Judge properly denied it as untimely.
 
The respondent suggests that the regulatory filing deadline for motions to reopen does not apply to her or, in the alternative, that any such deadline should be equitably tolled on the facts of her case. We disagree. The deadlines for filing motions to reopen were established by a final agency regulation which became effective on July 1, 1996, and aliens who were present in the United States under an outstanding order of deportation at the time the regulation went into effect were given 90 days to file such motions, measured from the July 1, 1996, effective date of the final rule. See 61 Fed. Reg. 18900-01, 18908 (April 29, 1996). Publication of these filing deadlines in the Federal Register was sufficient to give the respondent notice of them. 44 U.S.C. § 1507; Fed. Crop Ins. Corp. v. Merrill, 332 U.S. 380, 384-85 (1947).
 
As to the respondent’s invocation of the equitable tolling doctrine, we would simply note that neither this Board nor the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this proceeding arises, has he!d that equitable tolling applies to the filing deadlines set forth in the Immigration Court’s motions regulations. See also Abdi v. Attorney General, 2005 WL 3046741 (11th Cir. 2005). Furthermore, while an Immigration Judge does have limited discretionary power to reopen proceedings sua sponte, even after expiration of the relevant filing deadlines, such action is only warranted upon a showing of truly exceptional circumstances. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). No such exceptional circumstances have been shown in this instance.
 
*2 First, the respondent’s present assertion that she did not knowingly, intelligently and voluntarily accept a voluntary departure order is simply irreconcilable with assertions that both she and her former attorney of record made in various sworn documents that were submitted to the Immigration Court in December 1986. (Motion to Reopen, Exh. D, unnumbered pages 2-4). Absent a showing of egregious circumstances rising to the level of ineffective assistance, decisions of counsel are binding on the respondent in immigration proceedings. Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986). Although not framed as an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the respondent essentially contends that her former attorney (and, presumably, the notary who witnessed her affidavits and attested to the fact that they had been read to her in her native language) defrauded both herself and the court, for reasons unknown. At a minimum, the respondent’s former attorney should be given an opportunity to respond to such a serious allegation, but the respondent concedes that she has not complied with any of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), including the requirement that the attorney whose professional conduct is being questioned be notified of the charges and given a chance to explain his or her conduct.
 
Contrary to the respondent’s assertion on appeal, moreover, there is nothing inherently unfair about the streamlined procedures followed in her deportation proceedings. It is well-established that a full-blown deportation hearing need not be held where the respondent has admitted the factual allegations and charges in the Order to Show Cause and where there is no legal issue remaining to be decided. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.48(b). In this instance, the respondent’s affidavits contain unequivocal admissions of deportability, as well as affirmative acknowledgment that future Immigration Court proceedings would be conducted in her absence. Under such circumstances it is generally presumed that the Immigration Judge will dispose of the matter by summary order, as was done here. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.50(b).
 
Finally, the respondent has not demonstrated that she has exercised due diligence in filing her motion. Even viewing the facts of this case in the light most favorable to the respondent, she became aware of the existence of her outstanding deportation order in September 2003. Yet she did not file her motion to reopen with the Immigration Court until March 18, 2005, more than 500 days later. Although there is some indication that the respondent may have filed a prior motion in the wrong venue, even this motion was not filed until June 2004, many months after she discovered her predicament. Under the circumstances, we see no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s decision not to suspend the September 30, 1996, filing deadline in the respondent’s case.
 
*3 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. *1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). For the reasons set forth in the decision (I.J. at 1-3), we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is removable under sections 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) and (a)(7)(A)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) and (a)(7)(A)(i)(I). We further agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is ineligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act in conjunction with an application for relief in the form of adjustment of status (I.J. at 3). On appeal, the respondent contests the fairness of the proceedings. Specifically, he asserts that the transcript of the proceedings may have been defective and that he has been the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. He has not shown, however, that he suffered prejudice during these proceedings. See Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir.1999). In sum, the respondent has failed to put forth arguments or evidence which would now persuade us to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision or to remand the record for further proceedings. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the alien is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the alien fails to so depart, the alien shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the alien fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the alien shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2005 WL 3802252 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARGARITA HURTADO-SOLANO
File: AXX XX6 978 - Miami
November 29, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Douglas Lux, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny her motion to reopen an order of removal entered in absentia. According to the respondent, she hired an organization called Global Immigration Services to represent her, and was told by a person named “Emelyn” that she was an attorney. The respondent indicated that she informed Global Immigration Services that she had changed her address, and was told that it would provide the respondent’s new address to the Immigration Court. According to the respondent, the organization did not do so, and she was ordered removed from the United States when she failed to appear for her hearing. The respondent argues on appeal that she was ineffectively represented by an organization that purported to her that it was licensed to practice law.
 
Contained in the record is a letter from Emelina Carter that is addressed to the Florida Bar Association. Therein, she acknowledges having spoken to the respondent, but denies that she told the respondent that she was a lawyer, or would file the change of address form with the Immigration Court.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent failed to submit a sworn affidavit with the Immigration Court in support of her motion. The complaint submitted to the Florida Bar is not an affidavit filed with the Board. Even if we considered the complaint as an affidavit, we note that the respondent has failed to show that she was the victim of attorney negligence. We recognize that the Ninth Circuit has also held that if an alien is ineffectively represented by a non-lawyer who purports to be a licensed attorney, the alien is not barred from proving an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000). However, these proceedings fall under the jurisdiction of the Eleventh Circuit that has not recognized that legal theory. Further, even if we applied the Ninth Circuit’s reasoning to these proceedings, we are unable to make a finding that the respondent was misrepresented by Global Immigration Services or Emelina Carter. Emelina Carter denies the respondent’s allegations, and we cannot determine what was or wasn’t said to the respondent by Emelina Carter or Global Immigration Services based on the submissions contained in the record. The respondent therefore has failed to satisfy her burden of proving that an “exceptional circumstance” caused her to miss her hearing.
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2005 WL 3802276 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARMEN ELIZABETH CONTRERAS
File: AXX XX2 041 - Houston
November 28, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Dax Brian Deason, Esquire
*1 This matter was last before the Board on May 31, 2005, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s January 18, 2005, denial of her second motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of deportation entered on May 25, 1989. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on August 26, 2005. The motion will be denied.
 
As a preliminary matter, the respondent’s motion contains some aspects that appear to be in the nature of a motion to reconsider; for example, claims that the record does not support the factual determination that proper notice was sent to attorney Mendoza (her attorney at the time of the deportation hearing) and arguments that the correct legal standard to apply is whether prior counsel received “actual notice” of her deportation hearing. Because her motion was not filed within 30 days of our decision, and is therefore untimely as a motion to reconsider, we will not address any arguments that appear to be challenges to the legal and factual determinations made in that decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b).
 
As for the motion to reopen, any evidence she seeks to present now with regard to her ineffective assistance of counsel claim against attorney Mendoza could previously have been obtained and presented, and therefore cannot be considered new and previously unavailable under the applicable regulatory standards. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Nevertheless, she urges that she should be allowed to submit evidence of compliance with Lozada at this time because the subsequent attorneys who represented her for the prior two motions and at the Board all provided her with ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit has held that to prevail on a due process challenge, an alien must make a showing of “substantial prejudice.” De Zavala v. Ashcroft, 385 F.3d 879, 883 (5th Cir. 2004). Even if we were to assume, for sake of argument, that the evidence submitted with this new motion to reopen meets the procedural requirements set forth in Lozada, we nevertheless find that the respondent has failed to establish ineffective, assistance of counsel by Mr. Mendoza such that reopening of the in absentia proceeding would have been warranted. The respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim against attorney Mendoza is based on her assertion that he never informed her that her deportation hearing was scheduled for May 25, 1989. Yet, the respondent’s own affidavit establishes that, upon release from Harlingen, she went to California to live with her father, instead of going to live with her uncle at the address in Spring, Texas, that she had provided to counsel and the Immigration Court. Motion to Reopen, tab D (copy of affidavit initially submitted with Second Motion to Reopen). An affidavit from her uncle that she previously submitted further establishes that her uncle only lived at that address until “about April 1989.” Second Motion to Reopen, tab B. Neither affidavit indicates that the respondent or her uncle made any attempt to keep in contact with attorney Mendoza or let him know where the respondent was actually residing. As the Immigration Judge correctly found, proper notice of the scheduled hearing was sent to attorney Mendoza at his correct address on April 18, 1989 (Exh. 4). The Immigration Judge reasonably declined to give much weight to the assertions claiming counsel did not inform her of the scheduled hearing, especially where the affidavits do not reflect any attempt to keep counsel informed of the respondent’s whereabouts or a means to contact her, or that she or her family attempted to contact attorney Mendoza to ascertain the status of her deportation proceedings at any time during the 15 years that elapsed before she filed her motion to reopen. Nothing in this motion to reopen provides new evidence pertaining to these issues. Thus, given that the respondent has not established ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney Mendoza, her claims of ineffective assistance of counsel against subsequent attorneys fail because she has not shown that she was prejudiced by their actions. De Zavala v. Ashcroft, supra. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.

2005 WL 3802275 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OMAR HERRERA-OROZCO
File: AXX XX1 832 - San Diego
November 28, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Bill Waddell, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alan S. Rabinowitz
Deputy Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service), will be denied.
  
BACKGROUND
 
The respondent was charged with being subject to removal under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i), for being present in the United States without being admitted or paroled. On September 4, 2002, an Immigration Judge granted the respondent a period of 120 days voluntary departure, and the respondent waived his right to appeal to this Board. The respondent then filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, based on his marriage to a United States citizen. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen on February 7, 2003. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent was ineligible for adjustment of status under sections 245(a) and (i) of the Act, because he admittedly entered the United States without being admitted or paroled, and did not file the visa petition by April 30, 2001.
 
Former counsel Richard Pray then filed a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Judge, and argued that the respondent had not, in fact, conceded that he entered the United States without being admitted or paroled. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reconsider on April 23, 2003. The Immigration Judge stated that the respondent had indeed conceded that he entered the United States without being admitted or paroled, and therefore could only adjust his status under section 245(i) of the Act. The respondent was, however, ineligible for adjustment of status under that provision because a visa petition was not filed by April 30, 2001.
 
The respondent filed an appeal of the Immigration Judge’s April 23, 2003, decision. Represented by Richard Pray, the respondent argued that he was eligible for adjustment of status, because he had not admitted entering without inspection. We affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision on February 2, 2004.
 
The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on July 18, 2005, who determined on August 18, 2005, that jurisdiction over a motion was with this Board. On August 26, 2005, the respondent filed an untimely “Motion to Reopen due to Ineffective Assistance of Counsel.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
  
ANALYSIS
 
The respondent argues that the motions deadline should be tolled because he consulted with current counsel about the case in May, 2005 (Respondent’s Mot., at p. 6). Even were the motion deadline tolled, the pending motion would be denied.
 
The respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from attorney Richard Pray, because Pray should not have conceded that the respondent entered the United States without being admitted or paroled (Respondent’s Mot., at pp. 3, 7). Yet, the respondent in his affidavit fails to explain why, if he in fact entered the United States with a passport, he did not discuss with counsel why counsel was conceding that he entered without inspection. The respondent fails to explain why he contends that prior counsel was aware that he had “not entered without inspection” at the time of the removal hearing (Respondent’s Mot., at p.3; Respondent’s Affidavit). The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.

2005 WL 3802282 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN HIPOLITO MELCHOR-CANO
File: AXX XX9 057 - Imperial
November 23, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anthony M. Cacavio
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; cancellation of removal
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This matter was last before the Board on July 25, 2005, when we dismissed as untimely the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s November 29, 2004, decision denying his requests for cancellation of removal and adjustment of status and granting him the privilege of voluntary departure. The respondent has timely filed a motion to reconsider our prior decision. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the motion.
 
The respondent does not deny that his appeal was untimely filed but rather attributes the late filing to ineffective assistance provided by a “non-attorney.”¿FN1º In making this claim, however, the respondent has failed to comply with any of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See generally Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226-27 (9th Cir. 2002) (applying Lozada requirements to ineffective assistance claims against non-attorneys).
 
Although the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held that satisfaction of the formal requirements of Lozada need not be demonstrated where ineffective assistance of counsel is clear from the administrative record, no such showing has been made here. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000); Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
In this case, there is no indication that the respondent was prejudiced by the late filing of his appeal. The respondent is seeking relief from removal in the form of cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b), and also adjustment of status. However, he is not eligible for either form of relief.
 
Although he claims that he has now lived in the United States for over 10 years, he is ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act for lack of a showing that he has 10 years of continuous physical presence prior to service of the Notice to Appear on November 10, 2004. See Tr. at 29 (indicating that the respondent has lived in the United States since 1995). Further, although he is the beneficiary of a second-preference visa petition, a visa is currently unavailable to him (Tr. at 28). See Department of State, Visa Bulletin, No.88, Vol. VIII (December 2005) (listing February 1, 1992, as the current priority date for Mexico in the case of an unmarried son of lawful permanent resident visa category); see also Respondent’s Motion, attachment (Notice of Action listing July 5, 1996, as the priority date for the underlying approved visa petition). Therefore, he cannot demonstrate eligibility for adjustment of status, even assuming that he otherwise qualified for such relief.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.

2005 WL 3802220 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RACHID SAJIR
File: AXX XX9 885 - Philadelphia
November 22, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jean L. Celestin
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present in the United States without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals the immigration Judge’s decision, dated June 27, 2005, denying the respondents motion to reopen proceedings. The respondent argues on appeal that he was provided ineffective assistance of counsel. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Act claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representation counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond; and, (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec, 553, 556 (BIA 2003); cf. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Furthermore, the respondent must show that he was prejudiced by his representative’s performance. See Matter of Assaad, supra.
 
In this case, the respondent has completely failed to comply with any of the requirements for establishing that the received ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent did not provide an affidavit detailing the agreement between prior counsel and himself. He did not provide evidence that he notified prior counsel of his allegations or that he filed a complaint with the proper disciplinary authorities. Moreover, while the respondent claims that he is eligible to adjust his status, he failed to provide the application and all supporting documents. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (A motion to reopen proceedings for the purpose of submitting an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documentation.). Consequently, the respondent has not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s performance. See Matter of Assaad, supra. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is without merit.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

2005 WL 3802232 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NOE MUNOZ ORTIZ
File: AXX XX6 017 - Portland
November 21, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Dan Larsson, Esquire
 
ORDER
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated, in his or her decision”).
 
In a decision dated May 18, 2005, an Immigration Judge found that he had no jurisdiction to reopen these proceedings because the respondent was physically removed from the United States in accordance with the order of removal entered on January 12, 1999. The record reflects that the respondent was ordered deported from the United States in absentia, and sought reopening based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Immigration Judge denied the motion, and the respondent was removed from the United States before he had a 30-day opportunity to file an appeal of that decision with the Board. A stay of deportation was not pending at the time of the respondent’s removal. On appeal, the respondent argues that his due process rights have been violated because he did not have an opportunity to appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision.
 
In the instant case, no appeal was pending when the respondent was deported from the United States. Even if one had been, we note that the regulations specify that a departure during the pendency of an appeal constitutes a withdrawal of said appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.4. Furthermore, we have jurisdiction to render an appeal moot where a controversy has become so attenuated to where a change in the law or an action by one of the parties has deprived an appeal or motion of practical significance. Matter of Luis, 22 I&N Dec. 747, 753 (BIA 1999). We also note that the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this case falls, has found that the deportation or removal of an alien must be lawful in order to be considered a “departure.” See Wiedersperg v. INS, 896 F.2d 1179 (9th Cir. 1990); Estrada-Rosales v. INS, 645 F.2d 819 (9th Cir. 1981); Mendez v. INS, 563 F.2d 956 (9th Cir. 1977).
 
In the instant case, even if an appeal was pending before the Board, the respondent was subject to a final order of removal when the DHS removed him from the United States. A stay of deportation was not in existence, and an alien is not accorded an automatic stay of removal while appealing the denial of a motion. The removal of the respondent was therefore lawful even assuming an appeal had been filed. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act (providing for a stay of removal only “pending disposition of the motion by the Immigration Judge”).
 
*2 We further find that the DHS was not prohibited from executing the order of removal within the 30-day appeal period following the denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. We therefore agree with the Immigration Judge that he had no jurisdiction to reopen these proceedings subsequent to the respondent’s deportation. See Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281, 288 (BIA 1998); Matter of Okoh, 20 I&N Dec. 864, 865 (BIA 1994).
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2005 WL 3802232 (BIA)
End of Document

2005 WL 3833028 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FEI LONG YANG
File: AXX XX3 401 - New York
November 16, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gang Zhou, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s April 29, 2005, denial of his motion to reopen removal proceedings and to rescind the in absentia order of removal entered against him on October 12, 2000. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of China. In his motion to reopen filed before the Immigration Judge, the respondent asserted that his prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance, and that the time period for filing a motion to reopen should be tolled. He also sought reopening based on changed country conditions in China. The Immigration Judge denied the motion as untimely. On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in failing to toll the time for filing his motion to reopen.
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia may be rescinded if the alien files a motion to reopen, within 180 days of the date of the order of removal, demonstrating that he failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); Matter of Guzman, Interim Decision 3392 (BIA 1999). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act. Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act, a motion to reopen may be filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he did not receive notice in accordance with 239(a) of the Act. If a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is established, the time period for filing a motion to reopen may be equitably tolled. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000).
 
The respondent asserts that he did not receive notice of his October 11, 2000, hearing because he was “not then living” at the address where the July 20, 2000, hearing notice was sent, nor was “he able to receive the notice in care of any resident domiciled at that address.” Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 1. The respondent also asserts that he notified his counsel of his new address a few days after his release from detention in late March 2000, and that he requested that venue be transferred to New York. The record reflects that a separate notice, also dated July 20, 2000, was sent by the New York Immigration Court by certified mail to both the respondent and his counsel reflecting that venue had been transferred to New York.
 
*2 We find that the hearing notice sent to the respondent at his last known address constitutes sufficient notice pursuant to Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996). The evidence in the record indicates that the notice of the October 11, 2000, hearing was mailed to the respondent at his last known address.¿FN1º While both the hearing notice and the notice of change of venue were mailed on the same date, neither mailing was returned to the Immigration Court as undeliverable. The respondent was served personally with the Notice to Appear, which informed him of the requirement that he notify the Immigration Court “immediately” of any change of address (Exh. 1). The respondent failed to notify the Immigration Court of any change in his address.
 
As noted above, the respondent states that he notified his counsel of his new address a few days after his release from detention in late March 2000. Attachment 1 to Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 3. He also notes that his prior attorney was indicted in September 2000,¿FN2º and he argues that his former counsel’s ineffective assistance prevented him from appearing at his October 11, 2000, hearing. Even assuming that the respondent’s former attorney provided ineffective assistance in failing to notify the Immigration Court of the respondent’s change of address or to explain to the respondent his responsibility of notifying the court, we find that the respondent did not exercise due diligence in moving to reopen proceedings after learning that an in absentia order of removal had been entered. The respondent states that he had notice of the removal order one month after it was issued. While he argues that he attempted to obtain legal advice from several other lawyers on how to reopen proceedings over the next 4 years, we find that these efforts did not constitute due diligence. See Iavorski v. INS, supra. The statute provides for the filing within 180 days of a motion to reopen proceedings after the entry of an in absentia order of removal. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. Although the respondent had notice of the removal order in November 2000, he did not file his motion to reopen until March 18, 2005, more than 4 years later. The respondent’s affidavit, which outlines his efforts in obtaining new counsel, is insufficient to establish that he acted diligently during the entire 4-year period between his awareness of the removal order and the filing of the motion to reopen.
 
In sum, while an alien in removal proceedings has the responsibility of notifying the Immigration Court of any change in address, the respondent in this case never provided a new address to either the DHS or the Immigration Court. Even assuming that the respondent’s former attorney rendered ineffective assistance in failing to notify the Immigration Court of the new address or to explain to the respondent his responsibility of doing so, which would toll the time for filing a motion to reopen, we find that the respondent did not exercise due diligence in filing his motion after learning that an in absentia order of removal had been entered.¿FN3º Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent acknowledges in his appeal brief that the hearing notice was mailed to his “last know address.” Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 1. He states, however, that he was no longer living at the address at the time of mailing. The respondent further asserts, “¿oºn information and belief,” that the notice did not reach him, and that he was unable “to receive the notice in care of any resident domiciled at that address.” Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 1.

2005 WL 3952690 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANCISCO CORPAS-ARROCHA A.K.A. FRANKLIN WILLIAMS A.K.A. FRANCISCO CORPAS A.K.A. WILLIAM R. KENNEDY
File: AXX XX6 189 - Oakdale
August 30, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Monique Harris
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
  
APPLICATION: Termination of Removal Proceedings; Adjustment of Status
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s August 1, 2004, decision sustaining the charges of removability against him and pretermitting his applications for relief from removal. The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7) (2005), but his request for a waiver of the appellate filing fee is granted. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(c). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent is a native of Panama who entered the United States without inspection. In 1989 he was convicted in New York State of first-degree manslaughter, for which he served a term of imprisonment of 15 1/2 years. On the basis of the aforementioned facts, the respondent was found removable by the Immigration Judge as an alien convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude and as an alien present in the United States without having been admitted or paroled. See sections 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) and 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), 1182(a)(6)(A)(i). On appeal, the respondent makes four principal arguments. First, he appears to contend that the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”), formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service (the “INS”), is precluded from initiating the present removal proceedings against him because it had previously placed him in deportation proceedings under prior law but failed to prosecute those proceedings to a conclusion. Second, he argues that the Immigration Judge erred by failing to allow him to apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), in conjunction with an application for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i). Third, he contends that he may be a United States citizen by virtue of his birth in the Panama Canal Zone. And fourth, he maintains that his prior attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel during his proceedings before the Immigration Judge. We reject each of the respondent’s appellate claims.
 
*2 The respondent was the subject of deportation proceedings in 1992. After listening to the tape recording of those prior proceedings in the presence of counsel for the respondent and the DHS, the Immigration Judge determined that those deportation proceedings had been terminated by the Immigration Judge without prejudice. (Tr. at 11). There were no objections raised to the Immigration Judge’s characterization of the prior disposition. By terminating the respondent’s prior deportation proceedings “without prejudice,” the prior Immigration Judge brought those proceedings to finality while ensuring that they would have no preclusive effect vis-a-vis the initiation of subsequent proceedings with respect to the same claim. Dillard v. Security Pacific Brokers, Inc., 835 F.2d 607, 608 (5th Cir. 1988) (noting that dismissal without prejudice has no res judicata effect) (citing Plumberman Inc. v. Urban Sys. Dev. Corp., 605 F.2d 161, 162 (5th Cir. 1979)). Contrary to the respondent’s assertion on appeal, the Immigration Judge’s termination of his deportation proceedings was not akin to adjournment or administrative closure. Therefore, the respondent is not presently the subject of deportation proceedings and he is not entitled to have his present removal proceedings adjudicated under legal standards that were in effect under prior law.
 
The respondent claims that the DHS has treated him unfairly by waiting for more than a decade to institute proceedings against him and would have us estop the DHS from pursuing the present removal proceedings on equitable grounds. Yet even if we agreed with the respondent that the DHS’ delay in pursuing his removal was somehow inequitable and prejudicial to his interests (a highly dubious proposition in light of the fact that for nearly the entire period between 1992 and the present the respondent was in prison for first-degree manslaughter), we would simply note that neither this Board nor the Immigration Judge has authority to equitably estop the DHS from pursuing an otherwise lawful course of action that is committed to its discretion. Matter of Hernandez-Puente, 20 I&N Dec. 335 (BIA 1991). The decision whether (or when) to initiate removal proceedings is committed to the discretion of the DHS, and the propriety of that decision may not be reviewed by the Immigration Judge or this Board. Matter of Ramirez-Somera, 20 I&N Dec. 564, 566 (BIA 1992).
 
We also reject the respondent’s challenge to the Immigration Judge’s decision pretermitting his application for a section 212(h) waiver in conjunction with adjustment of status under section 245(i). Such a waiver would not avail the respondent because it cannot be used to waive his inadmissibility under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act. While inadmissibility under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act is no obstacle to adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act, the respondent is not otherwise eligible for section 245(i) adjustment because he has not proven that he is the beneficiary of an immigrant visa petition filed on or before April 30, 2001. See section 245(i)(1)(B) of the Act. The respondent has provided some documentation on appeal to indicate that his United States citizen wife attempted to file an immigrant visa petition (Form I-130) on his behalf during the late 1980s. (R. App. Brief, Exh. C). However, this same documentation shows that the INS sent a notice to the respondent’s wife in April 1988 asking her to provide supplemental information in support of the visa petition (including photographs and biographic information for both herself and the respondent), yet there is no indication that this supplemental information was ever provided to the INS. We note that the timing of this INS request for further information corresponded closely to the date of the respondent’s arrest and prosecution in New York on manslaughter charges. Hence, we find no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is not prima facie eligible for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act.
 
*3 As noted, the respondent also suggests that he may be a United States citizen by virtue of his birth in the Panama Canal Zone. The burden to establish alienage in a removal proceeding is upon the DHS. When there is a claim of citizenship, however, it is well-established that one born abroad is presumed to be an alien and must go forward with the evidence to establish his claim to United States citizenship. See, e.g., Matter of Tijerina-Villareal, 13 I&N Dec. 327, 330 (BIA 1969). The respondent concedes that he was born in the Panama Canal Zone and therefore the DHS has satisfied its threshold burden of proving the respondent’s foreign birth. Accordingly, the respondent has the burden of demonstrating by a preponderance of the evidence that he is in fact a United States citizen. Under section 303 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1403, an individual born in the Panama Canal Zone after February 26, 1904, is a United States citizen if one or both of the individual’s parents were United States citizens at the time of the individual’s birth. While the respondent does appear to have been born in the Canal Zone after February 26, 1904, he has offered no evidence that would support the conclusion that either of his parents were United States citizens at the time of his birth. Accordingly, he has not demonstrated by a preponderance of the evidence that he is a United States citizen under section 303 of the Act or that it would be worthwhile to remand the record for plenary rehearing on the citizenship question.
 
Finally, the respondent asserts that his prior attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to request his release from DHS detention on bond, by failing to request an adjournment of the removal proceedings during the pendency of a Freedom of Information Act (FOIA) request that the respondent had made and by failing to keep the respondent informed of the type of information he would need to provide to the Immigration Judge in order to demonstrate his eligibility for relief from removal. Assuming arguendo that the respondent’s prior attorney provided deficient representation, the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim fails because he has not shown how this ostensibly deficient performance prejudiced him. Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F.3d 84 (5th Cir. 1994) (alien claiming due process violation must establish substantial prejudice); Ka Fung Chan v. INS, 634 F.2d 248 (5th Cir. 1981) (same). Specifically, he has not explained how the outcome of his proceedings could have been changed had his attorney acted differently. The respondent appears to be subject to mandatory detention as a result of his very serious criminal conviction and the evidence he provides on appeal—which appears to have been given to him in response to his FOIA request—does not establish his citizenship or his eligibility for any form of relief from removal. Accordingly, we do not find that the respondent has suffered a due process violation arising from ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*4 The respondent does not appear to raise any further issues on appeal, and therefore the following order shall issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
