2005 WL 3952740 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROBERT MICHAEL ST. GEORGE GRANT
File: A34 113 347 - Somers, Connecticut
August 25, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening, termination
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on July 29, 1993, when we sustained the appeal filed by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) and reversed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 23, 1992, granting the respondent’s request for a waiver of inadmissibility pursuant to section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c). On May 26, 2005, the respondent filed a motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of his former counsel, and requesting termination of the proceedings on the basis that he is a derivative citizen of the United States. The motion will be denied.
 
At the outset, the claim to United States citizenship, as has been proffered by the respondent, raises issues directly related to this Board’s jurisdiction over the instant case. Thus, a claim to citizenship is never waived and may be tendered at any point during proceedings. The burden of proof in establishing alienage in deportation proceedings is on the Service. United States ex rel. Bilokumsky v. Tod, 263 U.S. 149 (1923); United States v. Neelly, 207 F.2d 281 (7th Cir. 1953). However, one born abroad is prima facie an alien. Matter of A-M-, 7 I&N Dec. 332 (BIA 1956). Furthermore, where it is undisputed that an alien sought to be deported was born in a foreign country and came to the United States from that country, the burden of proof to establish United States citizenship is upon the alien. United States v. Neelly, supra. Such an alien is required to affirmatively establish his citizenship claim. Matter of A-M-, supra. A base claim of citizenship, unsubstantiated by any evidence is insufficient to meet the alien’s burden. United States v. Neelly, supra.
 
However, on the record before us, we are unable to find support for the respondent’s citizenship claim. See Matter of Tijerina-Villareal, 13 I&N Dec. 327, 330 (BIA 1969) (finding that a respondent who could not prove his father resided in the United States for the requisite number of years required by the Act to retain citizenship did not meet his burden of proof that he was a citizen). The citizenship of children born abroad to an alien parent who subsequently becomes a United States citizen is governed by section 321 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1432, the law which was in effect at the time the respondent became 18-years-old, and which describes the conditions under which such citizenship is automatically acquired, and not section 322 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1433, as he contends in his motion.1 Prior to its recent repeal, Section 321 of the Immigration and Nationality Act provided as follows:
*2 (a) A child born outside of the United States of alien parents … becomes a citizen of the United States upon the fulfillment of the following conditions:
(1) The naturalization of both parents: or
 
(2) The naturalization of the surviving parent if one of the surviving parents is deceased; or
 
(3) The naturalization of the parent having legal custody of the child when there has been a legal separation of the parents or the naturalization of the mother if the child is born out of wedlock and the paternity of the child is not established by legitimation; and if
 
(4) Such naturalization takes place while such child is under the age of eighteen years; and
 
(5) Such child is residing in the United States pursuant to a lawful admission for permanent residence at the time of the naturalization of … the parent naturalized under clause (2) or (3) of this subsection, or thereafter begins to reside permanently in the United States while under the age of 18 years.
 
 
 
Examining the case before us, the respondent admitted that he was born in Jamaica, and has been residing in the United States pursuant to his admission as a lawful permanent resident on April 4, 1975 (Tr. at 20). Significantly, based on the evidence accompanying the motion, at the time the respondent attained the age of eighteen, only the respondent’s father had become a naturalized United States citizen. The respondent has presented no evidence that his mother had also naturalized prior to his eighteenth birthday.2 In order to benefit from the derivative citizenship provision of section 321 of the Act, the respondent would have to demonstrate that both his mother and father were naturalized prior to his having attained the age of eighteen, and there is no evidence that she ever naturalized. Cf. section 321(a)(1) of the Act. A person who claims derivative United States citizenship has the burden to establish it. See Matter of Leyva, 16 I&N Dec. 118 (BIA 1977); Matter of Tijerina-Villareal, supra. The respondent has not sustained his burden, in this regard.
 
Furthermore, even if we were to find the respondent has generally complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 627 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), regarding his ineffective assistance of counsel claims, and accepting his allegations as true, it does not alter any aspect of the Board’s decision. At the outset, we consider the motion to reopen is untimely. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on July 29, 1993. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before September 30, 1996. The record, however, reflects that the respondent’s motion was not filed until May 26, 2005. Consequently, the respondent’s motion appears to be untimely filed, and not properly before us. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
*3 Moreover, even if the respondent’s motion had been timely filed and he had established compliance with the Lozada criteria, reopening would still not be warranted. The respondent argues that his former counsel was obligated to file a petition for review with the Second Circuit and provided ineffective assistance when he failed to do so. We note, however, the petition for review that his former attorney failed to file was with the Second Circuit and not with the Board. Consequently, this alleged ineffectiveness, if true, occurred after the Board rendered its July 29, 1993, decision. We find no evidence of irregularity or Board error regarding the service of our July 29, 1993, decision. Thus, any error before the Second Circuit by former counsel does not implicate an action by the Board since the Board made no error as a result of former counsel’s alleged ineffectiveness on this ground. Moreover, the respondent can show no prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The Board can provide no remedy for any alleged ineffectiveness that arose after the Board rendered its decision. The proper forum over his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel lies with the Second Circuit and not with the Board. In addition, we find no support for the respondent’s contention that his former attorney provided ineffective assistance when he failed to explore his claim as to derivative citizenship, as the discussion above reflects the respondent was not entitled to said relief. See section 321(a)(1) of the Act.
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

The automatic citizenship provisions now found in section 322 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1433, as amended by the Child Citizenship Act of 2000, Pub. L. No. 106-395, 114 Stat. 1631 (“Child Citizenship Act”), are not applicable to his situation. We have addressed this specific issue and found that the Child Citizenship Act is not retroactively applicable. Matter of Rodriguez-Tejedor, 23 I&N Dec. 153 (BIA 2001). Therefore, as the respondent was over the age of 18 years as of the effective date of the Child Citizenship Act, he is ineligible for automatic citizenship under that provision.

2

The record reflects that at the time of his hearing before the Immigration Judge on September 23, 1992, the respondent testified that his mother was then a lawful permanent resident of the United States (Tr. at 21).

2005 WL 3952724 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VIATCHESLAV JAROV
File: A73032569 - New York
August 23, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jack Sachs, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mary Cheng,
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s November 4, 2004, decision denying his October 6, 2004, motion to reopen. The motion seeks an opportunity to apply for adjustment of status based on his July 3, 2001, marriage to a United States citizen. This was the respondent’s second motion to reopen, and it failed to address at all the fact that he was ordered deported in absentia on December 2, 1999. Nor does it reference the fact that a motion to reopen and rescind that order, that was filed by his prior attorney, was denied by the Immigration Judge on June 29, 2001, because he failed to demonstrate exceptional circumstances. However, the Immigration Judge denied the second motion on November 5, 2004, for failure to meet the standards for rescinding an in absentia order.1
 
On appeal, the same attorney who filed the second motion raises, for the first time, an allegation of ineffective assistance against the attorney who prepared the first. He also seeks to present new evidence that the respondent’s failure to appear in 1999 was due to exceptional circumstances. Although an alien in deportation proceedings may file more than one motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia deportation order, such motions must still be made within the statutory 180-day period unless a proper equitable tolling claim is made. Ineffective assistance of counsel can provide a basis for tolling the statutory period. However, in this case, the respondent has not complied with any of the procedural steps set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making such a claim, nor has he demonstrated that he exercised due diligence in seeking to reopen on that basis. Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 130 (2d Cir. 2000) (finding deadline for motions to reopen deportation proceedings may be equitably tolled, but alien failed to exercise due diligence required for equitable tolling). The motion that was filed by the attorney whose competence is now being challenged was denied by an Immigration Judge more than four years ago and the respondent offers no explanation for the delay. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the Section 242B rescission standards have not been met.
 
An alien is not required to rescind his or her deportation order under section 242B of the Act if he or she is pursuing an application for new relief and there is no evidence that the respondent received the required warnings under section 242B(e) of the Act. See Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). However, the motion to reopen is then subject to the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. §§ 3.2(e) and 3.23(b)(3). Under these standards, the respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was filed more than 90 days after the final order of deportation. He is accordingly precluded from having his motion considered under the general time limitations for a motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion is the correct result. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent is in deportation proceedings and the rescission standards of section 242B(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(c) apply.

2005 WL 3952728 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RUDOLPH MCFARLANE
File: AXX XX0 903 - New York
August 23, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on May 10, 2005, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent’s application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act abandoned. On June 10, 2005, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider, arguing ineffective assistance of his prior counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not filed a brief in response to the respondent’s motion. The respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider “is a request that the Board reexamine its decision in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or perhaps an argument or aspect of the case which was overlooked, while ¿aº motion to reopen is usually based upon new evidence or a change in factual circumstances.” See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 n.1 (BIA 1991). Although characterized as a motion to reconsider, the applicant’s motion does not specify errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision, based on the evidence that was previously presented to the Board, but rather seeks to present evidence not previously submitted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1). As the applicant’s motion does not specify errors of law or fact in the prior Board decision based on evidence previously presented, the motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Inasmuch as the respondent seeks to present evidence that was not previously submitted, the motion is properly characterized as a motion to reopen. In his motion, the respondent argues that his prior counsel provided ineffective assistance, based upon his prior counsel’s failure to submit his application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c)of the Act prior to the expiration of the deadline established by the Immigration Judge. However, the respondent has failed to comply with the procedural requirements as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). While the respondent has submitted an affidavit outlining the alleged ineffectiveness of his prior counsel, the respondent has failed to submit evidence indicating that he filed a complaint with the Disciplinary Committee of the New York Supreme Court or evidence that he informed his prior counsel of the alleged ineffective assistance and gave the attorney an opportunity to respond to the allegations. We find, therefore, that the respondent has failed to satisfy the procedural requirements for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD
 
2005 WL 3952728 (BIA)

2005 WL 3016047 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANTONIO V. TORRES AGUILERA
File: AXX XX1 837 - Newark
August 17, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Heidi J. Meyers, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa M. Golub
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent deported in absentia for his failure to appear for the hearing on February 29, 1996. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that he established ineffective assistance of counsel, and that equitable tolling should be applied to find his motion timely filed. The respondent also asserts that he is eligible for adjustment of status based on his marriage to a United States citizen. The respondent has submitted new evidence on appeal. The Board ordinarily will not consider any previously available evidence first proffered on appeal, as its review is limited to the record of proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995).
 
We find that the respondent has complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Furthermore, the Third Circuit Court of Appeals has determined that the 180-day time limitation may be equitably tolled. See Borges v. Gonzales, 402 F.3d 398 (3rd Cir. 2005)(180-day time limitation for motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances could be equitably tolled, and could be tolled for fraud). However, even if equitable tolling is applied for untimely motions to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent failed to exercise due diligence in pursing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and seeking reopening based on adjustment of status. As the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent became aware of his former counsel’s misrepresentations in June 2004 when he took possession of his file from her and discovered that she had never filed a motion to reopen on his behalf. See Respondent’s Affidavit, dated January 31, 2005. The respondent failed to file his motion to reopen until March 17, 2005, which is more than 180 days after he discovered the misrepresentations made by his former counsel. Therefore, the respondent failed to file his motion to reopen timely.
 
Finally, the respondent also seeks reopening to apply for adjustment of status. An alien is not required to rescind his or her deportation order under section 242B of the Act if he or she is pursuing an application for new relief, and provided there is no evidence that the respondent received the required warnings under section 242B(e) of the Act. See Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). Rather, the motion to reopen is only subject to the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3).¿FN1º Under these standards, the respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was filed more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s decision. He is accordingly precluded.from having his motion considered under the general time limitations for a motion to reopen.¿FN2º Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

Pursuant to 8 C.F.R.§§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3), a motion to reopen for the purpose of acting on an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documentation.

2

We therefore decline to address the issue of prima facie eligibility for relief from deportation, or whether the respondent would deserve a favorable exercise of discretion.

Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PORFIRIO D. DELA CRUZ
File: AXX XX3 863 - New York
August 17, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose Alexis Rojas, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 8, 2004, denying his motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for his failure to appear at the hearing on July 8, 2003. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that he established exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear due to the ineffective assistance of former counsel. He claims that he never received notice of the hearing, and he was not aware of the hearing date. The respondent indicates that he has informed his former attorney of his ineffective representation and has provided him with an opportunity to respond, but his former counsel has not contacted the respondent. With his appeal brief, the respondent has submitted an affidavit and a copy of a letter to his former attorney dated April 21, 2005, in support of his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Board ordinarily will not consider any previously available evidence first proffered on appeal, as its review is limited to the record of proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995).
 
The record reflects that the Immigration Judge had previously granted the respondent’s motion to reopen subsequent to an in absentia order dated March 13, 2001. Furthermore, a hearing notice dated April 29, 2003, reflects that the respondent received oral and written notice in court of the hearing scheduled for July 8, 2003. Consequently, the respondent’s claim on appeal that he never received notice of the hearing is contradicted by the evidence of record. Furthermore, although the respondent asserts a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel on appeal, he has not complied with all of the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2005 WL 3016064 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHANDRA MALLA
File: AXX XX4 026 - Buffalo
August 16, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Justin Conlon, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Steven Connelly
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen of Nepal, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his motion to reopen. We have jurisdiction over this matter pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. The appeal is dismissed.
 
The record reflects that an Immigration Judge on March 26, 2001, ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States because he failed to appear for his hearing. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on January 26, 2005, but that request was denied on February 25, 2005. This timely appeal followed.
 
Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C) provides, in pertinent part, that an in absentia order may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) states, “The term ‘exceptional circumstances’ refers to exceptional circumstances (such as serious illness of the alien or death of an immediate relative of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances) beyond the control of the alien.”
 
In the instant case, the respondent indicated through counsel, that his former attorney instructed him not to appear for his hearing. He argued on appeal that he was victimized by his attorney’s negligence, and he should not be denied an opportunity to seek adjustment of status even though his motion to reopen was filed untimely.
 
In Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000), the court recognized the doctrine of “equitable tolling” of the statute of limitations regarding the filing of a motion to reopen. The court held that an alien must demonstrate not only that the alien’s constitutional right to due process has been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll. Id. In that case, the court concluded that the alien failed to demonstrate such diligence, and concluded that equitable tolling did not apply. Id.
 
Accordingly, under Iavorski v. INS, supra, the Board must first address whether the respondent was denied due process in these proceedings based upon the ineffective assistance of his former counsel. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved applicant setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the applicant in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640.
 
*2 In denying the respondent’s motion, the Immigration Judge noted that the respondent neglected to provide a response of former counsel to the complaint, or an explanation for why a response could not be supplied. The Board need not resolve whether the respondent satisfied the procedural requirements for a claim based upon ineffective assistance of counsel because we find that the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely. The respondent indicated on page 3 of his affidavit that he received a letter from the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) a few weeks after his hearing that informed him that he had been ordered deported in absentia. According to the respondent, he contacted his former lawyer, but was instructed that the matter would be resolved. It was not until four years later did the respondent inquiry about his immigration status with a different attorney. Under these circumstances, we find that we cannot toll the statute of limitations for seeking reopening to a date after the respondent learned that he was subject to an order of removal. On such date, the respondent should have been aware that he may have received improper legal advice from his former attorney. It was therefore incumbent on him to seek reopening based upon ineffective assistance of counsel within the next 180 days. Having not done so, renders his request for reopening untimely. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act. We therefore are unable to find that reopening is appropriate.
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

2005 WL 3016041 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TERESITA ESCOBEDO-GONZALEZ
File: AXX XX1 383 - Los Angeles
August 16, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carlos A. Cruz, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. In our last decision, dated August 28, 2003, the Board denied the respondent’s prior motion to reopen because it was untimely and barred by the number restrictions of 8 C.F.R. 3.2(c). The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on March 12, 1998, and filed a timely motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, which he denied on June 6, 1998. The Board dismissed, as moot, an appeal from the denial of the first motion on December 12, 1998. On September 24, 2004, the respondent filed with the Board a motion to reconsider, which was substantively a second motion to reopen, raising a new claim of ineffective assistance against Mr. Bueno, the attorney who had prepared her first motion. However, as noted in a footnote in our last decision denying the motion, the respondent did not meet the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). We further found that the respondent’s second motion to reopen failed to establish exceptional circumstances warranting the exercise of our limited discretion to reopen proceedings sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
The respondent filed the instant motion, which is substantively her third motion to reopen, on May 9, 2005. It essentially restates the basis for the second motion, but is supported by evidence that may be viewed as substantially complying with the Lozada requirements. However, neither the respondent’s second motion nor her third motion states when she ultimately contacted her present counsel and concluded, based on her present counsel’s advice, that Mr. Bueno may have been ineffective in presenting her claim that exceptional circumstances prevented her appearance at the March 12, 1998, hearing. The motions simply state that “¿aºfter many years of questions and doubts about ¿ herº deportation order, the respondent visited the Law Offices of Carlos A. Cruz for assistance.” See Respondent’s September 24, 2002, motion at 2 and the May 9, 2005, motion at 3.
 
In any event, it is clear that she learned of the potential ineffective assistance claim against Mr. Bueno by the time Mr. Cruz filed the September 24, 2002, motion, since the allegation of ineffective assistance was made at that point. She did not file a motion to reconsider our August 28, 2003, denial of her second motion, and she did not file the third motion that is now before us until 19 months later. Under these circumstances, even if there had been a basis for tolling the 180-day time limit, the clock started again at least by September 2002, and had long run out when the third motion was filed. See Fajardo v. INS, 300 F3d 1019 (9th Cir. 2002) (the 180 time limit may be tolled until the point where the alien becomes aware of the harm caused by the ineffective assistance of counsel.). Once she became aware that she had been harmed by ineffective assistance, the statutory period did not continue be tolled while she made a second attempt with her present counsel to substantially comply with the Lozada requirements. The rescission requirements have not been met.
 
*2 The formal rescission requirements need not be met in certain circumstances where the alien is seeking to apply for relief that was previously unavailable. In such cases, however, the motion to reopen is subject to the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. §1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3). Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). The motion fails to meet the general requirements governing reopening. The motion is not timely because it was filed more than seven years after the final order in this case rather than within the 90-day time limit for general motions. Further, it is not supported by a completed adjustment application or evidence of the bona fides of the marriage. Finally, the fact that, after a final order of removal, the 35-year-old respondent married a United States citizen in his seventies and an invalid, and may potentially be eligible for relief based on that marriage if a visa petition is ultimately approved, does not warrant sua sponte reopening. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (our discretion to reopen proceedings sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to circumvent the regulations even when enforcing them may result in hardship).
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.

IN RE: MOSHE AMIR ARZOOAN
File: AXX XX7 884 - Buffalo
August 9, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Yoram Nachimovsky, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Georgina M. Picos
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent in the instant case has filed a document titled “Urgent Request For Relief and Immediate Release and/or Bond Determination.” Due to its content, it will be construed by the Board as a motion to reopen. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on March 19, 2004. With certain exceptions, a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the administratively final order. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before June 17, 2004. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until July 6, 2005. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time.
 
Although the respondent now raises a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not complied with the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We further note that inasmuch as the respondent has waited more than one year before filing the instant motion, the respondent has not exercised due diligence in pursuing the benefit of equitable tolling based on ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent also asks for a change in custody status. At this time, neither an Immigration Judge nor this Board has authority to set bond conditions because a final order of removal has been entered in the respondent’s case.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.

2005 WL 3016134 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WILLIAM ENCARNACION MATOS A.K.A. DANNY VARGAS A.K.A. WILLIAM ENCANOS
File: AXX XX3 014 - Varick
August 5, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose A. Rojas, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c)
 
The respondent appeals from the May 5, 2005, decision and order of the Immigration Judge denying his motion to reopen and to rescind an in absentia order. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
This case was previously before this Board on February 25, 1998, when we upheld the finding of deportability and the respondent’s ineligibility for relief from deportation, and then on October 11, 2001, when we granted the respondent’s motion to reopen his proceedings in order seek a waiver under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), based on the change of law in INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001). The Immigration Court issued a Notice of Hearing to the respondent’s prior counsel and to the address of the respondent’s mother. Neither the respondent nor his counsel appeared at the scheduled hearing and the Immigration Judge entered an in absentia order on March 15, 2002. On June 1, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reopen in order to rescind the in absentia order. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) did not oppose reopening; however, the Immigration Judge denied the motion.
 
We find that the Immigration Judge erred in denying the motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent failed to show due diligence or remedy any ineffective assistance of counsel in the filing of his motion to reopen. However, a motion to reopen may be filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he was in federal or state custody and he failed to appear through now fault of his own. Section 242B(e)(3) of the Act; see Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995). The respondent affirmed that he was in custody at the time of the scheduled hearing and unable to appear. The DHS did not contest this contention and did not oppose the motion to reopen. The record reflects that the respondent has remained in either state or federal custody since prior to the hearing date. The respondent, therefore, is not required to demonstrate due diligence or equitable tolling in the filing of his motion to reopen as there is no time limit for such filing.
 
The Immigration Judge also denied the motion to reopen as a matter of discretion. He considered the respondent’s most recent conviction in December 2004 for sale of a controlled substance. He determined that given the serious adverse factors of record, which include at least 3 drug-related convictions and a lack of rehabilitation, that the respondent would not merit a section 212(c) waiver in the exercise of discretion. However, the respondent is not required to show prima facie eligibility for the 212(c) waiver in the instant motion. Rather, he must demonstrate whether he has reasonable cause for his failure to appear. See Matter of Ruiz, 20 I&N Dec. 91 (BIA 1989). The respondent demonstrated that he was in custody and that he failed to appear through no fault of his own. Thus, he is entitled to a full evidentiary hearing on his application for the section 212(c) waiver. Accordingly, the appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is sustained and the in absentia deportation order is rescinded.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.

2005 WL 3016095 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PEDRO ARNOLDO PARADA-FERRERA
File: AXX XX1 852 - Los Angeles
August 4, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carlos A. Cruz, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was before the Board on two prior occasions. On February 8, 2000, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal as moot for having been abandoned. Subsequently, on November 9, 2001, we dismissed the respondent’s motion to reconsider and reissue our decision of February 8, 2000. The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen these proceedings so he may submit his updated application for adjustment of status. The motion will be denied.
 
Motions to reopen removal proceedings must be filed within 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). In the present case, this Board rendered the final decision in these proceedings on February 8, 2000. Therefore, under the strict language of the regulations, the respondent’s motion was due no later than May 8, 2000. The respondent’s motion to reopen, filed on May 9, 2005, is untimely.
 
We acknowledge that the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held that the doctrine of equitable tolling applies to the time limitations for motions to reopen or reconsider during periods when a respondent is prevented from filing his motion because of deception, fraud, or error, provided that the respondent acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003). The respondent argues that the time limitation on his motion to reopen should be tolled due to ineffective assistance of counsel before the Immigration Judge and the Board. The respondent was represented before the Immigration Judge by the law office of Antonio Bueno and Carl Dresselhaus. In addition, the respondent claims that his attorney, Antonio Bueno, filed the Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) before the Board, but did not indicate that the respondent was represented on appeal. Further, the respondent claims that the attorney indicated the wrong address for the respondent on the Notice of Appeal.
 
After the Board’s decision of February 8, 2000, the respondent hired new counsel, Mr. Alexander N. Lopez, who filed the August 10, 2000, motion to reconsider before the Board. This motion was denied on November 9, 2001. Subsequently, the respondent hired present counsel, who filed the pending motion, claiming that the respondent received ineffective assistance from his first attorney, Mr. Antonio Bueno.
 
After our dismissal of the respondent’s appeal and the denial of the motion to reconsider, the respondent was on notice that the appeal he submitted was deficient. The respondent did not raise a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against Mr. Bueno until over 5 years after the appeal was dismissed. Given that the respondent did not act with due diligence in asserting his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, we find that it is inappropriate to toll the statute of limitations under these circumstances. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra; see also Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999). Therefore, we conclude that the respondent’s motion is untimely.
 
*2 Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied.

2005 WL 3016096 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WIESLAW JANUS
File: AXX XX4 492 - Detroit
August 4, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND CERTIFICATION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Julia A. Thorne, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Victoria Christian,
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 This case was last before the Board on November 8, 2004, when the record was returned to the Immigration Judge for a new decision meeting the requirements of Matter of A-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 468 (BIA 1999). The record has now been returned to the Board with the Immigration Judge’s decision dated December 16, 2004, and the parties have been provided an opportunity to file briefs.
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s denial of a second motion seeking to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal that was entered on November 16, 2001. The respondent argues that the second motion fully complies with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and that the time and number restrictions on motions to reopen should be equitably tolled because he received ineffective assistance from his former counsel. The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that he did not meet the requirement that the accused counsel be notified of the charges against him. He points out that a certified mail receipt was provided with the second motion to establish that Mr. Owens was sent a copy of his affidavit, the complaint to the disciplinary committee, and the supporting documentation. We are persuaded that the respondent did provide notice of his complaint to Mr. Owens. That claim is buttressed by his submission with his appeal brief of an April 29, 2004, letter from the Kentucky Bar Association which states that a copy of the response to his complaint is enclosed.
 
The Sixth Circuit has not yet ruled on the issue whether ineffective assistance of counsel warrants the equitable tolling of the time and number restrictions on motions. See Scorteanu v. INS, 339 F.3d 407 (6th Cir. 2003). We are not inclined to reach the issue in this case because we will take a negative inference from the respondent’s failure to provide with his appeal brief the response that he received to his complaint against Mr. Owens. See Matter of Lozada, supra at 639 (any response from the accused counsel should be provided). He provided only the cover letter, most likely because that is favorable to his claim to have complied with the notice requirement of Lozada. The absence of the enclosed response is particularly troubling in this case because the counsel who represented the respondent before Mr. Owens withdrew as his representative citing in part the respondent’s deliberate disregard of their agreement, including paying counsel and reasonably communicating with counsel regarding his whereabouts. In light of the respondent’s history of failing to cooperate with counsel and his present failure to disclose an existing response to his complaint against Mr. Owens, we will not find that the respondent has demonstrated that he has been ineffectively assisted by him. The procedural requirements set forth in Lozada are designed to avoid the potential for abuse by making baseless allegations. The protective mechanism created by Lozada would be undermined if Lozada is read as allowing an alien to time such a motion so that the impugned counsel does not have adequate time to respond before the motion is filed, and then excuse the alien’s failure to provide to the Board a subsequently received response to the allegations. Therefore, even if equitable tolling claims based on ineffective assistance of counsel were to be recognized in this circuit, we find that he has failed to establish such a claim. The appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 3016097 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GONZALO RAMIREZ-LLAMAS
File: AXX XX2 138 - Los Angeles
August 4, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Beth S. Persky, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on March 3, 2003, when we denied the respondent’s second motion to reopen. The respondent, through counsel, now has filed another motion to reopen, which will be denied.
 
A party seeking to reopen removal proceedings must state the new facts he intends to establish, supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c), 1003.23(b)(3) (2005); INS v. Jong Ha Wang, 450 U.S. 139 (1981); Matter of Leon-Orosco and Rodriguez-Colas, 19 I&N Dec. 136 (BIA 1984; A.G. 1984); Matter of Reyes, 18 I&N Dec. 249 (BIA 1982). A motion to reopen will not be granted unless it establishes a prima facie case of statutory and discretionary eligibility for the underlying substantive relief sought. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
By way of background, the respondent is a 31-year-old Mexican national male who is married to a naturalized United States citizen. In our previous decision, we denied the motion to reopen despite the respondent’s claim that he was eligible for adjustment of status. We observed that the respondent had not voluntarily departed from the United States as required under the terms of his voluntary departure order, and he therefore was not eligible for certain forms of relief, including adjustment of status, absent exceptional circumstances pursuant to Mattter of Shaar, 21 I&N Dec. 541 (BIA 1996).
 
In the instant motion, the respondent is represented by the same attorney that represented him in connection with the previous motion to reopen filed in 2002. Counsel now claims that the respondent’s case should be reopened based on the alleged mistakes and misconduct of the attorney that he hired in 1998 to represent him at his immigration hearing. Counsel alleges that the respondent has satisfied the criteria to establish ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) by filing a complaint with the state bar association for the previous attorney.
 
Upon review, the motion will be denied. The respondent has failed to meet the standards for reopening. In this regard, we note that the respondent has waited until 2005 to allege ineffective assistance of counsel against an attorney who had represented him in 1998 and 1999, and he has waited more than 2 years after our previous decision to raise this issue. In addition, the respondent’s present counsel represented him in connection with his previous motion to reopen and presumably was familiar with the facts of his case. Therefore, present counsel could have raised an ineffective assistance argument in the motion to reopen filed in 2002. Finally, the respondent has not established his prima facie eligibility for the requested relief of adjustment of status in order to merit reopening. As we indicated in our previous decision, he is not eligible for adjustment of status because he failed to comply with his voluntary departure order. Matter of Shaar, supra. The motion will be denied.
 
*2 Therefore, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent is ordered removed from the United States.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 3016118 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE E. RAUDA
File: AXX XX0 369 - San Francisco
August 3, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joye L. Wiley, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James Gildea
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 241(a)(2), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)º - Entered without inspection
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; relief under the NACARA
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s December 17, 2004, decision denying his motion to reopen these deportation proceedings in order to apply for relief under section 203 of the Nicaraguan and Central American Relief Act of 1997, Pub. L. No. 105-100, tit. II, 111 Stat. 2193, amended by, Pub. L. No. 105-139, 111 Stat. 2644 (“NACARA”). The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied, but the appeal will be sustained, these proceedings will be reopened, and the record will be remanded for further proceedings consistent with this decision, and for the entry of a new decision.
 
We find that reopening these proceedings for the respondent to pursue his application for suspension under the NACARA is appropriate under the circumstances of this case. The respondent’s motion to reopen for relief under the NACARA was untimely. See Respondent’s Brief in Support of Appeal of Immigration Judge’s Denial of Motion to Reopen, dated March 17, 2005, at 8-10. However, subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen and his finding in that decision that the NACARA motion to reopen deadline is not subject to equitable tolling, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this matter arises, held that the time limit for filing a motion to reopen under the NACARA is a statute of limitations subject to equitable tolling, rather than a jurisdictional time limit. See Albillo de Leon v. Gonzales, 2005 WL 1345565 (9th Cir. June 8, 2005); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.43; see also Respondent’s Brief in Support of Appeal of Immigration Judge’s Denial of Motion to Reopen, dated March 17; 2005, at 5-8. In Albillo de Leon, the Ninth Circuit found that the alien had failed to file his motion to reopen in a timely manner due to the ineffective assistance of “counsel” by a non-attorney, and that because the alien exercised due diligence in pursuing the matter, equitable tolling was appropriate. Similarly, we find in this matter that the respondent failed to timely file his motion to reopen for relief under the NACARA due to the ineffective assistance of a non-attorney, and in light of the respondent’s due diligence in pursuing the issue, we find that equitable tolling is also appropriate in this case.¿FN1º See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, dated November 4, 2004, and attachments A-Q; see also Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785 (9th Cir. 2005).
 
*2 We further find that the respondent appears prima facie eligible for suspension of deportation under the NACARA. See Respondent’s Brief in Support of Appeal of Immigration Judge’s Denial of Motion to Reopen, dated March 17, 2005, at 10-11; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.61(a)(2), 1240.65; cf. INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988) (BIA may deny motion to reopen because movant has not established prima facie case for underlying substantive relief). Moreover, the respondent does not appear to be barred by “any other provisions of law.” 8 C.F.R. § 1240.65(a); former section 242(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252(b) (1989). Specifically, the respondent is not barred from reopening his in absentia deportation proceedings by former section 242(b) of the Act because he has demonstrated “reasonable cause” for his failure to appear for his hearing. See Brief in Support of Appeal of Immigration Judge’s Denial of Motion to Reopen dated March 17, 2005, at 12-14; Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, dated November 4, 2004, and attachments.
 
The respondent’s charging document states that the respondent’s hearing was “TO BE SET AT A LATER DATE AND TIME” (Exh. 1). An apparent duplicate of the subsequently issued Notice of Master Calendar Hearing, dated September 11, 1989, for the respondent’s November 20, 1989, hearing is present in the record and bears the same address for the respondent as does the charging document; however, there is no proof of certified or registered mailing of the Notice of Hearing, the respondent claims to have never received this notice, and it is unclear if, how, and when this notice was mailed to the respondent (Exh. 3). Accordingly, we find that the respondent has demonstrated “reasonable cause” for his failure to appear for his hearing, and he is, therefore, not barred from reopening his in absentia deportation proceedings by former section 242(b) of the Act. Cf. Matter of Huete, 20 I&N Dec. 250 (BIA 1991).
 
Hence, the appeal is sustained, these deportation proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded for the adjudication of the respondent’s suspension application under NACARA and any other application for relief for which the respondent is qualified, and for the entry of a new decision.

FOR THE BOARD

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
    1  Reversed and Remanded by Martinez-Farias v. Holder, 9th Cir., July 28, 2009
2005 WL 3016106 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARTINEZ-FARIAS, FRANCISCO
File: AXX-XX7-010 - Los Angeles
August 2, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ceballos, Gustavo, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen and reconsider our decision dated March 25, 2005. The motion argues that the respondent is not a public charge and therefore was erroneously found ineligible for adjustment of status. However, the respondent, confined to a wheelchair as the result of a stroke he suffered while in jail, admits that he has been receiving public benefits since at least 1993. As we noted in our prior decision, there is no evidence that family members have contributed any financial assistance to the respondent prior to the hearing. See Matter of Kohama, 17 I&N Dec. 257 (A.C. 1978)(granting a motion to reopen in part because the respondents submitted evidence that their children had provided financial assistance in the past).
 
The respondent also argues that public charge was never raised before the final hearing, so he was not prepared to address the issue. However, the record reflects that the Immigration Judge raised the issue at a hearing on March 24, 1999, while the respondent was represented by counsel. At the time of the final hearing, the case had been pending for nearly 10 years for attorney preparation and for the respondent to hire a succession of counsel. The respondent’s counsel were afforded ample time to prepare, and the motion does not argue ineffective assistance of counsel. There was no due process violation.
 
The motion attempts to introduce purportedly new evidence in the form of affidavits from the respondent and his son, as well as documentation from the Social Security Administration verifying the receipt of social security benefits. However, the motion does not adequately explain why this information was not introduced at the hearing before the Immigration Judge. The motion does not cite to any errors of fact or law in the prior decision that would warrant reconsideration, nor does it state new material facts that were not available, or could not have been discovered or presented, at the prior hearing. 8 C.F.R. §1003.2(b) and (c). Accordingly, the motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 3016132 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALI HUSSEIN HASSAN
File: AXX XX7 734 - Detroit
August 1, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Wilton A. Hom, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael B. Dobson,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge‘s decision dated July 13, 2004. On appeal, the respondent alleges he has received ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
In his brief, the respondent alleges that the respondent has advised his former attorney of his complaint and has filed a complaint with the “appropriate disciplinary authorities.” However, the respondent has failed to submit evidence of this.
 
The respondent has failed to fulfil the prerequisites for allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent has failed to attach to the appeal a sworn affidavit, signed by the respondent, setting out the alleged facts necessary to show a prima facie claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
This affidavit should have included the factual allegations underlying the claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.¿FN1º The sworn affidavit should have also included the contractual agreement with the former counsel. The sworn affidavit should have also indicated that the respondent has notified the former counsel of the allegation of ineffective assistance, enabling the former counsel to respond to the allegations. In addition, the sworn affidavit should have indicated that a complaint has been filed against the former counsel with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. See Matter of Grijalva, supra; Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

Because the factual statements in the brief are not sworn to be true, they are not evidence. Motions or briefs, containing legal argument, must be accompanied by a sworn statement of a person having personal knowledge of the facts alleged. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Cf. Matter of G-, 20 I&N Dec. 764 (BIA 1993); Matter of Laureano, 19 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 1983).

2005 WL 3016129 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WADE ANTHONY DRUMMOND
File: AXX XX7 407 - Batavia
August 1, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mark P. Murphy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) º - Controlled substance violation
Sec. 212(a)(9)(g)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(9)(A)(ii)º - Aliens previously removed
 
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal; waiver of inadmissibility; adjustment of status
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s March 2, 2005, decision finding the respondent removable as charged and ineligible for relief from removal. The appeal will be dismissed.¿FN1º
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident on May 9, 1984. The record reveals that on April 22, 1988, the respondent pleaded guilty to and was convicted of the Texas offense of unlawfull possession of a controlled substance, cocaine (Exh. 2 at Attachment).¿FN2º On December 2, 1992, the respondent pleaded guilty to and was convicted of the New York offense of criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree (Exh. 2 at Attachment). Thereafter, the Department of Homeland Security, (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service), commenced removal proceedings against the respondent charging him with removability under sections 237(a)(2)(B), and (C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B), (C) (Exh. 2 at Attachment). An Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed from the United States to Jamaica on May 21, 1998 (Exh. 2 at Attachment).¿FN3º The respondent was removed from this country on September 24, 1998 (Exh. 2 at Attachment).
 
On December 11, 1998, the respondent attempted to re-enter this country, without advance consent from the Attorney General, by presenting a revoked resident alien card (Exh. 2 at Attachment). The respondent was subsequently convicted of attempting to re-enter the United States after deportation (Exh. 2 at Attachment). Thereafter, the DHS commenced removal proceedings against the respondent alleging that the respondent was removable as one inadmissible at entry both as an alien convicted of a controlled substance violation as described in section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), and as an alien previously removed as described in section 212(a)(9)(A)(ii) of the Act.
 
The respondent on appeal challenges his removability and asserts, in part, that the first order of removal was unlawful and unfair because he was not considered for relief for which he was eligible. The respondent’s arguments are without merit.
 
*2 As an initial matter, the record reveals that the DHS presented clear and convincing evidence establishing the respondent’s removability as charged in both proceedings (Exhs. 2, 3). Turning to the issue of relief from removal, the respondent has failed to establish eligibility for both cancellation of removal and a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Act. With respect to cancellation of removal, as the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent has not demonstrated the requisite 7 years of residence. See section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). Contrary to the respondent’s arguments on appeal, the respondent’s 1988 controlled substance conviction ended the respondent’s continuous residence for cancellation purposes. See Matter of Perez, 22 I&N Dec. 689 (BIA 1999); See also Matter of K-S-, 20 I&N Dec. 715 (BIA 1993) (explaining that the Board is not bound to follow the published decision of a United States district court even in cases arising within the same district).
 
With respect to section 212(c) relief, although it appears that the respondent may have been statutorily eligible for a waiver of inadmissibility for his 1988 controlled substance conviction before his earlier removal, such relief has not been shown to have been available for the respondent’s 1992 firearms conviction. See Matter of Montenegro, 20 I&N Dec. 603 (BIA 1992). While the firearms conviction did not constitute a ground of inadmissibility that would preclude the respondent from seeking adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act, the record contains no evidence establishing the respondent’s prima facie eligibility to pursue adjustment of status in conjunction with a 212(c) waiver for the 1988 drug conviction. See Matter of Gabryelsky, 20 I&N Dec. 750 (BIA 1993). Furthermore, as the Immigration Judge noted, the record contains evidence indicating that the respondent may have a recent (1998) aggravated felony conviction, which would not qualify for a section 212(c) waiver (Exh. 2 at Attachment). In addition, we believe it would be inconsistent with the purpose of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44(k) to allow the respondent to collaterally attack his prior removal in circumstances where the regulation would bar reopening of that same proceeding
 
On whole, the record supports the Immigration Judge’s resolution. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

2

Notably, even if the respondent’s conviction had been set aside, under the circumstances, the respondent’s conviction would still count as a conviction for immigration purposes. See generally Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N 621, 623-24 (BIA 2003) (explaining that a respondent remains “convicted” for immigration purposes where the conviction is vacated for reasons unrelated to the merits of the underlying criminal proceedings).

Furthermore, to the extent the respondent claims ineffective assistance of counsel in relation to that appeal, he has not sufficiently shown patent error or complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).

2005 WL 1848384 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ABDULAH AHMED BALAGHI A.K.A. ABDULAH AHMED BALAEH
File: AXX XX4 265 - New York
May 11, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael P. DiRaimondo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Susan Egan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This matter was last before us on January 24, 2005, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen his removal proceedings after he was removed in absentia. On March 30, 2005, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Board, alleging ineffective assistance of his prior counsel. The respondent maintains that his prior counsel failed to appear at his hearing to inform the Immigration Judge of his illness and failed to timely file his motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge. The respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act provides that an in absentia order “may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances.” In Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998), the Board held that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the 180-day statutory limit for the filing of a motion to reopen to rescind an in absentia order of deportation. In this matter, we find that the respondent’s motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel is untimely, inasmuch as it was not filed until March 30, 2005, nearly a year and a half after he was ordered in absentia on October 17, 2003. Furthermore, even if we applied the principle of equitable tolling, the respondent’s motion to reopen, which was filed on March 30, 2005, would be untimely, since the respondent learned of his prior counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance, when his motion to reopen was denied by the Immigration Judge, as untimely, on August 11, 2004. See Iavorski v. USINS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000) (holding that “¿fºor an untimely claim to receive the benefit of equitable tolling … an alien must demonstrate not only that the alien’s constitutional right to due process has been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll”). Moreover, we decline to reopen the respondent’s removal proceedings pursuant to our sua sponte authority, due to the respondent’s history of failing to appear for his scheduled hearings and the fact that this is the respondent’s third motion to reopen proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 1766769 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANDRE PAUL NEWTON
File: AXX XX4 839 - Atlanta
May 4, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
*1 In an order entered on October 20, 1999, we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent reportedly was removed from the United States on October 4, 2002. On November 29, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen shall not be made by a person who is the subject of removal proceedings subsequent to his departure from the United States. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d).
 
We summarily dismissed the appeal because the respondent, then represented by counsel, failed to file a separate brief or statement after indicating the intention to do so. The respondent argues that he was deprived, due to ineffective assistance of counsel, of the opportunity to apply for a waiver of inadmissibility pursuant to the former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c). It is argued that the prior proceedings were fundamentally unfair, and that the respondent should consequently be allowed to apply for section 212(c) relief. The respondent cites two cases decided by the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit in support of his argument, United States v. Fernandez-Antonia, 278 F.3d 150 (2nd Cir. 2002); and United States v. Perez, 330 F.3d 97 (2nd Cir. 2003).
 
We note that the respondent remained in the United States for 3 years after our order was issued, until he was removed, and there is no indication that he attempted to reopen proceedings during that time on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent’s motion has been filed within the Second Circuit’s jurisdiction, since he is detained in New York and is reportedly in criminal proceedings related to his reentry after being removed. However, neither of the Second Circuit cases cited by the respondent compels, in our view, the reopening of proceedings. The respondent’s firearms conviction rendered him ineligible for section 212(c) relief. See, e.g., Matter of Saint John, 21 I&N Dec. 593 (BIA 1996); Matter of Hernandez-Casillas, 20 I&N Dec. 262 (BIA 1990, A.G. 1991). The respondent has not shown that he has a qualifying relative through whom he could adjust his status, so he cannot rely on our holding in Matter of Gabryelsky, 20 I&N Dec. 750 (BIA 1993), to garner section 212(c) relief. The respondent failed to exercise due diligence when he did not challenge the effectiveness of his counsel in the 3 years between the issuance of our order and his removal. Further, the respondent has failed to demonstrate any significant degree of compliance with our holding in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1 st Cir. 1988).
 
*2 Consequently, the respondent has not been deprived of pursuing section 212(c) relief, due to his ineligibility therefor. The respondent has not demonstrated that the prior proceedings were unfair; it is the pretermission of consideration for section 212(c) relief that is specifically challenged, and not the charges of removability. It has not been demonstrated how prior counsel’s assistance prevented the respondent’s applying for a relief for which he was not, and is not, eligible.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

REISSUED DECISION
 
*1 We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on November 14, 2002. On March 23, 2005, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). We will reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), and reissue our previous decision.
 
The respondent alleges that his former attorney failed to inform him about our prior decision, so he was unable to file a petition for review with the Third Circuit Court of Appeals.
 
We find that the respondent has met the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA1988). According to our records, our November 14, 2002, decision was returned as undeliverable, and the respondent presents evidence that former counsel has had an office at numerous addresses. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion, and we will treat the motion as unopposed. The Board’s decision will be reissued and treated as if entered on today’s date. See Firmansjah v. Ashcroft, 347 F.3d 625 (7th Cir. 2003) (Board has authority to reissue decision).
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reissue is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decision dated November 14, 2002, attached hereto, is hereby reissued and shall be treated as entered as of today’s date.

2005 WL 1396838 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: STEPHEN NJUGUNA KAGUNYE
File: AXX XX7 223 - Louisville
April 29, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kathleen M. Gillespie, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On February 23, 2005, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s motion to reopen as untimely filed. The respondent has filed a motion to reconsider. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
An Immigration Judge issued a final order of removal against the respondent on February 3, 2004. The respondent was found to be ineligible for adjustment of status. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen on April 8, 2004, finding that the respondent was ineligible for adjustment of status, “due to his false claim to U.S. citizenship.” An appeal was filed on December 22, 2004, which we dismissed as untimely on February 23, 2005.
 
The respondent now claims that he was not attempting to appeal the Immigration Judge’s April 8, 2004, decision but was trying to file a motion to reopen with the Board based on ineffective assistance of counsel (Motion to Reconsider, at 3). The Board lacks jurisdiction over a motion to reopen filed in this case. We generally do not have the authority to reopen a case that has never been properly before us. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974). As the respondent did not file an appeal from the Immigration Judge’s February 3, 2004, decision, the Board lacks jurisdiction over any motion to reopen filed in the case.
 
To the extent that the respondent still argues that he was filing an appeal on December 22, 2004, over which we would have jurisdiction if timely filed, we will not reconsider our decision that the appeal was untimely and no good excuse for such was provided. The respondent learned that his attorney had failed to submit an appeal on July 26, 2004. There is, as we stated in our previous decision, no reason why it took the respondent until December 22, 2004, to file an appeal. We decline to consider this appeal under our sua sponte authority. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The motion to reconsider is, therefore, denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 1396796 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BO TONG
File: AXX XX0 200 - Los Angeles
April 22, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ming Gang Li, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. An Immigration Judge issued an order of removal against the respondent on February 10, 2004. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of the Immigration Judge’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38. The appeal was filed untimely on June 7, 2004. In conjunction with the appeal, the respondent, through counsel, requests that the Board accept the late appeal. The request, to which the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded, will be denied, and the appeal will be dismissed as untimely.
 
The appeal was due on March 11, 2004. Counsel for the respondent argues that she believed that she was supposed to file the appeal with the immigration court rather than the Board. However, counsel refers to long-expired regulations to support this contention. Additionally, the respondent’s counsel’s letter suggests that she was already specifically on notice, via notice from another immigration court, that the appeal had to be filed with the Board. In any event, there is no proof submitted that the respondent actually tried to submit the appeal to the immigration court, as counsel claims.
 
Moreover, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). As for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, these cases require that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. The respondent has complied with none of these requirements.
 
We find that the appeal is untimely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.52, 1240.53, and 1241.31 (1999).

IN RE: ORITSERENEYE HENRY WILKINSON-OKOTIE
File: AXX XX0 395 - Houston
