IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Linda L. Walker, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Genie Zhang
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on March 29, 2005. This is the second motion to reopen that has been filed with the Board in this case. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion. The motion is untimely, and barred by numerical limitations for motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
Underlying the motion is a claim of ineffective assistance of the attorney who represented the respondent at the time we issued our last decision on May 18, 2004. However, the respondent is ineligible for the relief sought, adjustment of status, based on the respondent’s failure to depart under an order of voluntary departure, as the DHS argues. Pursuant to section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d), an alien who fails to depart following a grant of voluntary departure is statutorily barred from applying for certain forms of discretionary relief. In this case, our November 8, 2002, final order gave the respondent a period of 30 days to voluntarily depart the United States. The respondent was warned that a failure to do so would result in his being ineligible for adjustment of status. At the time we issued the November 8, 2002, decision, the respondent was not represented by counsel about whom he complains, and the respondent makes no claim that he was not promptly made aware of that order. As the respondent failed to comply with the order granting voluntary departure, he is ineligible for adjustment of status. He therefore fails to show prejudice resulting from the claimed ineffective assistance of counsel. De Zavala v. Ashcroft, 385 F.3d 879, 884 (5th Cir. 2004). The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARLOS OROZCO GUTIERREZ MARIA MARISOL OROZCO
File: AXX XX2 034 - San Francisco
AXX XX4 591
April 21, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION

Adria-Ann McMurray
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Larry Gallagher
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This matter was last before the Board on September 8, 2004, at which time we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondents’ applications for cancellation of removal and granting the alternative relief of voluntary departure. The respondents have filed a motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not filed an opposition. The motion will be granted as set forth below.
 
The respondents seek reopening so that they may file a brief on the merits of their appeal. They assert that, unbeknownst to them, their prior counsel failed to file a brief. Specifically, they assert that they hired their prior counsel, David A. Diaz, to file an appeal and to file applications for work permits, and that Mr. Diaz “was supposed to file a written legal brief” on appeal (Motion at Exh. E). They diligently tried to contact their counsel on a regular basis to learn the status of their proceedings. After the male respondent’s employer threatened to fire him unless he got a new work permit, the respondents hired their present counsel. Through present counsel, the respondents learned that Mr. Diaz never filed a brief and that he had been placed on inactive status with the State Bar of California. A few weeks thereafter, the male respondent confronted Mr. Diaz, and Mr. Diaz acknowledged his wrongdoing, apologized, and promised to write a letter to the Board. The respondents promptly filed a motion to late-file a brief; however, the motion was received by the Board after we issued our decision. Because Mr. Diaz was counsel of record at the time the Board’s decision was issued, the decision was mailed to him. He did not forward it to the respondents. As a result, they did not learn of the Board’s decision until after the voluntary departure period had expired.
 
The respondents have sufficiently complied with the requirements outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), including having filed a complaint with the State Bar of California. To warrant reopening based on the ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondents must also demonstrate that they were prejudiced by counsel’s failure to file a brief, Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003). We conclude that the male respondent is unable to demonstrate prejudice with respect to cancellation of removal because, as a matter of law, he cannot demonstrate the requisite period of continuous physical presence due to his departures in 1998 and 1999 under threat of deportation. Matter of Romalez, 23 I&N Dec. 423 (BIA 2002). However, we conclude that the male respondent was prejudiced by counsel’s deficient performance to the extent that he was unaware of the Board’s decision and, specifically, the voluntary departure order. Under these exceptional circumstances, we will grant the motion to reopen proceedings in AXX XXX XXX to the extent that our September 8, 2004, order will be reissued in that case. With respect to the female respondent, out of an abundance of caution we will grant the motion to reopen to permit her to file a brief on the merits in AXX XXX XXX. See Singh v. Ashcroft, 362 F.3d 1164 (9th Cir. 2004). A transcript will be issued to the respondent, and a briefing schedule will be set by separate order.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen proceedings in AXX XXX XXX is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen proceedings in AXX XXX XXX is granted to the extent that the Board’s September 8, 2004, decision is hereby reissued in AXX XXX XXX.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent in AXX XXX XXX is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent in AXX XXX XXX fails to so depart, the respondent in AXX XXX XXX shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent in AXX XXX XXX fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent in AXX XXX XXX shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2005 WL 1396807 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ADAMA DIANE
File: AXX XX0 474 - New York
April 19, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF APPLICANT:
 
Jan Allen Reiner, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Chartrisse Adlam
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Board on May 12, 2004. We have jurisdiction over this matter pursuant to 8 CFR § 1003.2(a). The motion will be granted and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
The record reflects that the respondent failed to appear for the noticed hearing on June 11, 1999, and the respondent was ordered deported in absentia. The respondent’s motion to reopen the deportation proceedings was denied by the Immigration Judge on August 26, 1999. When this case was initially before the Board on November 13, 2001, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the denial of the motion to reopen. Through a new attorney, the respondent on May 12, 2004, filed a motion to reopen his deportation proceedings to rescind the in absentia order of deportation against him to pursue relief from deportation. The respondent contends that his prior counsel’s ineffective assistance of counsel amounts to “exceptional circumstances” for purposes of rescinding an in absentia order of deportation.
 
We note that the respondent’s motion is both time and numerically barred from consideration. See Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998); Matter of A-A, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998) (holding that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception of the 180-day statutory limit for the filing of a motion to reopen to rescind an in absentia order of deportation under section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252 b(c)(3)(A) on the basis of exceptional circumstances). However, in Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000), the court recognized the doctrine of “equitable tolling” of the statute of limitations regarding the filing of a motion to reopen. The court held that an alien must demonstrate not only that the alien’s constitutional right to due process has been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll. Id. In that case, the court concluded that the alien failed to demonstrate such diligence, and concluded the equitable tolling did not apply. Id.
 
Accordingly, under Iavorski v. INS, supra, the Board must first address whether the respondent was denied due process in these proceedings based upon the ineffective assistance of his former counsel. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved applicant setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representation counsel did or did not make to the applicant in this regard; (2) the counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id at 640.
 
*2 In the instant case, we have reviewed the documents submitted with the instant motion and find that the respondent may have been the victim of negligence. We note that the record contains a statement of Mr. Kleefield in reply to the respondent’s complaint, wherein Mr. Kleefield indicates that he is old and trying his best. It also appears that counsel did little to keep the respondent informed of the status of his hearing. We therefore conclude that the respondent did not fail to exercise due diligence in seeking reopening, and we find that reopening is appropriate. We also note that the respondent appears eligible to reopen under Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). He appears prima facie eligible to adjust his status, and since the motion is joint, there are no time restrictions. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be granted.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.

*1 This case was last before us on October 31, 2002, when we affirmed, without opinion, the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s applications for relief and ordering his removal to China. The respondent filed the instant motion on January 21, 2005. It is untimely whether considered a motion to reconsider or a motion to reopen. See sections 240(c)(5)(B), (6)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § § 1229a(c)(5)(B), (6)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b), (c) (providing 30 days to file a motion to reconsider and 90 days to file a motion to reopen). See also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
To the extent that the respondent is arguing that the untimeliness is due to ineffective assistance of counsel, he has failed to comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). He has also not articulated an adequate basis for tolling the statutory and regulatory deadlines for motions under Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 130 (2d Cir. 2000) (finding deadline for motions to reopen deportation proceedings may be equitably tolled, but alien failed to exercise due diligence required for equitable tolling). Even if his prior counsel was ineffective in failing to timely advise him of our dismissal of his appeal, the respondent learned in August 2003 that his appeal had been dismissed, and he failed to exercise due diligence because he delayed filing a motion until January 2005. Accordingly, the motion is denied as untimely.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JORGE A. GALINDO-RODRIGUEZ MARIA VICTORIA GALINDO DIANA GALINDO
Files: AXX XX6 418 - Los Angeles
AXX XX6 419
AXX XX6 420
April 19, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Karla L. Kraus, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Randy A. Kung
Assistant District Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Suspension of deportation
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case is presently before us pursuant to the July 23, 2004, decision of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. The court upheld the Board’s ruling on the respondents’ ineffective assistance of counsel claim. However, it found that the Board erred in not addressing the respondents’ “newly available evidence pertaining to extreme hardship.” The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
The respondents are a father, mother, and daughter. They are now 49, 52, and 19 years old, respectively. The Immigration Judge found that the respondents did not satisfy the continuous physical presence requirement because he did not find the evidence that they presented of residence in this country prior to 1988 sufficient. We question whether the Immigration Judge’s finding in this regard can stand in view of Ninth Circuit’s recent decision in Vera-Villegas v. INS, 330 F.3d 1222 (9th Cir. 2003), and the male respondent’s consistent testimony that he came to the United States in 1986, and his wife and daughter joined him a few months later.
 
However, we need not reach this issue because the Orders to Show Cause were issued against all the respondents in this case on August 2, 1996. Even if the entry dates given on the Orders to Show Cause, February 24, 1988, are the correct dates of initial entry into this country, the respondents would still have the 7 years continuous physical presence required for suspension of deportation.
 
We now turn to the issue of extreme hardship. As noted by the court, the respondents previously submitted to the Board additional evidence regarding extreme hardship, which they wanted the Board to consider. The Immigration Judge should consider this evidence in the first instance, pursuant to the court’s decision. We do note that all of the respondents have now lived in the United States for at least 17 years, and the 19-year-old has lived here for virtually her entire life. Such long residence is an important factor to consider in assessing hardship.
 
Accordingly, the decisions of the Board dated July 18, 2001, and September 24, 2002, are vacated, the deportation proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision and the decision of the court.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE L. RUIZ FLORES GLORIA ROJAS-CHAVEZ ANDREA CELESTE RUIZ-ROJAS
Files: AXX XX8 837 - Los Angeles
AXX XX7 655
AXX XX7 654
April 14, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Arik Ben-Ezra, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (all respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Reopen
 
This case was last before us on May 9, 2002, when we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s August 24, 1999, denial of the respondents’ applications for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). The respondents now move to reopen their case arguing that their prior attorneys provided ineffective assistance of counsel and that they are eligible for cancellation of removal.
 
We have previously outlined the threshold requirements for having an ineffective assistance of counsel claim considered. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592 (9th Cir. 2004). In addition to the threshold requirements, an alien must demonstrate that he or she suffered prejudice as a result of the prior counsel’s actions. We initially observe that the non-citizen daughter, Andrea (AXX XXX XXX), does not have an effective Lozada claim. She argues that her prior attorneys did not attempt to establish that she was independently eligible for cancellation of removal. We observe, however, that the Act clearly requires that an alien must have a qualifying relative to be eligible for relief. See section 240A(b)(1)(D). There is no qualifying relative upon whom the non-citizen daughter can base her hardship requirement. Thus, we do not find that the non-citizen daughter established prejudice.
 
Similarly, we do not find that the lead respondent or his wife established prejudice. The Immigration Judge below found that the lead respondent and his wife met the physical presence and good moral character requirements. Thus, the only remaining requirement was whether the lead respondent and his wife demonstrated that removal would result in exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to their qualifying relatives. See section 240A(b)(1). To be eligible for cancellation of removal, hardship must be substantially beyond that which ordinarily would be expected to result from the alien’s deportation. See Matter of Monreal, 23 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2001). Even considering all of the new arguments included in the motion to reopen, we do not find that the respondent and his wife have demonstrated hardship factors rising to the level of hardship articulated in Matter of Recinas, 23 I&N Dec. 467, 470 (commenting that the hardship circumstances present in the case constitute the outer limit of the narrow spectrum of cases in which the exceptional and extremely unusual hardship standard will be met). Unlike in Recinas, the burden of supporting a family does not fall upon one individual. Furthermore, the lead respondent and his wife have family in Mexico who they could stay with and have fewer children than the single mother in Recinas. We therefore conclude that the lead respondent and his wife cannot establish their eligibility for cancellation of removal and have failed to demonstrate that they were prejudiced by their prior attorneys’ actions. See Matter of Lozada, supra
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is dismissed.

2005 WL 952455 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FERNANDO MENDIOLA SANCHEZ
File: A27 553 533 - San Francisco
April 14, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Helen A. Sklar, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Evan R. Franke
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We sustained an appeal of the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service), and found the respondent and his son, Mario Mendiola Araujo, A75 260 1811, ineligible for suspension of deportation, on July 18, 2002. The respondent then filed a motion to reopen seeking adjustment of status. We denied this motion to reopen as untimely on June 4, 2004. More than 6 months later, and represented by the same law firm, the respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen on December 16, 2004. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Moreover, the motion exceeds the numerical limitations for motions to reopen. Id. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent argues that the deadline for filing the motion to reopen should be tolled. The Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals has found that the motion to reopen period may be tolled due to “deception, fraud, or error.” Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1185-86 (9th Cir. 2004). As the DHS argues, any “error” here amounts to negligence on the part of counsel who still represents the respondent. The respondent’s attorney, Helen A. Sklar, states that she was unaware that the motions filing deadline would result in our June 4, 2004, decision which denied the respondent’s previous motion to reopen as untimely. Yet even after that decision, which was consistent with the pertinent regulations, and clearly said that the motion was dismissed as untimely, counsel made no effort to submit this motion claiming “ineffective assistance of counsel” until after the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals had ruled on our original decision in this case in the summer of 2004.
 
In any event, the respondent fails to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556, 561-62 (BIA 2003). As for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, those cases require that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226-27 (9th Cir. 2002); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525-526 (9th Cir. 2000)(Lozada requirements generally reasonable, but full compliance is not required if the record independently shows that the “ineffective assistance” claim is legitimate and substantial). The respondent here declines to make a complaint against counsel. The Board in Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 603-605 (BIA 1996), gave several reasons for the “bar complaint” requirement. We noted that the requirement protects against collusion between aliens and counsel in which “ineffective assistance” is used to achieve delay. Id. We found that the bar complaint, like the other filings required by Matter of Lozada, supra, greatly aids us in adjudicating the claim. Id. We also stated that the filing of such a complaint is a “relatively small inconvenience” for an alien seeking a new hearing after proceedings have been concluded at great government expense. Id. The disciplinary process could consider, and evaluate, the actions taken by former counsel. Matter of Rivera-Claros, supra; see also Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 596 (9th Cir. 2004) (“¿rºequiring sworn affidavits and formal grievance letters reduces petitioners’ ability to slip meritless claims past the Board”); compare Castillo-Perez v. INS, supra, at fn 11 (alien not required to submit a bar complaint in case where attorney had been subject to numerous counts of violations of professional ethics and had been punished by the Supreme Court of California).
 
*2 The pending motion is, therefore, denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS

Sam Shihab, Esquire
 
REISSUED DECISION
 
*1 We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on April 8, 2003. On January 25, 2005, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). We will reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), and reissue our previous decision.
 
The respondent alleges that his former attorney failed to inform him of our earlier decision, so that he could file a petition for review with the Third Circuit Court of Appeals, or depart voluntarily under the terms of our prior order. Contrary to the argument of the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service), we find that the respondent has met the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I & N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Former counsel submitted a disciplinary complaint against himself with the Texas state bar, in which former counsel admits that he and the respondent had a “communication breakdown” after our April 8, 2003, decision. Specifically, former counsel lost his cell phone — which was listed as his contact number — after we issued our previous decision, and an unknown person reactivated the service so that the respondent was able to leave messages which never reached former counsel. Former counsel admits that the respondent “thought he was leaving me messages”, which in fact were never received. The respondent in his affidavit says that he tried to keep in contact with former counsel monthly via the cell phone number, and provided updated contact information via that number. The respondent states also in his affidavit that he later tried to contact former counsel at a new telephone number he received from another attorney in early 2004, but former counsel did not return his call. The Board’s decision will be reissued and treated as if entered on today’s date. See Firmansjah v. Ashcroft, 347 F.3d 625 (7th Cir. 2003)(Board has authority to reissue decision).
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reissue is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decision dated April 8, 2003, attached hereto, is hereby reissued and shall be treated as entered as of today’s date.

2005 WL 952454 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YUBAI GE
File: A95 451 367 - Los Angeles
April 14, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Walter Burrier, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reissuance of Board decision
 
This case was last before this Board on October 7, 2004, when we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal as the statement in the Notice of Appeal failed to meaningfully apprise this Board of the reason underlying the appeal. The Notice of Appeal indicated only: “Reasons to be submitted in brief form later.” The respondent now moves this Board to reissue that decision due to alleged ineffective assistance of former counsel. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent asserts ineffective assistance of former counsel in timely notifying him of this Board’s October 7, 2004, decision. The respondent asks this Board to reissue our prior order so that he may petition for review of that decision in federal court. With his motion the respondent submits a statement, not an affidavit, that he met with Ana Zhang from Zhongmei Immigration Service and paid her to assist in his appeal. The motion contains a copy of a business card with Ms. Zhang’s name and the name and address of Bayley and Associates. The respondent also presents a copy of an “Immigration Fee Agreement” between the law offices of Guy R. Bayley and the respondent. The agreement specifically states the services to be rendered: “Asylum case handled through any notice of appeal to BIA, excluding any briefing to BIA.” The respondent states that he never met with Mr. Bayley. A timely Notice of Appeal and Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney was filed by Mr. Bayley before this Board on September 10, 2003. The respondent heard that a decision on his appeal could take over a year. The respondent went to check the status of his appeal with Ms. Zhang and learned that the office had closed and was occupied by another business. The respondent had assistance in contacting this Board by phone and found out on November 29, 2004, that a decision had been issued on October 7, 2004. The motion also contains a copy of an unaddressed letter from the respondent’s present counsel to Mr. Bayley, informing him that he would be contacting the State Bar of California and filing the instant motion before the Board. The respondent’s present counsel has also sent a complaint letter to the State Bar of California.
 
Even if we were to find that the evidence submitted with the motion to reopen substantially comports with the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), we do not find good cause to reissue our prior order. This Board has on occasion reissued its decisions, but only due to Board error or administrative problems involving receipt of the Board’s decision. See, e.g., Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968 (9th Cir. 2004). In the instant case the respondent does not allege any error on the part of the Board in serving its decision to former counsel, and the record does not reflect that our decision was returned or undeliverable to the address of former counsel, which is the same address noted by the respondent in his motion. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(f); 1292.4(a), 1292.5(a) (2004).1 We, therefore, do not find good cause to reissue our prior order.
 
*2 In addition, the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by the claimed ineffective assistance of former counsel in pursuing his appeal before this Board. Although the respondent was unable to file a timely petition for review before the federal court of this Board’s decision, the respondent has not shown that this Board erred in summarily dismissing his appeal. The respondent has never filed a brief or alleged any error of law or fact in the decision of the Immigration Judge. The respondent’s motion to reissue our decision does not also contain a motion to reopen in order to consider the merits of his appeal due to alleged ineffective assistance of his prior attorney. See generally Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592 (9th Cir. 2004); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003). Thus, we cannot find that there was any prejudice to the respondent in the purported ineffective assistance of his former counsel. Accordingly, the motion to reissue our prior decision is denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reissue our decision is denied.

We note that the record also includes an envelope with a return address for former counsel at 865 S. Figueroa St., Suite 1388, Los Angeles, California 90017.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EVA RAMOS BONILLA
File: A74 892 858 - New York
April 12, 2005
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Patrice Perillie, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Evalyn Douchy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent’s motion to reopen has been filed years out of time and will be denied. An Immigration Judge denied the respondent suspension of deportation1 on March 1, 1999. On March 29, 1999, present counsel Patrice Perillie, then associated with Wilens & Baker law firm, submitted a Notice of Appeal to the Board. On March 30, 1999, we rejected the appeal for filing defects, because the check submitted by the respondent was not signed. The March 30, 1999, notice was sent to Perillie. Almost 4 months later, on July 26, 1999, another attorney at Wilens & Baker sought to submit the appeal late. The appeal was filed on July 26, 1999, with Perillie listed as counsel of record. On September 24, 1999, the appeal was dismissed as untimely, and the decision was mailed to Perillie. More than 5 years later, on January 10, 2005, the respondent has filed an untimely motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(e)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
There is no adequate excuse provided for the extreme lateness of the pending motion. The respondent makes a claim against Wilens and Baker, but as the DHS argues, the Notice of Appeal was filed by current counsel, who was an associate at that law firm.
 
Even were the motions deadline tolled, the motion would be denied. As the DHS argues, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). As for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, Matter of Assaad, supra, and Matter of Lozada, supra, require that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. See Esposito v. INS, 987 F.2d 108, 119 (2d Cir. 1993). Here, there is no evidence that the respondent gave the complained-about counsel the opportunity to respond to the allegations of ineffectiveness. Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.

IN RE: GABRIEL MANUEL CRUZ-SANCHEZ

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL

Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal; termination of proceedings
 
The respondent has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s decision, dated October 21, 2003. We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, with the following comments. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the ¿Iºmmigration ¿Jºudge articulated in his or her decision).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the ineffective assistance of counsel has deprived him of due process. We have reviewed the record and conclude that the respondent has failed to demonstrate that counsel’s performance was inadequate or that it was so inadequate that it prejudiced the outcome of proceedings. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 894-97 (9th Cir. 2003) (ineffective assistance of counsel at a deportation hearing may result in a denial of due process under the Fifth Amendment only when proceeding is so fundamentally unfair that the alien is prevented from reasonably presenting his claim). There is no evidence in the record that respondent’s previous counsel prevented the respondent from having a fair opportunity to present his applications for relief. See e.g. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) citing Ramirez-Durazo v. INS, 794 F.2d 491 (9th Cir. 1986); Lopez v. INS, 775 F.2d 1015 (9th Cir. 1985) (alien must show not merely ineffective assistance of counsel, but assistance which is so ineffective as to have impinged upon the fundamental fairness of the hearing in violation of the fifth amendment due process clause).
 
Accordingly, in view of the above, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANCISCO GARCIA MENDEZ JACQUELINA CLEMENTINA GARCIA JUAREZ
Files: AXX XX8 443 - Los Angeles
AXX XX8 444
April 4, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondents appeal from an Immigration Judge’s December 3, 2003, decision denying their requests for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). With the following comments, we will adopt and affirm the decision and dismiss the appeal. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
The Immigration Judge denied the female respondent’s application for relief based on her finding that the respondent failed to establish that she had been physically present in the United States for a continuous period of not less than 10 years immediately preceding the issuance of her Notice to Appear on June 7, 1999. See sections 240A(b)(1)(A) and (d). In light of the inconsistencies in the record, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent failed to establish, though credible evidence, that she had been physically present in the United states for the requisite statutory period (see I.J. at 4-6). Compare Lopez-Alvarado v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 847 (9th Cir. 2004).
 
The Immigration Judge denied the male respondent’s application for relief based on her finding that the respondent failed to establish that he had been a person of good moral character for a continuous period of not less than 10 years immediately preceding the date of the application. See section 240A(b)(1)(B) of the Act. It is undisputed that the respondent was convicted in 1999 of the offense of solicitation of prostitution in violation of section 647(b) of the California Penal Code. Solicitation of prostitution qualifies as a crime involving moral turpitude under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I). See Matter of Lambert, 11 I&N Dec. 340 (BIA 1965). An alien whose conduct is described in section 212(a)(2)(A) of the Act is statutorily precluded from demonstrating good moral character where the conduct at issue occurred during the relevant good moral character period. See section 101(f)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(f)(3). In this instance, the respondent’s offense occurred during the relevant 10-year period immediately preceding the filing of his application for cancellation of removal. He cannot, therefore, demonstrate good moral character as required by section 240A(b)(1)(B) of the Act.
 
*2 The respondents, who were represented by counsel during the removal hearings, also contest the fairness of the proceedings based on their assertion that counsel was incompetent. Specifically, they assert that competent counsel would have filed a brief in support of their applications for cancellation of removal, and such competent counsel would have also filed timely applications for suspension of deportation on their behalf. We reject their contention. The respondents have failed to show, on appeal, how the preparation of a brief would have altered the Immigration Judge’s decision in this case. Furthermore, the respondents, who were properly placed in removal proceedings, are ineligible for suspension of deportation. See Ramirez-Zavala v. Ashcroft, 336 F.3d 872 (9th Cir. 2003). They have thus failed to show that they were prejudiced by the actions of counsel. See Melkonian v. Ashcroft, 320 F.3d 1061(9th Cir. 2003). They have also failed to comply with the procedural requirements to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Lo v. Ashcroft, 341F.3d 934 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondents are permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS, formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondents fail to so depart, the respondents shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondents fail to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondents shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

REISSUED DECISION
 
*1 We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on December 31, 2002, and denied a motion to reconsider on May 21, 2003. On February 2, 2005, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. Indeed, the Seventh Circuit, in Ahmed v. *1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law (both respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on September 1, 2004, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s August 1, 2003, decision denying the respondents’ motion to reopen and rescind the order of removal entered in absentia on February 19, 2003. The respondents’ current motion to reopen is granted.
 
In our prior decision affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondents’ motion to reopen and rescind the order of removal entered in absentia, we found that the record supported the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondents had failed to establish “exceptional circumstances” sufficient to excuse their absence from immigration court on February 19, 2003. The Board declined to consider the evidence and arguments proffered by the respondents for the first time on appeal. See Matter of Obaigbena, 19 I&N Dec. 533 (BIA 1988). The Board also concluded that the respondents had failed to satisfy their burden in supporting their assertion that they were the victims of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). However, through their current motion, we find that the respondents have successfully complied with Matter of Lozada, supra. Moreover, the respondents have established that the reason for their failure to appear was beyond their control and, therefore, have shown “exceptional circumstances” excusing their failure to appear on February 19, 2003.
 
Accordingly, the motion is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.

2005 WL 1104244 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HELEN OMOLO
File: AXX XX7 510 - New York
March 29, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Justin Conlon, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the board on February 19, 2002, when we denied the respondent’s motion as untimely filed. On February 7, 2005, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Board pursuant to the Stipulation and Order of Settlement and Withdrawal of the United States District Court, District of Connecticut, dated January 10, 2005. The District Court’s Order vacates all prior decisions of both the Board of Immigration Appeals and the Immigration Court in this matter, and remands the matter to the Board for a determination as to whether the respondent has satisfied the requirements of an ineffective assistance of counsel claim pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Furthermore, once the Board has determined that the respondent has satisfied the requirements under Matter of Lozada, the Board will remand to the Immigration Judge for a hearing on the respondent’s applications for relief.
 
We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, supra. Matter of Lozada requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. See also Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 130 (2nd Cir. 2000) (the deadline for motions to reopen deportation proceedings may be equitably tolled to accommodate claims of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
As noted by the respondent, her case has a very complicated procedural history. In her most recent motion, she is alleging that four of her prior attorneys committed ineffective assistance of counsel in representing her. The respondent has set forth in detail all of her separate claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has submitted detailed affidavits outlining the agreements reached with all of her prior attorneys concerning their representation; she has notified all of her prior attorneys (which she could contact) of the ineffective assistance claims that she is raising; and she has filed a complaint with the relevant disciplinary authorities against all of the attorneys that have represented her, who have not been disbarred. The respondent has either complied, or substantially complied, with all of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, for each of the attorneys against whom she alleges ineffective assistance of counsel. The record in the instant case supports the conclusion that based on the totality of circumstances, the respondent has established exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear. Consequently, we find that the respondent has presented valid claims of ineffective assistance of counsel and the proceedings will be reopened and remanded for further proceedings.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.

2005 WL 1104258 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LAURA ELENA ZAMBRANO VALENZUELA ZOILA NALLELI ARANDA-ZAMBRANO YESICA LIZET ARANDA-ZAMBRANO
Files: AXX XX5 356 - Los Angeles
AXX XX5 357
AXX XX5 358
March 28, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Christopher J. Todd, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (all respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on October 6, 2004, when we denied the respondents’ motion to reopen, based upon alleged ineffective assistance of counsel and the respondents’ motion to hold in abeyance our decision on the respondents’ motion to reopen until the respondents were able to comply with the requirements of our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). On October 21, 2004, the respondents filed a timely motion to reconsider, arguing that the Board failed to consider the evidence submitted by the respondents as a supplement to their motion to reopen. The respondents maintain that the Board received the supplement to the pending motion to reopen on September 22, 2004, over 2 weeks prior to the issuance of the Board’s October 6, 2004, decision denying the respondents’ motion to reopen. The respondents’ motion will be granted, the respondents’ removal proceedings will be reopened, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Due to the Board’s failure to consider the respondents’ supplement to their motion to reopen, we will grant the respondents’ motion to reconsider. In the respondents’ supplement to their motion to reopen, the respondents request reopening due to the ineffective assistance of their prior counsel. Specifically, the respondents argue that their prior counsel failed to present evidence regarding the lead respondent’s history of abuse by her husband and the learning disability suffered by the lead respondent’s United States citizen son. The respondents also claim that their prior counsel failed to properly prepare them for their hearing before the Immigration Judge.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, supra. In Matter of Lozada, supra, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. However, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held that an alien need not fulfill all the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel when the record reflects a clear and obvious case of such. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that attorney’s failure to timely file a suspension of deportation application was negligence); see also Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000). In addition to the three procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, it is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003) (stating that a due process violation requires a showing of prejudice, which can be made if counsel’s performance “was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings”).
 
*2 The record reflects that the respondents have fully complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Furthermore, we find that the respondents have established that they suffered prejudice as a result of their counsel’s ineffectiveness, insofar as the failure of the respondents’ counsel to present evidence relevant to the hardship that the lead respondent’s two United States citizens sons may face in Mexico may have potentially affected the outcome of these proceedings. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003) (stating that a due process violation requires a showing of prejudice, which can be made if counsel’s performance “was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings”). Since the respondents have complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, and established prejudice by their prior counsel’s ineffective assistance, we grant the respondents’ motion to reopen and remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings. Moreover, we note that the respondents have presented in the supplement to their motion to reopen new and previously unavailable evidence regarding hardship for cancellation of removal and eligibility for withholding of removal that should be considered by the Immigration Judge on remand. Accordingly, the respondents’ motion to reconsider is granted, the respondents’ removal proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.

FOR THE BOARD

2005 WL 1104165 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ABELARDO GUERECA ENRIQUEZ PETRA ALVARADO ENRIQUEZ
Files: A73 982 758 - San Francisco
A78 372 719
March 22, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Anthony D. Agpaoa, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa M. Koven
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondents’ motion to reopen has been filed out of time. We dismissed the respondents’ appeal on August 9, 2004. The motion to reopen was submitted late on December 21, 2004. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondents argue that the deadline for filing the motion to reopen should be tolled. The Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals has found that the motion to reopen period may be tolled due to “deception, fraud, or error.” Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182, 1185-86 (9th Cir. 2004). The respondents’ affidavit does not establish that their former attorney deceived them about the Board’s dismissal of the appeal, and does not establish when they sought out new counsel. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir.2003) (court found that to the extent that equitable tolling applies, based on actions of prior counsel, limitations period begins when respondent meets with new counsel and learns of fraud).
 
In any event, the respondents fail to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556, 561-62 (BIA 2003). As for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, those cases require that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226-27 (9th Cir. 2002); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525-526 (9th Cir. 2000)(Lozada requirements generally reasonable, but full compliance is not required if the record independently shows that the “ineffective assistance” claim is legitimate and substantial). The respondents here are only “considering” making a complaint against former counsel. The Board in Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 603-605 (BIA 1996), gave several reasons for the “bar complaint” requirement. We noted that the requirement protects against collusion between aliens and counsel in which “ineffective assistance” is used to achieve delay. Id. We found that the bar complaint, like the other filings required by Matter of Lozada, supra, greatly aids us in adjudicating the claim. Id. We also stated that the filing of such a complaint is a “relatively small inconvenience” for an alien seeking a new hearing after proceedings have been concluded at great government expense. Id. The disciplinary process could consider, and evaluate, the actions taken by former counsel. Matter of Rivera-Claros, supra; see also Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 596 (9th Cir. 2004) (“¿rºequiring sworn affidavits and formal grievance letters reduces petitioners’ ability to slip meritless claims past the Board”); compare Castillo-Perez v. INS, supra, at fn. 11 (alien not required to submit a bar complaint in case where attorney had been subject to numerous counts of violations of professional ethics and had been punished by the Supreme Court of California).
 
*2 The pending motion is, therefore, denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RICARDO MARTINEZ-DE LOS SANTOS
File: A79 370 213 - San Francisco
March 21, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frank Kim, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Harold K. Pickering
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; adjustment of status
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board of Immigration Appeals on March 31, 2004, when the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s applications for relief from removal was affirmed without opinion. The respondent filed a motion to reopen and stay of removal on December 14, 2004.1 The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) filed a response in opposition to the respondent’s motions. The motion to reopen will be denied.
 
Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with exceptions not pertinent here), a motion to reopen in a case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed within 90 days of the administratively final order. As noted above, the Board issued a final decision on March 31, 2004. The respondent’s motion was therefore due on or before June 29, 2004; however, the respondent submitted the current motion to reopen, through counsel, on December 14, 2004. The motion is therefore untimely.
 
However, the respondent, through counsel, asserts that the current motion to reopen is subject to equitable tolling because he was unaware of his eligibility for relief despite reasonable diligence. Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001). In this regard, the respondent claims that he was unaware of the existence of a visa petition filed on behalf the respondent’s father in February 2001 which would enable him to pursue adjustment of status pursuant to section 245(i)’s “grandfathering” provisions, see section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i), until sometime in July 2004. The respondent in his declaration states that it was at a family dinner in June or early July 2003, for the first time, he mentioned to his father the status of his case. This statement is hard to accept since the evidence submitted in support of his motion reveals that the respondent has been living with his father since December 2000. Specifically, the Form I-130 filed on behalf of the respondent’s father in February 2001 reflects that the respondent’s father intended to live at “1675 Shoreview Avenue, San Mateo, CA,” and the Form G-325 submitted by the respondent dated December 13, 2004, reflects that he has been living at “1675 Shoreview Avenue, San Mateo, CA” since December 2000. Further, the Immigration Judge’s decision reflects that the respondent testified that he and his wife live with his father (I.J. at 3). In any event, even accepting the respondent’s statement as true, since the respondent has been living with his father during the period of time he claims that he did not learn and could not have learned of the existence of the visa petition filed on his father’s behalf, we cannot find that he exercised reasonable diligence or was prevented by circumstances beyond his control from discovering the existence of the filing of the visa petition. Moreover, more fundamentally, we do not find that equitable tolling of the regulatory deadlines is applicable under the circumstances presented here.
 
*2 Additionally, it is noted that the respondent has not raised any arguments of ineffective assistance of counsel. Moreover, even accepting the arguments that the “grandfathering” provisions apply to the respondent, we decline to exercise our discretionary authority to sua sponte reopen proceedings.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JESUS GARCIA-RODRIGUEZ SANDRA GARCIA
Files: AXX XX7 456 - San Francisco
AXX XX7 457
March 17, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Rosario Maria Hernandez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kenneth S. Sogabe
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (both respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
 
The respondents, natives and citizens of Mexico, have appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision finding they had failed to demonstrate the requisite 10 years of continuous physical presence and, therefore, denying their application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondents failed to provide clear and convincing evidence of the requisite 10 years of continuous physical presence prior to the issuance of the Notices to Appear, in his case on August 9, 2001 (Exhs. 1A and 1B). See section 240A(b) and (d) of the Act. Despite the contentions to the contrary on appeal, the testimony and evidence introduced by the respondents do not demonstrate 10 years of continuous presence. The only documentation they have provided to support their claimed presence in the United States before 1993 are two affidavits. One of the affidavits simply states the affiant has known the female respondent since 1991, and a second, for the male respondent which, as noted by the Immigration Judge, provides an address for the respondent during the period in question which does not correspond to the addresses given by the male respondent (Exhs. 5 and 17). We also note that neither respondent was able to provide cogent information about where they lived in the United States between 1991 and 1993, with the male respondent providing varying addresses, and the female respondent simply not remembering where they purportedly lived (Tr. at 33-38, 41-43, 51-52). While the respondents may have been somewhat itinerant during this period, we agree with the Immigration Judge that this vague testimony and almost compete lack of documentary support fails to meet their burden of proof.
 
Further the record simply does not support the respondents argument that their failure to meet their burden of proof was the fault of ineffective assistance of counsel. In this regard we note that their former counsel, who they partly blame for their failure and who has been barred from practice, last represented them at a hearing on August 9, 2001, and their merits hearing took place over 2 years later on November 5, 2003. Further, their current attorney has not presented any additional evidence of the respondents presence in the United States or any indication of how past ineffective counsel prejudiced them or would alter the outcome of this case.
 
*2 Based on the foregoing, we will dismiss the appeal and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny relief in the form of cancellation of removal.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed and the decision of the Immigration Judge affirmed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondents are permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 240.26(c), (f). In the event a respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If a respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2005 WL 1104212 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AUGUSTIN RENDON HERNANDEZ
File: A95 291 798 - Los Angeles
March 17, 2005
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Toni A. Lawson, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
 
The respondent, a male native and citizen of Mexico, appealed from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated November 4, 2003. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged, and denied his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. The appeal will be dismissed.1
 
We adopt and affirm the November 4, 2003, decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). During the hearing on November 7, 2002, the Immigration Judge advised the respondent and his attorney of the importance to appear prepared and ready to proceed with the application for cancellation of removal during the merits hearing scheduled for November 4, 2003 (Tr. at 8-9). The Immigration Judge explained to the respondent and to his attorney how the respondent was required to proceed to prepare for the merits hearing (which included obtaining a current fingerprint check), and advised them of the consequences of the respondent’s failure to appear for the hearing prepared (Tr. at 9). On the scheduled hearing date of November 4, 2003, the respondent was unprepared to proceed with his cancellation of removal application, and he did not have the results of a fingerprint cheek (Tr. at 13-17). Consequently, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal (I.J. at 3, 5-7). As the record fully supports the result of the Immigration Judge’s decision, we affirm the conclusion that the respondents failed to demonstrate eligibility for cancellation of removal.
 
The respondent contends that he did not have the results of his fingerprint check in time for the hearing on November 4, 2003, because someone who recently began assisting him in preparing his cancellation of removal application advised him that the fingerprint check did not have to be completed until “later” (Tr. at 12-13). Overall, the evidence reflects that the respondent chose to disregard the explicit directions and instructions communicated to him and to his attorney by the Immigration Judge, and that he followed the advise of a non-attorney third person who never was his representative of record during these proceedings, and who never appeared in court on his behalf. Consequently, the respondent’s failure to prepare for the November 4, 2003, hearing was the result of his own action or inaction rather than of ineffective assistance of counsel. In view of the foregoing, the following orders shall be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shah be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
